Preface
This thesis has been prepared over a period of six months, from January 2023 until June 2023, at
both the Arctic Department of the Technical University of Denmark (DTU) and at the Department
of Structural Engineering at the Norwegian University of Science and Technology (NTNU), in partial
fulfilment for the degree Master of Science in Civil Engineering for DTU and the degree Master of
Science in Cold Climate Engineering for NTNU.
The fieldwork and soil sample collection took place in July 2022 at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut,
West-Greenland, in cooperation with Nukissiorfiit – the Greenland Public Utilities Company – who
initiated the project in May 2022. Some initial data processing was performed during the specialisation
project TKT 4550 at NTNU in the first semester of the academic year 2022-2023.
The project constitutes 30 ECTS and is completed with Prof. dr. Thomas Ingeman-Nielsen (DTU)
and Prof. dr. Arne Aalberg (NTNU) as supervisors.
It is assumed that the reader has a basic knowledge in the areas of geotechnics and geophysics, and
understands the basic principles of thermodynamics.
Tom De Ville
Trondheim, the 11th of June, 2023
I
==PAGE:6==
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
The electricity of Greenland’s second largest town, Sisimiut (West -Greenland), is supplied by a
hydropower plant, located some 20 km north from the town. In late 2021, Nukissiorfiit – the Greenland
public utilities company – observed severe settlements of some power line masts, constructed on
fine-grained ice rich permafrost. These settlements threaten the integrity of the power lines and may
potentially result in a disruption of power supply to the town. This project investigates the cause of
these settlements with an aim to suggest appropriate adaptation measures.
Frozen soil samples were retrieved from around one of the masts at the south shore of the Second
Fjord in Sisimiut, and soil temperatures are measured at nine locations. At each location, the soil
temperature is measured at depths of approximately 20 cm and 1 m below surface. Furthermore,
the air temperature and the temperature of the metal mast foundation is monitored. Finally, four
Electric Resistivity Tomography (ERT) profiles were collected and the whole site was surveyed by
a drone that provided detailed Digital Elevation Models (DEM) and orthophotos of the area. The
collected frozen soil samples were further processed in the laboratories of the Technical University
of Denmark in Kgs. Lyngby, and they show a stratigraphy consisting of an organic peat top layer,
followed by ice rich silt and clay layers at depth.
The soil sample information and temperature data is used to set up and calibrate a thermal model
using the software GeoStudio TEMP/W. This model is used to investigate several possible solutions
to stabilise the masts under current and future climate conditions. The solutions studied comprise
the construction of an elevated gravel pad around the foundation of the masts, or alternatively the
use of thermosyphons to stabilize the soil thermal regime. The effect of constructing the gravel
embankment with and without EPS insulation in the pad is considered as well. Lastly, the eventual
influence of the considered adaptation methods on the future thermal regime is compared with the
model output where no measures are taken at all.
II
==PAGE:8==
==PAGE:9==
Abstract
Sisimiut, de op één na grootste stad van Groenland, wordt voorzien van elektriciteit door een
waterkrachtcentrale die zich ongeveer 20 km ten noorden van de stad bevindt. Nukissiorfiit –
het bedrijf dat instaat voor de nutsvoorzieningen in Groenland – constateerde eind 2021 ernstige
verzakkingen van de fundering van enkele masten langs de hoogspanningslijn die zich bevinden
op sedimentaire permafrost met een significante fractie ijs. Deze verzakkingen vormen een reële
bedreiging voor de integriteit van de volledige hoogspanningslijn en kunnen mogelijk leiden tot
een verstoring van de stroomvoorziening van de stad. Dit project onderzoekt de oorzaak van deze
verzakkingen met als uiteindelijk doel om passende maatregelen voor te stellen.
In begin juli 2022, werd er in het kader van dit project gedurende drie dagen veldwerk verricht rond
een van de masten aan de zuidelijke kust van de ‘Tweede Fjord’ te Sisimiut, en werden er een tiental
bevroren grondmonsters verworven. Tijdens het veldwerk, zijn er ook sensors geïnstalleerd om de
grondtemperaturen op negen locaties continu te monitoren, op een diepte onder het maaiveld van
ongeveer 20 cm en 1 m per locatie. Bovendien wordt de luchttemperatuur en de temperatuur van
de metalen mastfundering ook continu gemonitord. Ten slotte werden er vier Electric Resistivity
Tomography (ERT) metingen uitgevoerd die de elektrische weerstand van de ondergrond weergeven,
en werd het hele gebied rond de mast in kaart gebracht met behulp van een drone met gedetailleerde
digitale hoogtemodellen (DEM) en luchtfoto’s als resultaat. De bevroren grondmonsters werden
verder verwerkt in de laboratoria van de Technische Universiteit van Denemarken (DTU) in Kongens
Lyngby. In het algemeen kan er gesteld worden dat de stratigrafie dicht bij het oppervlak uit een
organische laag bestaat en dat diepere grondlagen uit ijsrijke leem- en kleilagen bestaan.
De verzamelde gegevens en informatie over de site en de ondergrond worden vervolgens gebruikt
om een thermisch model op te stellen, en te kalibreren, met behulp van de GeoStudio TEMP/W
software. Het hoofdzakelijke doel van dit model is om de effectiviteit van verschillende mogelijke
aanpassingen aan het ontwerp te onderzoeken om de masten te stabiliseren onder de huidige en
toekomstige klimatologische omstandigheden. De beschouwde scenario’s zijn de constructie van een
talud uit lokaal gemijnde grind rond de fundering van de masten en het gebruik van thermosifons die
het thermische regime van de bodem dienen te stabiliseren om verdere verzakkingen te voorkomen.
Er wordt ook gekeken naar het effect van het gebruik van horizontale isolatie. Ten slotte worden
de op toekomstige klimaat modellen gebaseerde resultaten met de implementatie van de besproken
aanpassingen vergeleken met het geval er geen maatregelen getroffen worden om zo hun uiteindelijke
effect te begroten.

Acknowledgements
I would like to thank the following persons for helping me in achieving the results I am presenting
you in this Master’s Thesis:
First of all, I am very grateful to Thomas Ingeman-Nielsen, Associate Professor at DTU, for
giving me the opportunity to work on this utterly interesting topic and to introduce me to field work
in the Arctic. Your expertise and support were crucial during the process of this Thesis. Thank
you very much to allow me to use DTU equipment for the fieldwork and to give me access to DTU
laboratories.
Arne Aalberg, Associate Professor at NTNU, for supporting me and sharing his great knowledge
on the topic. Thank you for putting me into contact with technical personnel here at NTNU as well.
Martin Nauta, Project Leader at Nukissiorfiit and responsible for the Sisimiut powerline. Thank
you for your support and for making this Thesis project possible. I am also very grateful for your
help in organising the field work to the mast and for sharing your expertise on the matter. Many
thanks to John Thomson as well, for helping us in the logistics for the fieldwork.
Gustav Grimstad, Associate Professor at NTNU, many thanks for your valuable and expert help,
and for providing me with the necessary equipment to conduct conductivity tests.
Danielle Bridle and Anton Berggreen Abrahamsen, two co-students and very good friends.
Without you two I can undoubtedly say I would not be presenting what I am presenting today. Thank
you so much for supporting me in so many ways, and especially during the field and lab work.
I would also like to extend my gratitude to Marco Marcer, postdoc at Arctic DTU in Sisimiut, for
helping me with so many things and especially for setting up the first meetings, organising the field
work and collecting the temperature data. Tina Chapman, thank you so much for helping me
administratively and for putting me into contact with the right persons in Sisimiut. It has been an
absolute pleasure to work with the whole Arctic DTU crew.
Sigrid Glargaard, my fellow student and close friend, thank you for supporting me, offering your
room and for your flexibility and patience during my stay in Lyngby regarding the lab work. The lab
work has been only be possible thanks to you.
Ditte Valentin, Laboratory Assistants at DTU, and Ebba Schnell, Laboratory Coordinator at
DTU, thank you very much for your assistance and guidance concerning my laboratory work and for
your flexibility during my short stay in Lyngby.
Thomas Lorentzen, PhD student at DTU, thank you for your crucial help during the processing of
the ERT data.
Johanna Scheer, PhD student at DTU, thank you for your valuable assistance regarding the
observations in change of vegetation around the masts.
Lee Thomas Champe and Espen Andersen Torsæter, thank you very much for your help in
the geotechnical labs.
Katarina Kolding Skaarup, fellow student and close friend, and Jeff Larsen, PhD student at
DTU and good friend, thank you very much for your help and support during my time in Lyngby for
the lab work.
Aimilia Diamanti, fellow student and good friend, thank you for helping me out with the lab work
at DTU.
And lastly, but definitely not least, I would like to express my eternal gratitude to my parents, for
giving me this unique opportunity to go abroad and study for a second Master’s degree, providing me
with so many extraordinary possibilities. Their and my brother’s, Bence, support has been essential
during these two years. This Thesis is certainly also their merit.

XVII
==PAGE:26==
Chapter 1
Introduction
In May of 2022, Nukissiorfiit – the Greenland Public Utilities Company – reached out to Thomas
Ingeman-Nielsen regarding a geotechnical problem they are encountering with the power line from
the hydropower plant towards the city of Sisimiut, West-Greenland. After inspection, it appeared
that many masts along the southern side of the Second Fjord, that are constructed on sedimentary
and ice-rich permafrost deposits, show significant settlements and tilting of the foundation column
compared to the previous inspection which was performed three years earlier. The location of the
power line is shown in Figure 1.1.
Figure 1.1: Map of the location of Mast 9 along the power line in Sisimiut, West-Greenland.
At this moment, Nukissiorfiit does not exactly know why the settlements suddenly occurred and they
tried to repair already three masts. Two masts have been repaired by replacing the fill around the
foundation and another mast has been repaired by adding additional soil onto the surface around the
mast in order to pull the frost level up. At this moment, it is still too soon to conclude what the
eventual final result is of these adaptations.
1
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 1. Introduction
1.1
Objective
The main goal of this project is to determine what the cause is of the sudden settlements and what
the best adaptation method is to ensure that the power line can remain operational in the future. In
order to reach this objective, the project is made more concrete and it is opted to focus on Mast 9
of the power line, which is also indicated on the map in Figure 1.1.
A three day field trip was organised in cooperation with Nukissiorfiit to the mast in question in July
2022. During this field trip, the goal was to collect as much as possible valuable data of the site and
subsurface such that a reliable and representative thermal model can be composed in a later stage of
the project. A lot of effort was put into the storage of the collected permafrost samples in a freezer,
to preserve the ice structures in the samples to be able to perform thorough investigations in the
laboratories later on. The collection of temperature data of the subsurface, mast foundation and air
was prioritised as well, for mainly calibration purposes of the model.
During the laboratory investigations of the samples at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU)
in January 2023, the main goal is to classify the soil samples to obtain the stratigraphy, and to
determine some general soil characteristics such as the grain density, water content and Atterberg
limits. As the thermal properties are required for the model, the unfrozen water content, the thermal
conductivity, and the heat capacity needs to be measured as well. It is also intended with the soil
sample investigations of the two boreholes, to identify possible deviations in the soil structure in
function of the distance to the mast.
Lastly, the thermal model is set-up in such way that it is able to reproduce the thermal regime of
the site as accurately as possible, based on the collected data. The effectiveness of two adaptation
methods is investigated as well, by using climate scenarios to predict the future evolution of the
thermal regime.
1.2
Structure of the report
In order to reach the final goal of this project, the following topics are discussed in this Master’s
Thesis:
Chapter 1 introduces the reader to the problem that is considered in this project, together with a
description of the site, the mast itself and the climate at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut.
Chapter 2 discusses some relevant aspects for the project in a detailed Literature Study. The reader
is introduced to permafrost and to the physical processes encountered in areas with permafrost
present in the subsurface. Furthermore, a discussion is held on thermosyphons, which is one of the
considered adaptation methods in this project. Lastly, there is theoretical background provided to
the reader of some of the used methods in the field and in the laboratories.
Chapter 3 gives information about the activities that were successfully completed during the field
work, and discusses the obtained results that are not related to eventual laboratory work.
Chapter 4 discusses the activities performed in the laboratories of DTU in Kgs. Lyngby on the frozen
permafrost samples, together with the eventually obtained results. A thorough critial discussion is
held on some of the used methods as well.
Chapter 5 deals with all the relevant aspects regarding the thermal modelling in GeoStudio TEMP/W.
Both the methodology and the results are discussed, including future projections of the situation
with and without adaptation methods included.
Chapter 6 concludes this thesis with a concise summary, a general conclusion, and a critical review
of the presented results and used methods. There are also suggestions given for further research and
future related projects.
2
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 1. Introduction
It is important to note that some parts of the report of the Specialisation Course TKT 4550 have
been adapted and added to this Thesis, as they are relevant and indispensable for this Thesis [28].
The parts in question are: (i) the Site Description; (ii) the description of the climate in Sisimiut
based on DMI data; (iii) the sections regarding Solifluction and Soil Frost Action in the Literature
Study; (iv) parts of the discussion of the Fieldwork; and (v) the processing of the following data sets:
the drone footage, the active layer probing data, the grounding resistance of the ERT profiles, the
temperature data of only the last two days of the field work, and the thawed soil samples.
1.3
Site description
Mast 9 is located close to the end of the Second Fjord as shown on the map in Figure 1.1 and on
the drone overview in Figure 1.2. Mast 9 was built on the south shore of the Fjord and based on
the information provided by Karsten Pedersen in 2006 by mail, the soil there consists of a terrace of
melt water deposits which are older than the quaternary marine deposits that are found at higher
elevations. This is due to the fact that Greenland is rising up due to the melting of the ice cap which
is ongoing since the last Ice Age.
Regarding the rocks found in the surroundings, it can be said that in general gneiss can be
encountered with an age between 1,79 to 1,65 billion years old. The Second Fjord is indeed located
in the Nagssugtoqidian fold belt which stretched over a vast area from East to West Greenland. In
this folding belt metamorphised granites and gneisses are encountered, which are transformed from
sediments and volcanic deposits. Note that the source is added in Appendix A.7.
Mast 9
Camp
Power Plant
POWER LINE
Figure 1.2: Overview of the end of the Second Fjord with the location of the camp site, Mast 9, the
power plant and power line indicated.
The power line has been built in 2008 by the Icelandic company Istak and the information summarised
below regarding the mast is based on the technical drawings and reports from Istak that were made
available for this project. The type of foundation of the masts in sedimentary soil consists of a
column fixed onto a wooden base which is located approximately 2 m below surface. This wooden
base is rectangular and has an area of approximately 12 x 12 m2. The foundation column where the
mast is fixed onto has a hollow square cross section with outer dimensions of 300 x 300 mm2 and a
thickness equal to 8 mm.
3
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Part a of the foundation is a reinforced HE300A profile. The reinforcement is performed by using
four plates with a thickness of 10 mm and four plates with a thickness of 8 mm as shown in Figure
1.4 (a). Indeed, under the column the thickness of the plates that are used for the reinforcement is
larger. This part is attached to the column with bolts and welding and to the HE140A profiles by
using bolts. The reinforcing plates are also attached to the HE300A profile with welding as shown in
Figure 1.4 (b).
Due to the presence of permafrost in the sub surface where Mast 9 is located, there is also an
Expanded PolyStyrene (EPS) insulation layer with a thickness of 50 mm placed, horizontally at a
depth of 0.8 m below surface. Around the foundation column of Mast 9 there was also an insulation
layer glued with a thickness of 20 mm, starting from the surface downwards until the wooden foot is
reached. The latter insulation is meant to decrease heat transport through the steel mast foundation
into the surrounding permafrost. The horizontal insulation layer on the other hand, is designed to
protect the underlying permafrost of the ambient conditions, to favour the long term functionality of
the mast. The anchors are fixed with a steel profile approximately 2 m below the surface. Again,
an insulation layer is placed horizontally 0.8 m below surface. Note that both the anchor and the
wooden base are placed under the frost level, i.e. in the permafrost. The structural elements were
placed at depth by excavating a pit and filling it back afterwards by using the excavated soil.
Mast 9, as encountered during the fieldwork, is shown in Figure 1.6 and has according to the technical
drawings a height of 19.8 m relative to the surface level.

We arrived on site approximately two weeks after the last snow melted. This meant that the active
layer thickness at many places was quite thin. A first impression was that the soil and mosses
were quite wet around the anchors and the mast foundation. Furthermore, we found pieces of the
insulation sticking up and there was even a piece of insulation found lying next to the foundation
column. Figure A.1 in the Appendix shows how the insulation was encountered during the field trip.
5
==PAGE:31==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Figure 1.6: First view on Mast 9 during the field trip.
1.4
Climate
The climate in Sisimiut is characterised as an Arctic tundra climate, which means that the winters are
long, dark and cold and that the amount of precipitation is relatively low [29]. For Arctic tundras it is
not uncommon that the precipitation is quite limited with a yearly precipitation of approximately 250
mm. Knowing that the yearly precipitation in a desert is in general equal to 250 mm [30], it is indeed
true that the yearly precipitation in a tundra is considered low. The reason that these regions are still
relatively wet, is due to the lower temperatures such that evaporation is limited as well [29]. These
low temperatures are clearly seen in Figure 1.7 (a), where the Mean Annual Air Temperature (MAAT)
is depicted from 1961 until 2022 based on data from The Danish Meteorological Institute (DMI)
[1]. Another important characteristic of a tundra climate, is that permafrost is often encountered
which is also confirmed by our findings during the drilling around Mast 9 and by Figure 2.1 in the
Literature Study.
The presented graphs in Figure 1.7 , Figure 1.8 and Figure 1.9 have been generated with Python
code based on the tutorial of Thomas Ingeman-Nielsen for the processing of DMI climate data.
6
==PAGE:32==
Chapter 1. Introduction
(a) MAAT (1961-2022)
(b) Standard year for the air temperature compared
with 2014 air temperature data
(c) Fitting of the standard year with a sinusoidal
function.
Figure 1.7: Processed temperature data from the DMI report [1].
Regarding the temperature variation depicted in the graph of the standard year in Figure 1.7 (b)
and (c), it is clear that indeed only the period from roughly June to September has a positive mean
air temperature. Other months show subzero temperatures and consequently in these months the
precipitation is in the form of snow.
7
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 1. Introduction
(a) Mean Annual Wind Speed (1961-2022)
(b) Standard year for wind speeds
(c) Wind rose
Figure 1.8: Processed wind speed data from the DMI report [1].
Figure 1.7 (b) and (c), Figure 1.8 (b) and Figure 1.9 (b) show a standard year for respectively the air
temperature, wind velocity and the relative humidity for Sisimiut by processing the data from 1961
until 2022, that was retrieved from the earlier mentioned DMI report [1], as follows. For every day of
a year of 365 days, the mean value of the considered variable is used for that specific day of every
year in the period of 1961 to 2022. This ensures that extremes are omitted and also ensures that a
complete data set can be used in further calculations. A standard year also shows how a "normal"
year would look like, with no extremes, based on the used historical data. In the case there is no
measurement for a certain day and ’Not a Number’ is encountered in the data set, that certain day
of the considered year is not taken into account for the calculation of the mean value in the standard
year.
Regarding the relative humidity, one can see that the shown values in the standard year are to a
certain extent low, which is also in line with the low amount of precipitation and evaporation. Note
that the wind speeds seem to be relatively low as well for a coastal area.
8
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 1. Introduction
As shown in Figure 1.7 (c), it is also possible to fit the standard year with a sinusoidal curve. This is
performed in this case by using a curve optimising algorithm and the result for the expression of the
air temperature in a standard year based on data from 1961 to 2021 is as follows:
T(d) = 10.3611 ⋅sin(0.0176435d −2.19074) −2.94972
(1.1)
with T(d) being the air temperature on day d with d = 1 being the first of January of the standard
year.
With air temperature data from 1961 to 2022, the fitted curve for the standard year changes as
follows:
T(d) = 10.3551 ⋅sin(0.017656d −2.19547) −2.9296
(1.2)
This shifted curve, with the 2022 data included, implies a slight upward shift of the standard year
curve in the temperature - time domain, which means that the winter and summer months got
generally warmer, compared to the standard year exclusively based on data from 1961 up to and
including 2021. This observation is in line with the climate change and the Arctic Amplification
phenomenon [31]. The term Arctic Amplification refers to the observation that generally speaking
the Arctic is warming twice to even three times as fast compared to the rest of the planet. This is
probably due to sea ice loss and shorter winters, such that the reflection of incoming solar radiation
decreases as non-snowy and -icy surfaces have a lower albedo index.
The wind rose in Figure 1.8 (c) shows that the dominant wind direction is from the South and the
East with a maximal wind speed of approximately 10 m/s. Note that the shown wind speeds are not
wind gusts. Wind directions and wind speeds are useful when assessing for instance snowdrift.
Figure 1.9 shows lastly the processed data from the DMI report regarding the relative humidity in
Sisimiut. In the standard year it is indeed clearly visible, that the relative humidity is relatively low
for the majority of the year, and that during the summer months the relative humidity increases due
to the increasing evaporation.
Initially, it was planned to use the standard years as described above in the thermal modelling that
is discussed later in Chapter 5. However, as the scaled ERA5 data proved to be a more realistic
input data set for the thermal model, as extreme weather events are cancelled in a standard year, the
discussion of the standard years is merely kept in this Thesis to describe and illustrate the governing
climate for Sisimiut.
(a) Mean Annual Relative Humidity (1961-2022)
(b) Standard year for the Relative Humidity
Figure 1.9: Processed Relative Humidity data based on the DMI report [1].
9
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Another important climatological parameter to consider in Arctic areas are the thawing and freezing
indices. When using for instance the modified Berggren equation to estimate the thaw depth in a
certain area under certain climatological conditions, the thawing index is clearly the most decisive
parameter which links the calculated thaw depth with the actual climatic conditions [4]. Thawing
and freezing indices also indicate the extent of thawing and freezing, i.e. the larger the absolute value
of the index, the larger the thawing or freezing period in the considered area. The two indices are
determined graphically as shown in Figure 1.10 where the cumulative degree-days in function of time
for the historical data of 2010 in Sisimiut are shown. Cumulative degree days are a very practical
tool to objectively quantify the variability of air temperatures during a certain period [4]. Cumulative
degree-days are computed by adding the mean temperature, with the sign taken into account, of the
considered day to the sum of mean temperatures of all previous days in the considered period. Note
that the cumulative degree-day value of the first day in the considered period is equal to the mean
temperature of that day.
Once the curve as shown in Figure 1.10 is known, it is possible to compute the thawing and freezing
index as follows. The thawing index is defined as the difference in cumulative degree-days between
the day with the lowest cumulative degree-day and the day with the maximal cumulative day after
a period of thawing, where the slope of the curve becomes negative (again). Note that this point
is around 450 days in Figure 1.10 and not around 50 days. The first maximum in the curve is not
usable for the determination of the thawing index, as not the complete thawing period is comprised
in the considered period.
However, this first maximum can be used for the determination of the freezing index, as this index is
defined as the difference in cumulative degree-days between the day where the thawing ends and the
day when the freezing ends. These day are determined as the maximum value and minimal value
respectively.
Figure 1.10: Illustration of a typical cumulative degree-days curve for almost two years for Sisimiut.
The graphical representation of the freezing and thawing index is also given.
Figure 1.11 depicted the cumulative degree days curves for all years in 1962 until 2022. Note that
for the sake of visualising the thawing and freezing index clearly in the plot, there are almost two
years of data needed per considered year for which the indices need to be determined. In general,
these curves show a warming trend over the considered period as the last years are ending in general
at a higher cumulative degree-day value. However, this does not imply a significantly higher thawing
index (It) as the value of the freezing index (If) is generally decreasing in function of time and the
thawing index remains approximately constant.
10
==PAGE:36==
Chapter 1. Introduction
Figure 1.11: Cumulative degree days for Sisimiut from 1962 until 2022 based on DMI data.
The general trend is perhaps even more visible in Figure 1.12, where the cumulative degree days
curve for 1961, 2021, and 2022 are depicted. Note that in this case the three extremes are not
explicitly present in the graphs as only one year of data is used. This graph also clearly shows that
the thawing index is not affected in the same way by climate change, compared to the freezing index.
Lastly, Figure 1.13 shows the evolution of the thawing and freezing indices as a function of time. As
already observed in the previously discussed graphs, the thawing index is more or less constant, while
the general trend of a decreasing absolute value of the freezing index is observed as well.

data.

Chapter 2
Literature Study
During the fieldwork, it was observed by both probing and drilling that the soil relatively close to the
surface was frozen and contained a significant amount of ice. Combined with the information from
Nukissiorfiit, it is certain that the mast has been built onto permafrost which is a key element in
the cryosphere, i.e. the frozen water part of the Earth [3]. According to the book Frozen Ground
Engineering by Andersland and Ladanyi (2004) [4], permafrost, or alternatively perennial frozen
ground, is defined as soil which remains at a temperature below 0 ○C continuously from year to year
and this for a minimum of two years [32]. Indeed, the definition does not require that all the possible
moisture has to be ice or frozen, as it is known that in permafrost one can encounter an unfrozen
water phase as well. Consequently, permafrost is a four-phase medium: it consists of soil particles,
unfrozen water, ice and air [4]. Note that the (water) vapour phase is neglected.
Figure 2.1: Map of the northern hemisphere with the different types of permafrost indicated [2].
Generally speaking, climate is the most influencing factor regarding the continental distribution of
13
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
permafrost, which implies an increased occurrence of permafrost with an increasing latitude [32].
Indeed, permafrost may be encountered in areas with a Mean Annual Air Temperature (MAAT)
of 0 ○C or below [32]. The mostly used categorization of permafrost is by considering four types:
continuous-, discontinuous-, sporadic permafrost and isolated patches [3]. A certain area is said to
contain continuous permafrost when this permafrost is practically omnipresent in the subsurface. A
rule of thumb is that continuous permafrost is encountered in areas where the MAAT is below -7○C
[32]. Discontinuous permafrost on the other hand, implies that only a certain part of the soil in the
considered area has been frozen for at least two years. Based on the definition applied by Obu et al.
[3], areas contain discontinuous permafrost when 50 - 90 % of the considered unit area is covered
with permafrost. Sporadic and Isolated permafrost are extreme cases, with only a limited amount of
the soil in that area being frozen continuously for at least two years.
Figure 2.1 and 2.2 are based on the CryoGrid model of the Mean Annual Ground Temperature
that has been calibrated and validated by using mainly remotely sensed land-surface temperatures,
downscaled ERA5 climate data and soil temperature measurements from several boreholes [2, 3].
Based on the model output, the classification of the permafrost has been carried out by considering
the probability of the occurrence of permafrost at an area of 1 km2.
Figure 2.1 shows the permafrost coverage of the Northern Hemisphere. The best estimate of the
model regarding the fraction of the Northern Hemisphere that is covered by permafrost is 14.6 %.
Continuous permafrost is encountered in the Northern part of Canada and Russia, Greenland and the
Himalaya. There is also continuous permafrost in Svalbard, Franz Josef Land, Jan Mayen, Novaya
Zemlya and the Wrangel Island. According to the model, there is in Iceland, Norway, Sweden and
Finland only continuous permafrost encountered in the high mountainous areas. The majority of the
areas with permafrost are there discontinuous and sporadic. The model also shows permafrost in
some more southern mountain ranges such as the Alps, the Pyrenees, the Sierra Nevada (USA) and
the Caucasus.
The permafrost situation according to the model in the larger Sisimiut area is shown in Figure 2.2.
Mast 9 is indicated with the red dot and the map shows that the end of the Second Fjord is situated
at a zone which transfers from continuous to discontinuous permafrost.
The thickness of permafrost varies generally between a couple centimetres to several hundred meters
[32]. Due to the seasonal variations, there will be a seasonally frozen part of the soil column as well.
Indeed, the active layer is the part of the permafrost which seasonally thaws and freezes and has a
thickness which varies between 15 cm in areas with continuous permafrost and 3 m in mountainous
regions with discontinuous permafrost [32].
The thermal regime of permafrost is an important aspect to consider and monitor, and is therefore
also the property which is often modelled. The thermal regime comprises the temperature of the
permafrost and how this varies in time. Hence, the thermal regime is a direct measure of the ice
content, the strength and the general state of the permafrost including the extend of the active layer
[32]. It is known that the thermal regime and the permafrost existence is determined essentially by
the next factors. First, several climatic factors determine if permafrost can exist in the first place on
a certain location. Indeed, cold climate and sub-zero degrees for a sufficiently long period have to
be occurring. However, these factors are certainly not sufficient to create beneficial conditions for
permafrost existence. Other factors such as the local geology (characteristics of the soil and rocks),
hydrology (amount of ice and water content) and topography (altitude and slope) can create such
conditions that no permafrost is encountered in the subsurface. Biological factors, such as vegetation
and peat layers influence the interaction between the atmosphere and the soil, and its disturbance or
destruction might cause eventually the thaw of the permafrost. Indeed, a last important factor is the
human factor, which can easily destruct the conditions needed for permafrost existence on so many
levels, with construction and pollution of ground water being the main human causes [32].
14
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
Figure 2.2: Types of permafrost at in a part of West-Greenland, covering the broad area around
Sisimiut, Kangerlussuaq and Ilulissat [3]. Note that the same legend is applicable as for Figure 2.1.
A unique feature of especially fine-grained permafrost, is as stated by Andersland and Ladanyi, that
there is still an unfrozen water film on the soil particles in some cases even up to temperatures as
low as -70 ○C. This is due to the fact that the formation of ice releases latent heat due to the phase
change, which facilitates the existence of water at subzero temperatures [4].
As permafrost consists of four instead of three phases, and that the thaw-freezing cycles, and
other natural or human effects, are expected to alter the state of the soil, there are several failure
mechanisms specific for subsurfaces containing permafrost. Andersland and Ladanyi categorise soil
mass movements in permafrost based on the field investigations of McRoberts and Morgenstern
(1973) along the Mackenzie River Valley in Canada [4]. The classification scheme is shown in Figure
2.3 and indicates that there are three main categories when soil mass movement is considered. Firstly,
there are soil mass movements which are dominated by flow movements. Flow implies that the
movement of the mass has similar characteristics than viscous fluid flows with a uniform motion
distribution all through the flowing material.
Slide mass flows on the other hand differ from flow mass flows in the sense that with slides the
movement consists of shear strain and displacement along one or several surfaces and strongly
resembles a rigid body motion. Lastly, fall mass movements indicate a movement of a detached mass
from a steep slope or cliff on which little or no shear displacement takes place. The mass will travel
through the air by free fall, bounding or rolling.
15
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
Figure 2.3: Suggested classification for soil mass movements in permafrost by McRoberts (1978) [4].
As is discussed in Section 3.7.2, the average slope for our area of interest is slightly larger than 10 %.
Based on the summary of known solifluction areas by Matsuoka [7] and the discussed cases in the
Master Thesis of Vatne [33], it is likely that solifluction could be encountered at the area where
the considered mast is located. This is why in the next section this particular soil mass movement
phenomenon is discussed into further detail. Note that this does not mean that other type of mass
movements could not occur in our area of interest.
As frost heave and thaw weakening is one of the main driving processes involved in solifluction, it is
opted to discuss the soil frost action before solifluction is discussed into further detail.
2.1
Soil Frost Action
Andersland and Ladanyi describe soil frost action as the combination of frost heave during a downward
advance of the freezing front followed by a loss of strength during the spring thaw [4]. Indeed, frost
heave can also occur in locations with no permafrost present, as the only requirement for soil frost
action is the freezing and thawing cycle of a certain part of the (upper) soil.
In areas with permafrost, only the upper soil layer thaws during the spring and summer period.
The thawed layer of the soil is called the active layer and lays consequently on top of the deeper
permafrost layer. Frost heave will occur in the active layer and there will be consequently a frozen
top, followed by a thawed layer and then by the frozen permafrost.
First, frost heave is discussed into further detail. The main driving process of frost heave, is the ice
nucleation and growth of ice lenses and the continuous supply of unfrozen water which facilitates the
nucleation process continuously in time [4, 5, 6]. It is possible to describe the frost heave process
based on several models and theories. Examples are explanations based on capillary theory, the
theory of secondary frost heave with discrete ice lenses and the frozen fringe, and coupled heat-fluid
transport model [34]. In this case, it is opted to explain the frost heave phenomenon by using
the model based on thermal osmosis proposed by Førland et al. in 1988 [5]. The explanation is
complemented with some aspects from the quasi-static model for ice lens growth proposed by Ji et
al. in 2019 [6].
Frost heave is encountered almost exclusively in fine-grained soils, and especially in silts. As the supply
water is crucial for having a significant amount of frost heave, the capillary rise and permeability of
16
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
the soil are important factors to consider. The capillarity of the soil is larger with decreasing grain
size, while the permeability decreases with decreasing grain size. Hence, these two effects cancel
each other out with decreasing grain size such that generally silt has the optimal grain size compared
to clay or sand to be susceptible significantly for frost heave.
It is important to note that when the atmospheric conditions allow the freezing front to propagate
deeper into fine-grained granular soils, then the unfrozen water is absorbed onto the soil particle
surfaces and remains liquid, and in equilibrium at subzero temperatures as discussed before [6]. This
fact is important to keep in mind during the thermodynamic description of frost heave, as the water
phase influences the heat and water transport.
The volume expansion during frost heave is caused due to the presence of two types of water in
saturated soil [5]. Firstly, there is bound water, which is located in the pores and does not cause the
loss of soil particle contact. In other words, this water is present in the pore volume corresponding to
the stress and consolidation state of the soil. On the other hand, there is also free or excess water.
The presence of this water causes the destruction of original soil particle contact and leads to soil
strength decrease.
During frost heave, it is observed that the soil expands [5] and that the increase in volume is however
more than the corresponding volume increase of the freezing of only the bound water in the unfrozen
soil. This implies that during the freezing of the soil, water migrates to the formed ice lenses and
hence excess water can be built up [5].
For the next part of the discussion, it is convenient to divide a vertical soil column with frost heave
occurring in four zones as shown in Figure 2.4. Through the zones, there will be transport of water
and heat during frost heave. This transport will occur in two steps.
During the first step of the process, the capillary rise through the fine grains of the unfrozen soil
transports water through the connected pore space upward from level 1 (ground water level) to level
2 (start of frozen fringe). In the meant time, there is a transport of water from level 2 upwards. As
there is no access to air, the voids must be filled by water from below. The transport of water is
consequently driven by both the capillary rise from the water table, and the pressure reduction at
level 2 due to the upward migration of water in the partly frozen soil. As also shown in Figure 2.4,
there is a temperature gradient present throughout the vertical soil column. In between the levels 1
and 2, this gradient is the main reason for an upward heat flux.
Level 2 is the start of the frozen fringe and is depicted schematically in Figure 2.5. At level 2, the
ice and unfrozen water is in equilibrium. Furthermore, based on the laws of Thermodynamics, it is
known that the relative amounts of both water and ice at level 2 can be changed without changing
the Gibbs free energy. Hence, as fluid water reaches level 2, it freezes, while fluid water transported
from level 2 originates from the melting of the present ice as will be explained more in detail in the
second step of the frost heave process.
17
==PAGE:43==
Chapter 2. Literature Study

Figure 2.4: Schematic representation of frost heave based on Førland et al. [5].
At the start of the second step, the ice will melt at level 2 and the resulting unfrozen water will be
transported through the fringe according to the thermal gradient, i.e. in the direction of the lower
temperature which is upwards. This type of transport is called thermal osmosis. It cannot be stressed
enough that in this zone the temperature is below zero, but with unfrozen water due to the special
conditions in the capillaries. Once the temperatures are low enough, the majority of the unfrozen
water will freeze and a frozen crust is formed in the upper part of the vertical soil column. This crust
does not allow water transport.
It is possible that thermal osmosis occurs over a membrane. In this case, it can be assumed that
the partly frozen soil acts as the membrane, as at the boundaries of this part of the soil section
absorption or release of heat is taking place. Indeed, at level 2 (latent) heat is absorbed as the ice is
melting and at level 3 (latent) heat is released as ice is formed. Note that level 3 is located below
the frozen crust, such that the ice lens formation and grow causes an increase of pressure. This
increase of pressure under the crust acts on both the soil skeleton and in the pores around level 3.
Once the pores at level 3 are filled with ice and the soil skeleton breaks at a weak point due to the
increased pressure, the ice crystal can continue to grow. Eventually, an ice lens is formed below the
frozen crust and the growth of this ice lens continues until the transport of water to level 3 is ceased
or when the crust breaks.
The location of level 3 is in function of the atmospheric temperature. If the temperature decreases,
the crust thickness increases and the other way round. If the crust thickness is increasing, it is
possible that a new ice lens is growing below an earlier formed lens. This causes the creation of
layers of lenses between soil layers.
18
==PAGE:44==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
Figure 2.5: Schematic representation of the fringe, i.e. the partly frozen soil [6].
If the atmospheric temperature is increasing and positive temperatures are reached, the ice in the
soil will start to melt and water will be released. As in the frozen crust there is a significant amount
of excess water present due to the transport of water during the ice lens formation, the soil will lose
its strength significantly.
2.2
Solifluction
Following the broad definition of solifluction as stated by Matsuoka, it covers all phenomena of slow
material movement under the influence of gravity that is associated with freeze-thaw actions and it
is generally observed in sandy to silty soils with low liquid and plasticity indices [7].
There are several types of solifluctions as shown in Figure 2.7. The classification is based both on
the location of the particles involved in the solifluction and the extent of movement which is linked
to the depth where ice lenses develop during frost heave [7].
The first discussed category of solifluction is frost creep. There are two main types of frost creep:
diurnal, i.e. daily, and annual [7]. In general, the term frost creep covers the down slope movement
of soil particles originating from the frost heave and thawing cycles as shown in Figure 2.6. Indeed,
a soil particle experiences an upward movement during frost heave, perpendicularly on the slope.
During thawing, the same soil particle will settle and move vertically downward due to the effects of
gravity over a distance of ∆f. After several freeze-thaw cycles, the soil particle will have moved over
a horizontal distance ∆s.
The difference between diurnal and annual creep is determined by the period that is needed to
complete one freeze-thaw cycle which influences on its turn the frost penetration depth and the depth
where ice lenses are formed [7]. Consequently, for diurnal frost creep the soil particle movement is
situated just below the surface, while for annual frost creep the movement occurs some centimetres
below the surface. The latter two types of solifluctions are illustrated in Figure 2.7 B and C. Note
that annual frost creep is occurring in permafrost areas, while diurnal frost creep is occurring in areas
with no permafrost present.
19
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
∆f
∆s
α
Figure 2.6: Schematic illustration of the working mechanism of solifluction after several freeze-thaw
cycles.
Another category of solifluction is needle ice creep (NIC) as depicted as A in Figure 2.7. NIC is in fact
a special case of diurnal frost creep as one or some soil particles on or close to the surface experience
an upward movement due to the formation of ice needles [7]. Ice needles form during nocturnal
cooling where the air temperature drops just below 0 ○C. During this relatively limited period the
frost penetration of the soil is limited to the uppermost centimetres. An additional condition to limit
the frost penetration, is that the directly underlying soil is still warm which is the case in early winter.
The formed ice needles are mechanically weak, such that they can break. This causes on its turn the
downhill movement of the lifted soil particle(s).
In general, all types of diurnal freeze-thaw cycles cause a superficial but relatively rapid downhill
material movement [7].
Gelifluction is shown in Figure 2.7 C, as it is also associated with seasonal thawing and is often
difficult to identify and distinguish from annual frost creep and other components of the total soil
movement. Gelifluction can be caused by both the presence of a plastic soil layer that is a result
of thaw consolidation and by the influx of additional water from melt or rain. In combination with
prefailure stresses, the increased water content will cause soil deformations.
Lastly, there is the plug-like flow solifluction type. This type of solifluction is exclusive for areas with
cold permafrost in the subsurface [7] as in these areas two-sided freezing can occur. The latter is the
ice lens growth both from the lower and upper part of the active layer due to large thermal gradient
at the bottom of the active layer. As there will be in this case ice lenses at the bottom of the active
layer, the thawing of these lenses will induce movement of the whole active layer along this plane as
a plug. This type of solifluction is depicted in Figure 2.7 D. This type of failure may also involve
gelifluction along the deeper plane where the movement will occur [7].
20
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
Figure 2.7: Schematic representation of the different types of solifluctions [7].
2.3
Vegetation
At the start of the literature study, the effect of variations in vegetation and the peat layer on the
thermal regime and state of the permafrost was mentioned as an important influencing factor. That
is why this section gives more details on the topic, especially as during the field work and drone
footage processing significant vegetation changes around Mast 9 were observed.
In general, local factors such as vegetation variation, snow cover variation and water content variation,
overrule, or are at least as much significant as the macroclimatic factors when the influence on the
thermal regime is considered [32]. Hence, especially in the discontinuous zone, the detection of
permafrost proves to be effective when surface indicators such as vegetation and snow, are studied.
Indeed, the vegetation cover is according to Duguay et al. one of the best indicators regarding the
21
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
spatial distribution of permafrost conditions in the upper 2 m of the subsurface [32]. This is mainly
due to the interaction of the vegetation and the soil in the upper layer regarding water and nutrient
intake, and rooting as frozen soil acts as an impervious layer [35]. As the possibility of water intake
and the lower boundary in the upper soil layer of this water intake, influences the appearance and
thrive possibility of certain species, it is possible to use the predominance of the occurring species to
conclude in what state the upper soil layers are. An example is that shallow-rooted species, such as
tamarack and white and black spruce, tend to be thriving in areas with very thin active layers. More
deep-rooted species, such as willows and sages, tend to be more often found when the active layer
thickness increases. The height of these bushes and trees increases with increasing active layer as
well. Pure dense willow stands indicate generally that the subsurface is saturated and that the active
layer thickness is significant.
An important note to the generalisations above is that there are of course exceptions, and that
drawing conclusions only based on the vegetation occurring on the considered site should never be
used as the only indicator of the permafrost conditions. The vegetation is however an additional
indicator in combination with other data.
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.8: (a) Tamarack [8] and (b) a willow specie found in Greenland (Salix Arctica) [9]
The first layer of the sub-surface is often an organic, peaty-layer which is known to have seasonally
dependent ground-insulating abilities [32]. This layer is connected with the active layer conditions
and hence also with the occurring vegetation. If this organic layer is dry, it is a good insulator, while
in wet state it is a better energy conductor. In general, a thin active layer is found in areas with
a significant peaty, organic layer. On the other hand, mosses and marsh sedges prove to have a
significantly lower beneficial effect on the protection of the thermal regime of the permafrost.
Apart from the insulating effects of the organic layer, vegetation provides direct shading of direct
sunlight such that the surface temperature is decreased [35]. As permafrost exists at each moment
in time in a certain energy equilibrium, the disturbance of the vegetation might cause the destruction
of this equilibrium and consequently the permafrost as well.
As vegetation is one of the important factors of the existence conditions of permafrost, and as
vegetation species have certain optimal thriving conditions, both permafrost degradation and the
state of the permafrost can be deduced based on the occurring species. In the case of permafrost
degradation, or a change in the thermal regime, it is known that a shift in vegetation species is
induced [35]. Especially in ice rich permafrost, the thaw induces the presence of excess water which
22
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
cannot infiltrate that easily due to deeper impervious frozen soil layers. Hence, the shift from a
terrestrial ecosystem to a wetland ecosystem occurs, which can lead on its turn in a secondary
succession. Secondary successions are a change in vegetation after a disturbance of the original
vegetation, but of an insufficient severity such that not all the existing flora is removed and destructed
[36]. An example of such a secondary succession is the decrease of the population of trees that grow
on mound edges with clayey soils and shallower permafrost table due to the formation of “drunken”
forests under the disturbances of thermokarsting as shown in Figure 2.9 (a). A ’drunken’ forest is
the description of a forest where trees started to tilt due to the permafrost degradation. These trees
will eventually die and fall into the scar bog [10]. thermokarsting on the other hand, is a term with
a broad and changing definition through history [37]. The definition used nowadays implies that
thermokarsting is the collection of processes during which characteristic landforms are created due
to the thawing of the ice-rich permafrost or due to the melting of massive ice. The characteristic
landforms appear due to the consolidation of the soil and due to deformations, both caused by
the melting of the significant amount of ice. Theremokarst lakes and pits are also schematically
illustrated in Figure 2.9 (b).
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.9: (a) Illustration of permafrost degradation in an arctic forest [10] and (b) illustration of
the change in vegetation due to permafrost degradation in an Arctic tundra climate [11].
In Greenland, Arctic tundra climates are almost exclusively encountered, so in the last part of this
23
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
section more detailed information about the vegetation composition and vegetation dynamics for this
typical Greenlandic climate is given.
As already indicated by Figure 2.9 (b), there are no trees in a tundra climate, due to low summer
temperatures, low annual precipitation and short growing seasons [11]. The typically encountered
vegetation species in Arctic tundra climate, are patchy, low-ground vegetation comprising shrubs,
graminoids such as sedges, grasses and rushes, forbs, mosses, and linches [11].
Graminoids generally prefer poorly drained, very saturated soil conditions, while better drained,
more elevated or sloping areas are drier and shrubs are more likely to found on those locations.
Shrubs still like relatively moist soils, but they cannot tolerate waterlogged conditions. On the
contrary, sedges have the ability to thrive in anaerobic water-saturated environments. An example of
vegetation succession, based on the aforementioned specie-specific characteristics is as follows [10]:
With persistent thermokarsting for instance, warmer soils and improving drainage may eventually
favour shrubs and deciduous trees. In addition, bare soil from a thaw slump is initially colonized
by graminoids and forbs. In a next stage of the vegetation succession, the originally thaw slump is
further colonised by forbs and dwarf shrubs and then after a few hundred years bryophytes will start
to thrive. All the relevant vegetation species for this example are summarised in Figure 2.10.
(a) Shrub: Dwarf birch
(b) Graminoid: Dark Brown Sedge
(c) Graminoid: Cotton grass

2.4
Thermosyphons
Stable permafrost is intrinsically a very strong material to build on. However, once the permafrost
starts to thaw, the strength of the material is lost, as depending on the ice content, severely saturated
and weak soils are created. Especially due to the climate change, and the Arctic Amplification [31],
there is a risk that a stable and strong permafrost subsurface can change during the design life
span of the construction into a weak and oversaturated subsurface. Therefore, engineers have been
looking for solutions to this issue.
2.4.1
Introduction
One of the possibilities to ensure that the permafrost remains stable during the designed life span
of the construction, is to ensure that the surrounding permafrost remains stable. This method has
been used for the first time by the US Army Corps of Engineers in 1960 during the construction of
some communication sites in Alaska [38]. In that project, each of the legs of the communication
tower were fitted with steel pipes, filled with propane that would function as the refrigerant of the
surrounding soil [38]. This type of pile foundation was called a ’Thermopile’, which is short for
’Thermosyphon pile’, and was accepted on the International Conference on Permafrost in 1963 as a
viable method for ensuring the construction of a stable foundation in relatively warm permafrost in
Alaska [38]. The communication site in Alaska has been operational since 1960, proving that this
method can be very effective [38].
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.11: (a) View on the Trans Alaska Pipeline and schematic illustration of the used thermosyphon
technology [12] and (b) an example of a thermosyphon pile from a communication tower in Kwigillinok,
Alaska [13].
25
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
Another interesting project where thermosyphons have been operating outstandingly, is the construc-
tion of the Trans Alaska Pipeline which started in 1970 [38] and was completed in 1977 [13]. The
purpose of this pipeline is to carry a vast amount of oil each day, originating from the Beaufort
Sea to the harbour of Valdez in the South of Alaska [12], and for its construction there have been
approximately 120000 units of thermosyphons used [13]. The design of the used thermosyphons is
shown in Figure 2.11 (a). A thermosyphon’s ability to cool the surface originates from the phase
change of the refrigerant in the syphon, and there are many chemicals to choose from. In this case,
ammonia was initially used until during technical inspections in the mid 1980’s it was discovered that
in the upper parts of the thermosyphon hydrogen gas was accumulated which is a non-condensable
gas [38]. This phenomenon is called ’cold-topping’ in literature. In order to solve the issue, the
refrigerant was changed to carbon dioxide in all thermosyphons [38] and this made the thermosyphons
perform according to their original performance. Nowadays, carbon dioxide is almost exclusively
used as refrigerant [38], as it is a much less harmful chemical compared to ammonia or propane
and no cold-topping is observed with systems operating with carbon dioxide. Cold-topping is the
phenomenon that the top of the thermosyphon gets filled by non-condensing gasses which are formed
due to the corrosion of the material of the thermosyphon [13].
2.4.2
Technology
The original design of a thermosyphon, as applied for instance at the Trans Alaska pipeline, consisted
of a pressurised, sealed pipe which could operate in a passive way. Its operation is exclusively
controlled by the temperature difference between the soil and the air [39], i.e. there will be only heat
transfer from the soil outwards when the temperature at the condenser is lower than the temperature
at the evaporator. In order to establish the ability to transport heat out of the soil, one part of the
pressurised pipe is buried in the soil and is denoted as the evaporator, while the upper part of the
pipe sticks out above the surface and functions as the condenser. The condenser is often equipped
with fins, which enhance the condensation of the refrigerant as a larger surface area is exposed to
the wind. A schematic representation of this type of thermosyphon, also simply called a passive
thermosyphon, is shown in Figure 2.12.
The passive system ensures a one way, i.e. outward, heat transport as follows [13]. Once a significant
temperature difference between condenser and evaporator is occurring, the pressurised gas in liquid
phase at the evaporator-end of the syphon vaporises. During this endothermic phase change, heat
is extracted from the surrounding soil. The vapour rises next towards the condenser through the
adiabatic section of the thermosyphon and changes phase by condensing upon contact with the
walls of the condenser. During this second exothermic phase change, there is release of latent heat
which is on its turn released towards the outer air. During the latter phase change, the pressurised
gas returns to its liquid phase and hence it returns under the influence of gravity to the evaporator.
Furthermore, the phase change also causes a slight decrease in pressure which enhances the rise of
the vapour towards the condenser as well [38].
An important note is that condensation of the refrigerant only occurs if the air temperature is lower
than the saturation temperature of the pressurised gas [13]. Consequently, this is also a condition to
have natural convection in the syphon, where the absorbed heat from the soil is transported and
released to the outer air. Once the air temperature is higher than the soil temperature, the outward
heat transfer stops and the thermosyphon becomes dormant and ensures that the summer heat is not
transported into the soil [38]. A major benefit of thermosyphons is the limited amount of required
maintenance [38]. Generally, the only maintenance that has to be carried out is checking the soil
temperature remains in the range where the pressurised gas can extract the designed heat and that
the units are still operational. The best way to ensure the operation of the system is by monitoring
the ground temperature at the evaporator and the air temperature [13].
26
==PAGE:52==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
Figure 2.12: Schematic representation of a passive thermosyphon [13].
There are in our current days several types of thermosyphons developed. Firstly, the way how the
convection cycle is facilitated, yields in the following categorisation of thermosyphons [13]:
• Passive thermosyphons
• Active thermosyphons: The convection cycle is driven by a heat pump which enables the
use of the system in a broad range of temperature climates.
• Hybrid thermosyphons: These systems also make use of a heat pump to facilitate the
convection cycle. However, when the air temperature is below 0 ○C, the natural convection
takes it over from the heat pump which allows the reduction of energy costs.
Apart from the categorisation based on the convection cycle driver, there are several types of
thermosyphons depending on the application as shown in Figure 2.13.
The vertical thermopiles have already been discussed earlier in this section.
Sloped thermosyphons, as shown in Figure 2.13 (b), is a thermosyphon design typically used to
cool the sub-surface below heated at-grade structures, such as buildings. The condenser will remain
vertical, and the evaporator has typically a slope of 3 to 10 %.
Flat thermosyphons on the other hand are a variation on the sloped type, and bring the extra difficulty
that the evaporator needs to be placed exactly horizontal.
The other types of thermosyphons as sown in Figure 2.13 (d) and (e) are further not discussed in
this section, as they are not relevant regarding the cooling of soil around piles.
27
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
Figure 2.13: Schematic representation of several thermosyphon designs [13].
2.4.3
Performance
In order to design a thermosyphon which functions over the design life span as efficiently as possible
in the expected ambient and soil conditions, it is important to consider the following factors during
the design process.
In general, it is stated that the heat transfer rate of a thermosyphon is influenced by the choice of a
refrigerant, the symmetry of the design, the used material for the thermosyphon and the surrounding
environment [13]. In this section, each influencing factor is discussed into further detail.
First, the effects of the chosen refrigerant on the performance of the thermosyphon is discussed.
As the whole system relies on the cyclic behaviour of the refrigerant, it is crucial that the chosen
chemical substance has a sufficiently low viscosity combined with a sufficiently high density. This
combination will allow the condensed pressurised gas to move down towards the evaporator.
As the refrigerant is expected to work in significant sub-zero temperatures, it is important that the
freezing point is compatible with this. Furthermore, to ensure a sufficient amount of heat transport,
the refrigerant should have a sufficiently high thermal conductivity.
Next, regarding the vapour pressure the following requirements should be met. As the vapour
pressure measures the tendency of the refrigerant to change into the gaseous or vapour state, with
an increased tendency with increasing temperature [40], it is crucial that this vapour pressure is
sufficiently high at low temperatures to ensure that the convection cycle can exist.
A last requirement regarding the refrigerant, is that it has to be chemically pure, stable and compatible
with the material used for the thermosyphon [13].
The dimensions of the thermosyphon, such as the length of the evaporator, the area of the condenser
and the diameter of the pipe, also influence the performance of the system. As a larger area of the
condenser is beneficial for the performance, fins are used frequently as already indicated in Figure
2.12.
Apart from the symmetry and dimensions, the material of the pipe, fin design and material and
surface treatment of the material are obviously known to affect the performance as well. Lastly, soil
properties, air temperatures, wind velocities and the season of installation are the last significant
influencing factors. One argument regarding the choice of the optimal season of construction, is that
during wintertime the passive thermosyphon could start immediately with cooling the surrounding
soils, such that the thermal disturbance due to the construction can be minimised. The performance
28
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
of the thermosyphon increases with increasing wind speed as well.
The analytical characterisation of the heat extraction performance of thermosyphons is discussed
next and based on the work of Long and Zarling (2004) [13]. The heat flux q in a thermosyphon
(W) can be expressed by the following expression.

sake of conciseness, it is sufficiently accurate to only consider the resistance to heat flow between the
soil and the evaporator (Rs) and between the air and the condenser (Rf). Both resistance factors are
heavily dependent on for instance wind speed and the evaporator slope. Hence, there exist empirical

2.4.4
Limitations
A first important note is that passive thermosyphons need in many cases some time to achieve
significant cooling and refreezing of the surrounding soil [38]. If rapid cooling is required, it is
recommended to use an active or a hybrid thermosyphon system instead, with the hybrid system
being the most cost-efficient and flexible system [38].
One should also pay extra attention in the case the permafrost is shallow or when it is expected to
have unfrozen water below the tip of a vertical evaporator [38]. This could cause the formation of
ice lenses and hence also heave.
As thermosyphons are the most cost-effective when used in long-term cooling, it is crucial to account
for climate change during the design of the thermosyphon [38]. A good rule of thumb is that the
system should be still operational after three of the warmest anticipated seasons have occurred in a
row. Therefore, the design should include the application of accurate and reliable thermal modelling
and the final decision about the design should be finalised with the addition of experience from the
field [38].
Lastly, vertical thermosyphons might involve a hazard as they can be damaged more easily than other
type of thermosyphons [13]. If the vertical units are close to motorised traffic, a collision poses a risk
29
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
to damage the unit. Another danger is the possibility of puncturing the thermosyphon by a bullet.
Both damages can be however repaired relatively easily and as carbon dioxide is not a toxic chemical
substance, the units do not pollute the environment when damaged.
2.5
Theoretical background
During several stages of this Thesis, there was a need to apply some specific methods to obtain some
important characteristics of the investigated soil. Therefore, before the actual methods and results
are discussed, it is intended to introduce some of the methods in a theoretical framework.
The first method which is discussed is the Electrical Resistivity Tomography (ERT) method which
was used in this case to map mainly the frozen and unfrozen soil layers around the mast during the
fieldwork. During the lab work at DTU, the temperature of the soil in function of time after emitting
a heat pulse was firstly measured in order to obtain information about the thermal characteristics of
the soil. Apart from knowing information about the thermal behaviour, the permittivity measurements
of the soil provided data about the temperature dependency of the unfrozen water content.
Lastly, it is important to know what the salinity is of the pore water, as it influences for instance the
unfrozen water content in function of the temperature. Hence, the procedure to obtain data about
the salinity is discussed to conclude the literature study.
2.5.1
Electrical Resistivity Tomography
The Electrical Resistivity Tomography (ERT) method is a geoelectrical or direct current (DC) method
which allows the mapping of the subsurface resistivity [14]. The mapping of the electrical resistivity of
the subsurface is certainly relevant as it is an indicative parameter which allows to make a distinction
between certain geological formations [14]. In the case permafrost is studied, this method proves to
be extremely useful to identify unfrozen and frozen soil layers, and also bedrock in the considered
subsurface. Indeed, the resistivity of ice is significantly higher compared to the resistivity of water as
the latter is indeed known to conduct electrical current relatively efficiently.
Apparent resistivity
The basic concept where the ERT method is built on, is the measuring of the resistivity ρ of the
earth. ρ is often also denoted as the specific electrical resistance and has the unit Ohm⋅m (Ωm) [14].
An increasing value of ρ is translated to an increased resistance of the soil to conduct an electrical
current, hence the following relation between the conductivity and resistivity is true:
ρ = 1
σ
(2.5)
with σ being the electrical conductivity with unit Siemens per meter (S/m).
In the most simple case, measurements of the resistivity are performed with four electrodes, sym-
metrically placed with respect to a midpoint as schematically illustrated in Figure 2.14 [14]. The
resistivity is obtained by passing an electric current through two of the electrodes, indicated by A and
B in Figure 2.14, and this results also in an electrical current through the ground. As the current
electrodes, i.e. electrode A and B, are the two outer electrodes, the two inner electrodes can be used
to measure the potential difference over these two electrodes caused by the electrical current passing
through the ground. Hence, the two inner electrodes are called the potential or receiver electrodes
and are indicated with M and N in Figure 2.14. The measured potential difference depends on the
distance between the electrodes, the transmitted current and the resistivity distribution in the ground.
30
==PAGE:56==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
It is important to note that the resistivity distribution in the subsurface, i.e. the sequence of soil
layers with each a certain resistivity, cannot be directly measured with these methods. The electrical
current and the potential difference only allow the direct determination of the apparent resistivity ρa
as will be discussed further in this section. Indeed, in order to obtain the actual layering of the soil
based on the resistivity, it is needed to perform a mathematical inversion procedure. Such a procedure
is an iterative method which starts with an assumption of the layering of the soil and this layering is
adapted until the assumed layering of the soil produces such an electrical field which matches with
the measured apparent resistivity. More details regarding the mathematics of an analytical inversion
are given in the last paragraph of this section.
Figure 2.14: Schematic representation of the two current (A and B) and the two potential (M and
N) electrodes [14]. The electrical current and the potential field in a homogeneous medium are also
shown.
The most simple assumption of the layering of the subsurface based on the resistivity, is a one
dimensional model (1D) as shown in Figure 2.15 (a) [14]. In such a model, it is assumed that the
subsurface consists of horizontal, homogeneous, and isotropic soil layers with parallel and plane
boundaries with respect to the surface (h1 = 0). It is also assumed that Ohm’s law is valid in the soil
layers. The considered soil layers in the model are characterised by a thickness di and a resistivity ρi
with i being the index of the considered soil layer. This type of soil model was in the past the only
model that could be used to obtain results in a reasonable amount of time [14]. However, nowadays
more complex and more accurate 2D and 3D models are used, where the resistivity and the thickness
of soil layers changes in respectively two and three directions instead of only in function of depth.
Especially with the multi-electrode systems, i.e. configurations where several electrodes are put on
a long line such that many more sets of four electrodes, as shown in Figure 2.14, with a different
inter-distance are considered. This allows to map larger areas and increases the investigation depth
as shown in Figure 2.15 (b) [14].
In order to link the measurable current and potential difference with the resistivity, Ohm’s law is
used which describes the proportionality between the injected current and the measured potential as
follows:
∆V = R ⋅I
(2.6)
with ∆V being the potential difference over the resistor, R being the resistance and I being the
current through the resistor. If the resistance of a larger medium is considered and not just of a
31
==PAGE:57==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
resistor used in electrical engineering; Ohm’s law is reformulated as follows:
E = ρ ⋅j
(2.7)
with E being the electric field vector with unit Volt per meter (V/m), ρ the resistivity and j the
current density vector with unit Ampere per square meter (A/m2). Note that the resistivity ρ is not
written yet as the apparent resistivity to keep Ohm’s law general.
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.15: (a) 1D soil model [14] and (b) illustrating the increased investigation depth with an
increased inter-distance between the electrodes [14].
To determine now the resistivity from the measured current and potential, an assumption needs to
be made for the layering of the subsurface. In the simplest case, when the subsurface is assumed to
be a homogeneous halfspace as shown in Figure 2.16, the potential V can be determined in function
of the distance from the electrode in radial coordinates as follows [14]:
V (r) = ρI
2πr
(2.8)
with V being the potential, ρ the resistivity, I the current and r is the distance from the electrode
to the point where the potential is determined in a radial coordinate system. Note that the other
current electrode is infinitely far away from the considered electrode in Figure 2.16. An equipotential
field, as shown in Figure 2.14 and Figure 2.16, is always perpendicular to a current field. Along an
equipotential line, a charge experiences the same potential energy, and it does not require any work
to move a charge along equipotential lines.

electrode which is depicted as an arrow [14].
