As already mentioned, the depth of investigation of ERT measurements depends on the distance
between electrodes. A good rule of thumb for the penetration depth of measurements performed
with the Wenner protocol is ≈0.3a [14]. This means that the measured apparent resistivity is in
fact the average of resistivities in a range of depths of [0.1a,0.75a], with the main weight at 0.3a
depth. It is important to note that this rule of thumb should be used with caution, as it is based on
a homogeneous halfspace [14].
Sources of Error
In the proposed method to investigate the resistivity of the soil layers in the surveyed area based on
geoelectric measurements and inversion calculation methods, it is clear that there is a risk for errors
in every step from the data acquisition to the final resistivity profile. In general, the possible errors
can be divided into three categories: data inaccuracy, model inconsistency and errors of interpretation
[14].
33
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
First, a major source of potential errors is during the data collection in the field. It is possible that
for instance some of the electrodes do not have proper contact with the soil, due to the presence of
ice or gravel/rocks. This can prevent the potential measurements and the conduction of current
through the soil.
Secondly, regarding the assumed 1D model which will be matched with the measured apparent
resistivities, it is fact that this model only represents reality approximately. The layer thickness,
anisotropy and changing soil properties may be unknown and be sources for error.
Lastly, due to inaccuracies in the data, and due to a systematically limited amount of available and
known data, and due to inherent limitations of the geoelectrical method, an interpretation of any
result will always be prone to errors [14].
An important note to make regarding sources of error during the measuring of the data is the following.
As it is a simplification of reality to assume that Ohm’s law is valid at all times for conduction of
current in soil [14], it is not true that the stationary value of the current, is immediately realised
on the electrode under the influence of an applied voltage. Indeed, in reality, the value of a current
caused by an applied voltage rises from zero to its stationary value gradually. Furthermore, when
the application of the voltage ceases, the actual voltage present in the electrode will not disappear
immediately and will instead decay over a short time interval. These phenomena are called Induced
Polarisation (IP) and are observed in soil as soil is both an Ohmic resistor and has a capacitance
[14]. Ohm’s law is in other words not valid when current and voltage are changing, the law is only
valid during the stationary state of the current and the voltage.
Inversion methods
All physical laws are formulated in a forward manner, which implies that the distribution in space and
time of all the relevant physical parameters are known. Indeed, the outcome of a certain measurement
can be predicted by solving the equations that govern the problem. In other words, it is possible to
calculate the model response directly based on measurements. However, knowing everything about a
physical system is the most ideal situation possible, and as stated by Christensen and Christiansen
[14] this state of affairs seems ideal for an omniscient being unfolding the universe, but when mortal
humans wish to extend their knowledge of their surroundings based on measured data, we have the
opposite problem, an inverse problem.
Such an inverse problem implies that the applicable model has to be identified based on a set of
discrete data. In this particular case for ERT data processing, the layer resistivities and thicknesses
of a 1D geoelectrical model from apparent resistivity data have to be determined.
The majority of mathematical and numerical methods are designed to solve problems in a forward
manner, meaning that there is generally no direct solution to the inverse problem [14]. Instead, the
best and only approach is to solve the forward problem using a range of models and then compare
the results with the actual measured data to determine which model is best in accordance with the
data. Geophysical inversion techniques aim to do just that by selecting the most efficient sequence
of forward calculations to minimize the computational load. Essentially, the goal is to wisely select
models and parameters that will provide the best match with the observed data [14].
As there are only very few linear inverse problems in geophysics, mostly nonlinear inverse problems
need to be solved and this generally by using locally valid linear approximations to the functional
relationship. Convergence of the solution is then obtained by updating the mode1l and the locally
linear approximations and repeating the process until convergence is reached.
Experience teaches us that for geoelectrical data, a logarithmic transformation of the apparent
resistivity, the resistivity and the soil layer thickness in the 1D model facilitates to linearise the inverse
problem more [14]. Indeed, ln(ρa) is a more linear function of ln(ρi) and ln(di) than the relation
between the three characteristics without applying a logarithmic transformation. At the same time,
the transformation ensures that none of the model parameters can become negative [14].
34
==PAGE:60==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
In most cases, an iterative, least-squares inversion method is used to solve the actual inversion
problem [14, 42]. This method has two main objectives: (i) the identification of a model that is as
compliant as possible with the data and (ii) the assessment of the reliability of the result by means
of a uncertainty analysis on the model parameters.
A least squares criterion is practical in use to quantify the extend of fit between a model and measured
data and the residual that needs to be minimised can be expressed as in Equation 2.15.
Resdata =

the natural logarithm of the apparent resistivity based on
the considered model and ∆lnρdata

normalisation by the latter term is beneficial, as by doing so, Resdata = 1 implies that on average
the measured data have been fitted as accurately as possible, considering the limitation of accuracy
due to noise originating from the data [14].
The iterative inversion process consists in general out of the following steps in order to reach
convergence [14]:
1. Initial model: The model response and residual are determined, based on a first reasonable
guess of the final model.
2. Linearisation: In order to know how the model response changes in function of changing
model parameters, the first derivative of the response with respect to all M model- and all N
data parameters is determined. This is later also used for a Taylor expansion of the model
response. The set of equations that need to be solved is given below and consists of N
equations with M unknowns.
D1 −y0

(2.16)
with Di the i-th data point from the measurements, y0

model and ∆pj is equal to the change of the j-th model parameter. Note that the indices i
and j have a range i = 1...N and j = 1...M respectively.
3. Updating model: The model parameter pj is now updated according to the obtained
parameter change:
pj →pj + ∆pj
(2.17)
Once the updated model parameter value is known for all M model parameters, the new model
response is determined. Based on this new model output, the new residual is calculated.
4. Iterative inversion: steps 2 and 3 are now repeated until the residual change is below a
certain preset threshold value.
It is important to note the following regarding the uniqueness of a final model after one inversion
process is completed with a certain starting model. It is known that there exist a range of different
35
==PAGE:61==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
models that will produce model responses identical within the uncertainty of the measurements [14].
The collection of these models is the so called equivalent models.
Geoelectrical data inversion results always in multiple equivalent models, as there is in every case an
uncertainty on the model parameters as discussed before. The number of equivalent models can
however differ per inversion of a certain data set of measurements.
Furthermore, it is possible that these equivalent models differ significantly. Hence, it is important to
consider these differences by considering the uncertainties on the model parameters. Indeed, when
the model uncertainties are small, the differences between equivalent models will be small as well. In
other cases, a similar model response is produced by a wide range of different models, implying that
one or several model parameters are prone to high uncertainties. It is obviously important to be aware
of the latter type of situations, as they express the fundamental limitations of geoelectrical methods.
There are mainly three categories in the latter equivalence case: high resistivity equivalence, low
resistivity equivalence and layer suppression.
High resistivity equivalence is encountered when a high resistivity layer is over- and underlain by
low(er) resistivity layers [14]. This causes a significant increase in uncertainties regarding the layer
thickness determination of the high resistivity layer. Furthermore the depth of the deepest, low(er)
resistivity layer is uncertain as well.
Next, low resistivity equivalence is the opposite situation compared to the high resistivity equivalence
[14]. Indeed, when a low resistivity layer is over- and underlain by a higher resistivity layer, it is
quasi-impossible to be certain about the thickness of this low resistivity layer. Furthermore, the
depth of the deepest high resistivity layer is very uncertain. An important note for both high and low
resistivity equivalence is that the equivalent models have exactly the same number of layers.
Lastly, when the set of equivalent models do not have the same number of layers, layer suppression
is encountered when a geoelectric survey can be interpreted by means of a model with fewer layers
than in reality [14].
2.5.2
Thermal properties
In this section, some basic principles of describing thermal behaviour of soils are discussed, as thermal
processes are one of the main driving factors behind permafrost formation and degradation.
General Theory
A precondition of any heat flow in a body, is that different parts of that body have a different
temperature [43]. In that case, the heat will flow from the hotter part towards the cooler part of the
body. This transfer of heat can take place in three distinct ways, i.e. (i) Conduction, where the
heat passes through the portion of the affected material of the body, (ii) Convection, where heat
is transferred by relative motion of portions of the heated body, and (iii) Radiation, where heat
is transferred direct between distant portions of the body by electromagnetic radiation [43]. As in
solids radiation is most of the time negligible and convection is not characteristic for heat transfer in
solids, the focus in this section will be on the conductivity and conduction properties of solids [43].
The conduction of heat is the transfer of thermal energy between two considered regions of matter in
a body, caused by the existence of a thermal gradient. The mathematical description of this process,
is based on the Mathematical Theory of the Conduction of Heat in which the stated hypothesis has
been suggested by the following experiment [43]. The experiment is also illustrated in Figure 2.17.
In this experiment, a plate of some sort of solid is considered, which is placed between two other
planes indicated as respectively Upper and Lower plate in Figure 2.17. The dimensions of the three
plates are of such extent, that it can be assumed that they are infinite, if only some points in the
centre of the three plates are considered. As indicated in Figure 2.17, the upper and lower plate
36
==PAGE:62==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
are kept at different temperatures, however with a temperature difference between the two plates
of such extent that the properties of the solid plate in the middle are not affected. The constant
temperature in the centre of these plates is ensured by a constant (melting) ice supply on the upper
plate and a constant (luke-)warm water flow on the lower plate. In a next step of the experiment,
these boundary conditions at the upper and lower plate are maintained for a sufficiently long time, in
order to allow the points in the limited region at the centre of the three plates to reach a constant,
or so called steady, thermal regime.
ICE
WATER
Tw
Ti
∞←
→∞

Upper plate
Figure 2.17: As built locations of the soil temperature sensors relative to the edge of the foundation
column of the mast. The dimensions are given in meter.
If it is now assumed, that the part of the three plates depicted in Figure 2.17 is in fact a cross
section of a cylinder with surface area S and a height d equal to the thickness of the considered plate
between the upper and lower plate, there is no heat flow through the boundaries of this cylinder.
Next, as Ti < Tw, experiments with a wide variety of solids suggest the following relation between
the heat flow q and the temperature gradient:
q ∼S(Tw −Ti)
d
(2.18)
This relation implies that the heat flux through the considered material, increases with both a
decreasing thickness of the material and an increasing thermal gradient and surface area. If a
proportionality constant is introduced, the equation yields:
q = λS(Tw −Ti)
d
(2.19)
where λ is called the Thermal Conductivity of the considered material, which depends on the material
itself as well. It is important to note that this relation is not proven by this experiment, as the
experiments only suggest that such a law must be valid. The exact verification of the discussed law is
performed by showing agreements between experiments and calculations based on the mathematical
theory which is on its turn based on the validity of this law [43]. Furthermore, it is known that the
conductivity is not constant for a given material, as it varies with temperature [43].
At steady state, where the temperatures in the centre part of the middle plate remain constant, it is
known that lines of equal temperature are in fact parallel with the faces of the plate as shown in
37
==PAGE:63==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
Figure 2.17. Such lines are also called isotherms, and the more general definition is that an isotherm
represents the collection of points where the temperature at the considered instance is the same [43].
As each point of the material cannot have two or more temperatures at the same time, isotherms
cannot cut each other. This property of isotherms ensures furthermore that the thermal gradient is
defined in all points.
For the following, only isotropic continua are considered which are media whose properties are
independent on the considered direction [43]. This implies that the heat flux vector in a certain point
of an isotropic continuum is along the normal to the isothermal surface in that particular point, in
the direction of the decreasing temperature.
If the temperature variation between two (parallel) isotherms with a temperature of respectively T
and T + dT (dT > 0) has an interspacing of dx, the temperature variation over this infinitesimal
distance yields in the following heat flux:
q = λS T −T −dT
dx
= −λS dT
dx
(2.20)
with dT
dx representing the thermal gradient in the considered material. As isotropic continua are
considered, this heat flux is valid in the x, y and z direction. In vector notation Equation 2.20 can be
written compactly per unit area as follows:
f = q
S = −λgrad(T)
(2.21)
Equation 2.21 is also known as the fundamental hypothesis for the Mathematical Theory of Conduction
of Heat [43] and describes the rate at which heat crosses from the inside to the outside of an isothermal
surface per unit area per unit time at a certain point in the isotropic continuum.
The interpretation of the negative sign in the expression in Equation 2.21 is that there is a positive
heat flow in for instance the positive x direction, when with respect to the positive x direction the
temperature decreases, causing a negative thermal gradient in that positive x direction.

Thermal Conductivity
Generally speaking, it is assumed that the thermal conductivity λ is constant for a certain material.
However, as already shortly mentioned, this is not completely true. Indeed, as stated by Carslaw and
Jaeger (1959) [43], the following linear expression is a more accurate equation for the conductivity,
as the temperature dependency is also taken into account:
λ = λ0(1 + βT)
(2.40)
40
==PAGE:66==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
with β being a small and generally negative scalar for most materials and λ0 the thermal conductivity
of the considered material at 0 ○C. This linear equation that describes the temperature dependency
of the thermal conductivity is however not that accurate for permafrost. Indeed, as permafrost
consists out of four phases (ice - water - air - solid), a more formal expression is used in practice to
describe the temperature dependency of permafrost. Andersland and Ladanyi discuss in Chapter 2 of
their book [4] the method that Johansen proposed in 1975, to estimate the thermal conductivity
of saturated permafrost both in frozen and unfrozen state with the geometrical mean method as
expressed in Equation 2.41.

of respectively ice, water and the soil particles. Although permafrost consists generally out of four
phases, the gas phase is not considered as Equation 2.41 is only valid for saturated permafrost.
The unfrozen water fraction can be computed by using one approximation out of many possibilities
as shown in Equation 2.42. This approximation is also recommended by Andersland and Ladanyi [4].
Alternatively it can also be measured as discussed in Section 2.5.3. The porosity can be determined
by performing standardised soil tests as explained in Section 4.1.5 or as an approximation one also
can use values found in literature for the considered soil type.

with α and β characteristic soil parameters tabulated in literature. Note that the total water content
is the sum of the unfrozen water content and the ice content.

2.5.3
Dielectric permittivity
One of the most important soil characteristics is the water content, as it influences for instances
its strength and deformation properties [45]. That is why it is fundamentally important to try to
quantify this soil property as accurately as possible. In lab environments, it is possible to determine
the water content of soil samples by using the time consuming and destructive method from the
norm as discussed in Section 4.1.3, but if it is intended to monitor the water content in situ, or in
function of the soil temperature, a direct and reliable method, based on soil physical properties is
more appropriate.
Topp et al. suggested in 1980 to base conclusions regarding the water content of soils on the
measurements of the complex dielectric permittivity [46]. The complex dielectric permittivity is
a bulk property of matter that gives an indication about the coupling between a relatively weak
electromagnetic field and a material substance. Its value depends on the frequency f of the field,
the molecular constitution of the substance, and the state parameters temperature T and pressure p
[27]. In other words, this complex physical property of matter gives an indication of the ability of a
substance to hold an electrical charge, or to what extend an electric field is permitted to exist in the
considered material [47]. This complex material property can be expressed as [46]:
K∗= εr −jεi
(2.55)
with K∗being the complex dielectric permittivity, εr the real dielectric permittivity, εi the imaginary
dielectric permittivity and j is equal to the square root of −1 [46]. In general, the real part represents
the energy storage in the form of rotational polarisation which is strongly related to the water content
in the soil. The imaginary part on the other hand represents the energy loss and can be used to
estimate the electrical conductivity in soils relatively accurately [47].
One method of measuring the dielectric permittivity is to use an electromagnetic soil sensor that
uses an oscillating radio frequency [47]. Indeed, as Topp et al. remarked that the real part of the
complex dielectric constant is not strongly frequency dependent in the range of 1 MHz to 1 GHz, but
is highly sensitive to the volumetric water content, it is possible to characterise the water content by
sending electromagnetic waves through the soil and measuring the resultant signal related to the
wave which was send out [46, 47]. Knowing both the sent wave and the measured resultant, it is
then possible to link the dielectric permittivity with the volumetric water content. An important note
here is that the soil properties, such as density, salinity and texture will influence the propagating
electromagnetic wave as well, but to a much lower extend.
43
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
Indeed, as the radio wave propagates through the soil, the wave will get reflected in function of
the soil properties and the water/ice content, with the water/ice content dominating the extend
of influence on the wave as mentioned before as well. The wave properties that are influenced
significantly by the water content, are the frequency, amplitude, impedance and the time of travel
through the substance [47].
The use of an electromagnetic wave to measure the water content is only a practical way of measuring,
as water is a polar molecule, which implies that one part of the molecule carries a negative charge
while the other half of the molecule carries a positive charge [47]. On the other hand, most soil types
are non-polar, indicating that the charge on the molecules is better distributed and that there is not a
positive and negative side. Hence, the polarity of water causes a rotational dipole moment under the
influence of an electromagnetic wave, while soil is not affected significantly by the electromagnetic
wave. This means that water will rotate and reorientate with the variation of an oscillating electric
field due to the propagation of the electromagnetic wave emitted by the sensor, while soil remains
mostly unaffected. From 1 MHz to 1 GHz, the water rotational dipole moment will occur at the
same frequency of the electromagnetic wave. It is this rotational dipole moment of water that is
responsible for the high dielectric constant of water of about 80. Due to this particular difference in
the reaction on a propagating electromagnetic wave, large changes in the dielectric permittivity are
directly correlated to changes in soil moisture.
As already mentioned in this section, there are indeed some factors, such as the soil density and
salinity, that influence the measured dielectric permittivity. Another important influencing factor is
the temperature, as an increasing temperature causes an increasing vibration of the molecules in the
considered medium [47]. The vibrations of the molecules due to temperature effects are affecting
the rotational dipole moment of liquid water under the influence of a propagating electromagnetic
wave. Hence, especially the real dielectric permittivity decreases with increasing temperature.
However, the effect of temperature on the rotational dipole moment of water in soil is more complex,
due to for instance the bound water effects. In soils, there is indeed interaction between water and
soil particles, such that correcting the measured real dielectric permittivity is significantly dependent
on the soil type [47]. Temperature effects also the imaginary part of the dielectric permittivity,
similarly as temperature affects the bulk electrical conductivity.
There are two commonly used methods to quantify the difference between the emitted electromagnetic
wave and the sensed electromagnetic wave, based on one of the characteristics of waves. Firstly,
there is a method that uses the wave velocity which is linked to the time the wave needs to travel
from emitter to sensor. This method is called the Time Domain Reflectometry (TDR) [46]. Next,
the method which uses the change in frequency of the electromagnetic wave to quantify the dielectric
permittivity is called Frequency Domain Reflectometry (FDR) [47]. Both TDR and FDR only measure
the apparent permittivity εa, which is a combination of the real and imaginary part of the dielectric
permittivity and is expressed as [47]:
εa = εr
2 {1 + [1 + tan2 (εi
εr
)]
1/2
}
(2.56)
If only the apparent dielectric permittivity is measured, there are assumptions required regarding
the imaginary permittivity to obtain the real part [47]. By mixing the real and imaginary part into
one variable, the measurement is prone to error since other factors such as salinity, temperature,
conductivity and mineralogy, can independently influence the real and the imaginary permittivity.
Errors can occur when such variables are not independently characterized in measuring the real and
the imaginary permittivity. The “real dielectric” represents water alone. The “imaginary dielectric”
represents the other things that are not related to water.
To cope with this issue, the permittivity sensor HydraProbe from Stevens Water Monitoring Systems
Inc. uses a different method and is able to measure both the imaginary and real dielectric permittivity
44
==PAGE:70==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
separately, reducing the risks of error [47]. The method to determine the real dielectric permittivity
is called the Coaxial Impedance Dielectric Reflectometry and measures the ratio of the amplitudes of
the emitted and reflected waves, which allows the determination of the impedance of the measured
medium between the probe rods of the sensor [47]. For the latter method, the emitted radio wave
has a frequency of 50 MHz. As with this method the real part is known, the sensor also uses FDR to
determine the imaginary part from the apparent dielectric permittivity. More information about this
particular sensor is provided in Section 4.1.8.
As explained in this section, the real part of the dielectric permittivity εr is directly relatable with the
(unfrozen) volumetric water content. In the context of permafrost research, the measurement of
this bulk soil property allows the determination of the unfrozen water content in function of the soil
temperature, if εr is measured for a wide range of temperature, including sub-zero temperatures as
well. Due to the bonding of water molecules to soil molecules, the freezing point of of some parts of
the water is reduced significantly («-5°C) [45]. This causes the existence of a significant amount of
liquid water at sub-zero temperatures, where a volumetric water content of 10 % and more is not
uncommon [45].
The operation of the majority of sensors, including the Stevens HydraProbe, at lower temperatures
requires the following assumption as these sensors are not designed to operate in these temperatures.
Indeed, it is assumed that the measured permittivity in sub-zero temperatures is only linked to the
unfrozen water content in the soil [45].
The relation between the unfrozen water content and the measured real part of the dielectric
permittivity is only given through a wide variety of empirical relations, with calibrated parameters
based on tests on certain soil types. Equation 2.57 depicts an empirical equation for silty clay
permafrost deposits, which was proposed by Agergaard and Ingeman-Nielsen based on soil samples
from Kangerlussuaq [45]. In the experiments used for the calibration of the empirical expression, the
Stevens HydraProbe was utilised as well.
wvol = 0.165 ⋅√εr,TC −0.239
(2.57)
Note that in Equation 2.57 a corrected form for the real part of the dielectric permittivity is used.

[22] that some of the expressions linking the real permittivity and the volumetric water content
require a temperature correction as shown in the following equation:

water content with the real part of the dielectric permittivity:

2.5.4
Salinity
Originally, the salinity was defined in order to provide a measure of the actual mass of salt per unit
mass present in seawater [49]. This concept, where the salinity is based on the mass of salt, is
called the absolute salinity SA. Especially in the past, it proved to be a challenge to determine SA
accurately, due to the lack of a simple, easily reproducible measurement of the actual dry mass of
salts in seawater. However, due to the presence of some volatile salts in sea water that disappear
during the evaporation or heating process to obtain the dry mass [49], the determination of the mass
of salts proved to be not that accurate.
A first guideline for the determination of the absolute salinity dates from 1889, where a solution was
sought to solve the problem with volatile components in seawater. This method was based on the
replacement of bromines and iodines in the seawater with chlorides, such that a chlorinity of seawater
could be defined. Chlorinity is defined as the weight in grams in vacuum of the chlorides in 1 gram
of sea water, also in vacuum. The commission at that time, prescribed that this chlorinity could
be determine by using a volumetric titration with silver nitrate. A general expression that gives the
absolute salinity in function of the chlorinity is shown in Equation 2.64 [49]. The coefficients A and
B depend on the location and the time of the measurement.
46
==PAGE:72==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
SA = A + B ⋅Cl.
(2.64)
with Cl. being the chlorinity and A and B two coefficients depending on time and location.
In order to determine the chlorinity, based on the volumetric titration, one must know the atomic
weights of the involved substances [49]. As these atomic weights were changing together with the
increase of the accuracy of scientific measurements, it was specified that the determination of the
chlorinity based on titration should be based on tables provided of Copenhagen Normal Water as the
standard. In other words, this means that the chlorinity is defined in terms of atomic weights as
they were known in 1902. Not much later, a new method was proposed by Jacobsen and Knudsen
to set the salinity determination free of chlorinity values based on the store of normal water from
Copenhagen. They defined chlorinity as the mass of silver required to precipitate completely the
halogens in 0.3285234 kg of sample seawater. This was the last definition before the Practical
Salinity Scale 1978 was made the standard.
Another method in order to determine the salinity of seawater, is the measuring of both the electrical
conductivity and the temperature, which is a technique that has been used since 1930 [49]. In
the early stages of this technique, very accurate measurements were not that easy to obtain and
precision salinometers were only widely available once modern electronics got developed more and
more. In the period 1955-1959 the instruments used for the determination of the electric conductivity,
utilised a thermostatically controlled temperature bath which caused the procedure of measuring to
be quite cumbersome, but however with standard deviations of approximately 0.005. In the sixties of
that century, there were more compact and non-thermostatic devices developed, which made this
technique more accessible and more practical in use in the field. In general, all the instruments gave
conductivity ratios and temperatures which at that time were used to obtain the salinity based on
the results of Thomas, Thompson, and Utterback. However, very soon it was discovered that there
were unacceptable discrepancies between salinities based on conductivities and salinities based on the
chlorinity titration method.
This long evolution of the search for an internationally accepted, practical and accurate method to
obtain the salinity leaded eventually to a method which solved all the mentioned issues.
Indeed, Lewis and Perkin got assigned this task at the 1975 Joint Panel on Oceanographic Tables
and Standards meeting (JPOTS), to revise the definition of salinity in order to eliminate ambiguities
of greater magnitude than those associated either with the equations, or with instrumental error
[49]. The eventual result was the Practical Salinity Scale 1978 (PSS 78) which got accepted in 1980
for international and inter-institutional use [50], and it has been the internationally recommended
method to determine salinity since its introduction.
According to Lewis and Perkin [49], there is certainly no unique solution to the salinity problem.
However, any solution should comply with the following three criteria: the resulting salinity, based on
the definition (i) must be replicable in any major laboratory anywhere in the world, independently
of the ionic content of local waters (contrary to the idea of the Copenhagen Standard Water), (ii)
must be a conservative property, which means that the dilution of a given sample by an equal mass
of pure water should halve both the salinity as the chlorinity or conductivity, and (iii) must allow
density differences in any given water mass to be computed to acceptable limits.
As previous studies showed that a salinity scale based on a “conductivity ratio” performs better in
comparison to a “chlorinity” scale for density determination, the two authors decided to base the new
salinity scale on conductivity measurements instead. This decision carries the consequence that all
waters with an equal conductivity ratio have the same salinity as well.
The PSS 78 scale is founded on a standard seawater that has a practical salinity of 35 ‰by definition,
which corresponds with a conductivity ratio of unity at 15 ○C with a KCl solution containing a mass
of 32.4356 gKCl in a mass of 1 kg of solution. It has been remarked that the Merck “Suprapur” KCl
47
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 2. Literature Study
possesses an adequate purity for being consistent to serve as a standard. The proposed expression
for the practical salinity in the PSS 78 scale is as follows [49]:

that this definition is only valid for practical salinities in the interval [2,42], which implies that for
waters with very low salinities another approach is required.
Before the discussion is held how to deal with practical salinities lower than 2, it is first required to
consider the determination of practical salinities at variable temperatures, pressures and conductivities

It is clear that the practical salinity should be equal to zero, when the conductivity is equal to the
conductivity of pure water, which is practically equal to zero. Therefore, the proposed method will
converge to a salinity of 0 when the conductivity reaches zero as well. With this working method,

Chapter 3
Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in
Sisimiut
The Fieldwork took place from July 6th until July 8th, 2022. The team is shown in Figure 3.1 and
consisted of myself, and two very experienced and enthusiastic friends, Anton and Danielle. The
successfully performed tasks during this fieldwork, are discussed further in this section.
Figure 3.1: Team on site for the fieldwork.
3.1
Drone
The use of a drone provided very useful information and data about the larger area, which can also
result in some observations on a bigger scale. The reason why the results from the drone footage will
be of particular interest, is that on the 28th of August 2021 Nukissiorfiit already observed a settled
area around Mast 9 of roughly 40 meters long in the direction along the Fjord and 29,5 meters long
along the slope of the hill. Hence, during this field trip, the drone was also used to provide a better
view of how large the actual affected area around the mast really is. Many thanks to Marco Marcer
for providing us with the drone which is shown during action in Figure 3.2.
51
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
The drone footage consists of overlapping pictures of the surface and the mast, taken from several
heights and angles. The overlap and the vast number of pictures is required during the processing
in order to reduce the uncertainties. In this project, the pictures taken by the drone are processed
by using the Agisoft Metashape software [52]. This software can create Digital Elevation Models
(DEM), orthophotos and 3D models based on purely the drone footage. In this case, there are three
types of footage collected during the field work. Firstly, there are overlapping pictures made from an
aerial view of the area and this both above and below the electricity cables. Lastly, the mast itself
is also photographed from all possible angles below the electricity cables. There is drone footage
collected for both for Mast 9 and 8, however for this project only the footage for Mast 9 has been
processed and discussed in this report.
The resulting DEM, orthophoto and 3D model, based on the drone footage, proved to be useful in
assessing the hydrological situation on the surface, the type of vegetation, and the variation of the
vegetation over the surveyed area. Furthermore, the obtained DEM, with a relatively high resolution,
was extremely useful in the processing of the geoelectric surveying data.
Figure 3.2: The drone in action.
3.2
Electrical Resistivity Tomography
In order to map the thawed and frozen soil layers, four Electrical Resistivity Tomography (ERT)
measurements have been performed. The method and the theoretical background have already been
addressed in Section 2.5.1. It was opted to put the lines in such way that the different measured
ERT profiles overlap with each other, in order to allow the verification of the obtained resistivities.
The location of the ERT profiles is depicted on the map shown in Figure 3.27. The used electrodes
and clips are shown in Figure 3.3. We used two cables which enabled us to use eventually 41
52
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
electrodes. The device that carried out the measurements is the ABEM Terrameter LS 2 which uses
a user-friendly interface and enables the visualisation of the apparent resistivity of the underlying soil
layers on site.
There are several measurement protocols, i.e. several ways to send current through the soil and
which electrodes along the measuring line need to be combined to obtain the eventual resistivity of
the soil in function of depth. We opted to use the Wenner protocol, which considers four equidistant
electrodes in series at a time [53] as already explained in Section 2.5.1.
Figure 3.3: Detail of the type of clamps, electrodes and cable for the ERT measurements.
Regarding the performed ERT profiles, the following can be said. The first measured profile was
SIS2F-01 and has the lowest spacing of the electrodes of 0.75 m.
This gave a very detailed
measurement of the upper layer(s); however the depth was relatively small. This is why SIS2F-02
was performed on the same location as SIS2F-01 with an electrode spacing of 1 m instead. Next,
SIS2F-03 was measured along the slope of the hill to the East of Mast 9, with a same spacing of
1 m. This profile is overlapping with SIS2F-01 and SIS2F-02. Lastly, SIS2F-04 was performed at
least 15 m towards the West of Mast 9, far away from even the anchors, with the goal of measuring
’natural’ soil conditions where the influence of the mast is negligible. In the last case a spacing of
2 m is chosen such that the profile will give more certain resistivities at larger depths. Note that
the coordinates of the edges and the middle point of the four profiles are measured with GNSS
equipment.
The data obtained from the measurements are the apparent resistivity and have to be processed by
applying inversion techniques to obtain the actual resistivity in function of depth along the profile
which enables us to make conclusions about the soil conditions around the mast. This mathematical
inversion is performed with the Aarhus GeoSoftware [54] and the results are discussed in Section
3.7.4.
3.3
Thawed soil samples
By using copper tubes as shown in Figure 3.4, which are open at both ends but could be sealed with
plastic caps, it was possible to retrieve soil samples of the active layer as undisturbed as possible. It
is extremely complicated to obtain completely undisturbed soil samples, and the method used in this
case results in a relatively low amount of disturbance as the tubes are pushed gently into the ground.
53
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
So, the assumption that these soil samples are undisturbed is still a simplification. In anyway, these
soil samples provide some useful information of the upper part of the organic active layer.
In this case, soil samples have been taken at depths of approximately 15 cm below surface, every
time just above the soil that was still frozen at the time. First, a hole was dug after the removal of
the vegetation. Once the frozen soil was reached, the copper tube was placed gently and horizontally
in the unfrozen soil. Once the tube was pushed completely into the soil, the soil sample could be
retrieved. The coordinates of the location where each sample has been retrieved, are determined in
this case with a Garmin GPS, hence the accuracy is much lower compared to GNSS coordinates.
These samples were processed to some extent in the lab in Sisimiut. The water content and the bulk
modulus have been determined. Furthermore, the colour of the soil was determined as well by using
the “Revised standard soil color chart" by Oyama and Takehara (1967) according to the Munsell
system [55]. Note that from each sample two subsamples have been taken: the A subsample was
dried and used to determine the bulk density and water content, while the B subsample was used for
the colour determination. All subsamples are stored separately in a closed plastic bag. The limited
number of investigations on these samples was due to time restrictions.
Figure 3.4: Material used for the retrieval of the thawed soil samples on site.
3.4
Drilling
As in the area there have not been any frozen samples collected and investigated yet, a Stihl hand
drill and some core barrels have been brought to site to attempt the collection of frozen soil samples.
There were two types of core barrels: diamond core barrels with a non-removable cap and a core
barrel with larger teeth at the cutting front and a removable cab. From both types we brought a
smaller and a larger diameter core barrel. In the end, the removable cap showed to be very practical
as when the soil sample got stuck inside the barrel, it was possible to push it gently out. The used
equipment is shown in Figure 3.5.
We managed to extract soil samples from two boreholes: one of the boreholes was close to the mast
and has a depth of approximately 2 m relative to the surface level. The other borehole is located at
20 m towards the West of the mast, even outside the area with the anchors. For this borehole, we
54
==PAGE:80==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
reached a depth of 0.8 m relative to the surface level and it is located along SIS2F-04. Especially the
latter borehole showed extremely ice rich samples, and this from almost directly from the surface.
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 3.5: (a) selection of core barrels used during the fieldwork, (b) the set-up to power the freezer,
(c) Stihl hand drill with the diamond core barrel and (d) drilling of SIS2022-03.
All the soil samples have been labelled and stored immediately in a portable freezer at – 8 ○C.
However, during the transport from the Second Fjord to Sisimiut, the temperature of the freezer was
set to – 18 ○C to ensure that there was more time to resolve eventual problems with for instance
the power supply. Luckily, no major issues occurred, and the soil samples survived the transport to
Sisimiut by boat. During the whole time, the freezer was connected to portable batteries as shown in
Figure 3.5 (b). There were two lithium batteries on site, and they were charged in turns by a Honda
55
==PAGE:81==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
generator. This set-up proved to be reliable and was operating perfectly. It was created at a very
short notice with the help of the Toyota service in Sisimiut, for which I am extremely grateful as it is
only thanks to them that we could bring frozen samples back to Sisimiut and in the end to Denmark.
All the soil samples have been photographed in situ with the depth and other information present
on the picture. Furthermore, we kept track of the actual depth of the borehole independent of the
measured length of the retrieved soil samples. Pictures of the soil samples in function of the depth
can also be found in the Appendix A.5.
3.5
Active layer probing
In order to get a detailed grid of the active layer around the mast, an area of 10 x 10 m² with the
mast in the centre is set out. On the northern part of the area, there are measurements every 0.5 m,
while on the southern part of the area there are measurements every 1 m. It was opted to cover
the area around the mast as good as possible to map the change in the active layer thickness quite
accurately. On site we observed indeed that the soil close to the mast looked ’mushy’ and thawed
and further away (couple meters) the change was significant, as the soil got firmer, less saturated
and the active layer thickness decreased as well.
To perform the probing of the active layer, a T-shaped steel bar with a length of approximately 1.2
m was used. A measurement of the active layer at a certain location consisted of pushing the steel
bar in the soil, until a hard layer, i.e. the frozen soil, was reached. The determination of the actual
depth of the active layer was facilitated by the notches on the bar.
3.6
Temperature sensors
The last task that was carried out during the field work, is the installation of ten HOBO temperature
loggers with each logger having two sensor channels. The goal to install the temperature sensors is
to monitor the mast, air and soil temperatures. Both the logger and the two temperature sensors
are shown in Figure 3.7 (a). All the loggers record the temperature every hour and it was double
checked that all the loggers have enough battery and memory to operate for at least four years. The
as built locations of the eight soil temperature loggers around the mast are shown in Figure 3.8.
Note that the distances are measured from the edge of the foundation column, and hence not from
the centre of the mast.
One of the ten loggers is placed on the mast to record the mast and air temperature and another
logger is installed close to borehole SIS2022-04, relatively far away from the mast and anchors. Just
before leaving the site, temperature data was downloaded from the previous day of all loggers, except
for the logger which was placed close to borehole SIS2022-04.
56
==PAGE:82==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
Figure 3.6: Ongoing probing at the western side of Mast 9.
The logger that was placed on the mast is indeed recording both the mast and the air temperature,
and the two sensors are both placed on the same side of the mast. The air temperature sensor is
placed in a cylindrical, white container and is placed approximately 2 m above the surface. It was
however not straightforward to determine the position of the sensor on the mast relative to the
surface, as the surface is sloping. The mast sensor has been placed 30 cm below the upper edge of
the foundation column. The final configuration on the mast is shown in Figure 3.12 (a).
In order to push and fixate the sensor onto the mast, a wooden squared desk has been carved out
slightly such that the cable and the sensor fitted better. Then, it was glued onto the mast by using
anchor fixating silicone. The cables are put in a larger, flexible tube to protect them from the
elements and wildlife. The logger is put inside the truss construction and both the logger, and the
air temperature sensor are also placed on a wooden piece and fixed to the mast with anchor fixating
silicone and zip ties.

The selection of the outer black tube, where the sensor cables are placed into, was quite a challenge.
Indeed, it is important to have an outer tube which is stiff enough to be pushed into the soil and
which has a rather small diameter as we were limited with a long drilling bit of 10 mm in diameter.
However, the metal sensor head, as shown in Figure 3.7, is also 7 mm in diameter, which made the
options regarding the outer tube diameter very limited. Eventually, the Toyota service in Sisimiut
came in this case again to the rescue and provided us with the perfect solution: we could use some
PVC tubes that can be straightened out by heating them up as the tubes are stored on a roll. Figure
3.11 (a) shows how the cables were cut to a length of approximately 1.5 m and were straightened by
heating them up and clamping them to a wooden desk. It was required to take slightly longer tubes,
as due to the clamps the tubes were smashed.
As the sensor cables are not exactly 2 m long and vary in length, the following strategy was applied
to determine the eventual depth of the sensor. First, the tubes where the sensor cables will be
60
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
inserted into, have been duct taped together. Next, the ends have been sealed with the waterproof
anchoring silicone as shown in Figure 3.12 (b). Once the silicone was hardened, the sensor cables are
put in the tubes and are pushed until the very end of the tube. Then, the location on the sensor
cable where the sensor cable exits the black PVC tube is indicated with some duct tape. Next, the
sensor cable is pulled out of the tube and the part of the sensor cable that is in the tube is measured.
Consequently, when the sensor cable is put back into the tube and the tube us installed into the soil,
then by measuring the stick up of the tube, it is possible to determine the eventual depth of the
sensor as the cables are fixed with both silicone and duct tape into the tube. In other words, the
effective length of the cable per sensor is measured and that length is put equivalent to the actual
depth of the considered sensor.
As both the cables and the tubes are fixed, the cables are not moving in the tube and the distance
between the bottom and top sensor is fixed per logger. The latter is important in calculations of the
thermal gradient, as the actual depth of each sensor in function of time cannot be assumed constant
due to frost heave.
The final as built depth is now determined by subtracting the measured stick up from the measured
length of the sensor cable in the tube. Note that the cables are fixed onto the top of the tubes with
a different type of silicone than the silicon used for the plug at the bottom of the tubes.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.12: Picture (a) shows how the mast and air temperature sensor are mounted onto the mast
and (b) shows how the end of the tube is sealed.
In general (also based on the probing), we had an active layer thickness varying from 10 cm to 60
cm. So, this means that the top sensor will be already placed in the active layer or will be soon in
the active layer, while the bottom sensor is in general placed into the frozen ground and will remain
frozen for a much longer time than the other sensor. Finally, all temperature sensors around the mast
were installed and operational on July 7 2022, at 4.10 pm West-Greenlandic time. Sensor number 9
was installed the next day, close to borehole SIS2022-04.

3.7
Results
3.7.1
Active layer probing
The active layer has been mapped based on the performed probing. In total, there have been 380
data points recorded during the fieldwork in a relatively dense mesh around the mast. The probed
area has Mast 9 in its centre and has an area of 10 x 10 m2. Mast 9 is represented in the resulting
chart in Figure 3.13 as a blue dot.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.13: Visualisation of the probed active layer thicknesses around Mast 9: (a) map around
Mast 9 with orthophoto and with the probed area indicated, and (b) the active layer thickness in the
probed area. The unit of the colour scale indicating the active layer depth is cm.
In a first instance, there has been a measurement with a distance between data points of 0.5 m
in both directions. Afterwards, due to time limitations, there has been a probing every 1 m in
62
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
both directions. More specific, towards the north of the mast, there are every 0.5 m measurements
and towards the south there are every 1 m measurements. In general, there is still a quite densely
measured grid obtained around the mast.
The results are represented in plot as shown in Figure 3.13 (b). The mast location is represented by
a blue dot and the active layer thickness is smaller towards the east which corresponds approximately
with the uphill part of the area relative to the mast. This difference in active layer could be caused
by the downhill water flow which can influence the thermal regime and equilibrium significantly and
which might also be able to transports the heat in the soil originating from the mast.
At the location where the active layer is indicated to be equal to 120 cm, there was a quite large
surface rock present. Around this rock, the probing stick was actually not even long enough, so the
active layer is at least 120 cm.
At this point, it is certainly not sure what the cause is in the difference of active layer thickness
around the mast. However, as at a further distance from the mast and the anchors the active layer
was very thin, in the order of magnitude of 20 cm, it is certain that the construction and the mast
itself have an influence in some way in the active layer thickness as encountered during the field trip.
Note that the active layer thickness directly next to the mast was also different for the four sides of
the foundation column. At the downhill side of the mast foundation column, the active layer was
more than the length of the probing stick, while on the other sides it was not more than 1 m.
3.7.2
Drone footage
In this section, the results are shown after processing the drone footage from Mast 9. Firstly, the
orthophoto is discussed, where after the Digital Elevation Model (DEM) and the 3D model of the
area are considered into further detail. All the shown results are obtained with the Agisoft Metashape
software package which showed to be a powerful tool during the drone footage processing [52].
Figure 3.14: Estimated area affected by the construction and the presence of the mast based on the
orthophoto.
63
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
Orthophoto
The orthophoto has been assembled based on the available drone pictures and has a pixel size of 1.7
x 1.7 cm2. An example of the level of detail of the orthophoto is shown in Figure 3.14. Indeed, the
orthophoto provides a much better accuracy compared to the available Google satellite images as
shown in Figure 3.15. The extra details can be useful to assess for instance the vegetation variations
and the surface water flows. It also gives a clear view of at which locations the ERT profiles are
taken and where the boreholes were drilled.
Regarding the vegetation variation, Figure 3.15 shows for instance a much greener area around
Mast 9 and shows that the road constructed during the construction of the power line itself is still
clearly visible in the landscape. The fact that the vegetation is clearly much greener around the
mast compared to other locations, might be explained by the extra heat the mast conducts into
the surrounding soil and by an altered hydrological regime. Especially knowing that the snow has
been only melted for two weeks at the moment that the footage was been made, it is clear that the
vegetation close to the mast has in some way an advantage compared to the vegetation further away
from the mast.
More specific regarding the change in vegetation, it is clear that around the mast mainly willows, shrubs
and graminoids were encountered, and clearly much less mosses compared to other locations further
away from the mast, for instance around borehole SIS2022-04. Based on the detailed description of
how vegetation can be linked with the hydrological regime and the active layer thickness in Section
2.3, it is clear that the observed flora indicate wetter and larger active layers. Especially mosses are
encountered in areas with relatively thin active layers.
This observation confirms the change in thermal and hydrological regime around the mast and taking
the growth rate of flora in the Arctic into account, the changes are not from the last 3 years, as
already relatively large bushes were observed around the mast.
Figure 3.15: Estimated area affected by the mast based on the orthophoto.
The affected area has been estimated to be 48 x 48 m2 as shown in Figure 3.14 and these dimensions
64
==PAGE:90==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
are similar, but also slightly larger compared to the estimations of Nukissiorfiit during their field trip
in 2021.
Digital Elevation Model
Agisoft Metashape can calculate elevations based on drone pictures. The depth and elevations are
estimated by considering the relative position of overlapping parts of the pictures and shadows. The
algorithm proved to be efficient, as it is able to provide a Digital Elevation Model (DEM) with a
pixel size of 1.7 x 1.7 cm2. This high accuracy enables us to try to estimate the affected area around
the mast by looking at some settlements and discontinuities in contour lines of the area.
Figure 3.16 shows the DEM of the greater are around the mast. At first sight, the slope looks quite
gradual, as it was observed during the field trip as well and is towards the Fjord. Some of the streams
and the road are also visible on the DEM.
If now a closeup is considered from the area around the mast as shown in Figure 3.17, it is clear
that there is a kink visible in the uphill contour line above the mast. As the other contour lines are
all similar and quite smooth without sudden changes, it is assumed that this kink in the contour
line is caused by settlements due to the presence of the mast and the construction. It is however
impossible to determine precisely to what extent the mast itself or the effects of the construction
contributed to the change in the slope.
The dimensions of the affected area based on the DEM are smaller than the area based on the
orthophoto. This might be due to the accuracy limitations of the DEM and the presence of vegetation
which might influence the elevation calculations. In any way, the direct area around the mast is
certainly altered.
Lastly, based on the DEM, the slope towards the Fjord has been calculated to be in a range of slopes
of 1:10 to 3:20.

3.7.3
Soil temperature data
During the field trip, there has been already data retrieved from the air and mast temperature logger
and the eight soil temperature loggers directly around Mast 9. In order to have a sufficient amount
of temperature data for the thermal modelling, Marco Marcer went out to the mast on the 31st of
March 2023 to collect the recorded data of all ten temperature loggers. The whole data set is shown
in Section A.2 in the Appendix and the soil temperatures around the mast on the 8th of July 2022
are shown in Figure 3.20 (a).
Per soil temperature logging location, there are two temperatures recorded: a top and bottom
temperature. At the moment of installation, the actual depth is known of both the top and bottom
sensor, as there have no significant heave or other movements occurred. As time passes, the actual
depths of the sensors will become unknown. In Figure 3.20, negative x values indicate measurements
from sensors which are placed parallel with the Fjord, while positive x values indicate measurements
in the downhill direction. Note that in the graph in Figure 3.20 (a), at each data point the measured
depth of the sensor when built is indicated in meters.
Considering the way of interpretation for Figure 3.20, the following conclusions can be drawn. Firstly,
it is clear that in the downhill direction, the topsoil temperatures are significantly higher compared to
the direction parallel to the Fjord. Furthermore, the temperature closest to the mast is much higher
compared to the direction parallel to the Fjord as well. This observation is certainly in line with what
has been said regarding the variation of the active layer thickness in Section 3.7.1.
Another observation is that for the three bottom soil temperature sensors, furthest from the mast
and in both directions, the soil is still frozen. For the topsoil temperature sensors, the recorded
temperatures are all above 0 ○C. This confirms our aim, that at the start of the measurements the
deeper sensor should be in frozen soil, while the shallow sensor should be already in the active layer.
67
==PAGE:93==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.20: (a) Soil temperatures for both the deep and shallow sensor around Mast 9 on July 8th,
2022 at 00:10:00. Note that the depth of each sensor is mentioned at the corresponding data point
in the graph in m; (b) Soil temperature gradient on the same day.
As the effect of ambient conditions on the soil temperature is more significant closer to the surface,
it is worth considering the depth effect on the presented soil temperatures in Figure 3.20 (a). Indeed,
the highest recorded temperature is also the shallowest temperature such that the thermal gradient
for all logging locations is calculated for the same date as shown in Figure 3.20 (b). It is clear that
there is still a significant difference between the soil temperature gradient downhill and parallel to
the Fjord, such that our earlier conclusion is confirmed that the soil temperatures are systematically
higher downhill compared to parallel with the Fjord. If furthermore the gradient is considered 12
hours later than the previous thermal gradient, it is also clear that there is much more variation in
the downhill soil temperatures compared to the soil temperatures parallel to the Fjord.
As our deepest sensor is only 1.16 m below surface, it is of course very difficult to make conclusion
68
==PAGE:94==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
regarding the thermal state of deeper soil layers. Later, based on the combination of temperature
data and the resistivity profiles discussed in Section 3.7.4, it is possible to extend our depth of
investigation.
Air and Mast temperature
Figure 3.21 (a) and (b) show the relation between respectively the daily air temperature and the daily
mast temperature, and the derivative of the air and mast temperature. Figure 3.21 (a) indicates
that a linear trend line describes the relation sufficiently accurately. However, a parabolic relationship
provides an even better fit, as in fact the mast temperature seems to be warmer compared to the air
temperature for temperature lower than -10 °C. In the temperature range [-10, 5] the air proves to be
warmer compared to the mast temperature, while in temperatures above 5 °C the mast temperature
is warmer again. In the latter case, the solar radiation might cause this effect. Further in this section,
there will be more attention payed to identify one of the causes for this slight temperature difference.
However, it is still valid to state that on a daily scale, the mast and air temperature follow roughly
the same temperature variation.
The general spreading of the data points around the identity line can also be due to the effect of the
direct solar radiation: On sunny days in the period where the sun reaches above the horizon for a
long enough time, the mast temperature is more likely to be higher than the air temperature. On
all the other days, when it is not that sunny, the mast temperature is lower compared to the air
temperature.
The daily change of the considered two temperatures is depicted in Figure 3.21 (b). In general, it
can be said that the change in mast temperature changes slower compared to the change in air
temperature, although the difference is very small. This slight difference is however as expected, as
there is a delay in the transfer of heat from the mast to the air and vice versa.
Figure 3.21 (c) and (d) show the hourly air and mast temperature measured during the first 16 days
in August 2022 and January 2023 respectively. These graphs are useful to investigate the effect of
direct solar radiation. In summer, a significant amount of direct sunlight and higher temperatures are
very often encountered in the Sisimiut area. The extract from the data confirms this and shows that
on warm days the mast temperature is generally higher than the air temperature. This increased
mast temperature is certainly not beneficial for the stability of the thermal regime of the surrounding
soil layers.
In January, the total daylight lasts only for approximately two - three hours, such that the effect of
the sun can be ruled out. Indeed, the mast temperature is practically equal to, and often lower than
the air temperature. These two graphs certainly confirm the significant effect of direct sunlight on
the mast on the mast temperature.
To investigate which other atmospheric parameter than the air temperature has a significant effect
on the mast temperature, the correlation and relation between the solar radiation, wind velocity
and mast temperature is considered into further detail. As there are only direct measurements
available of the air and mast temperature, the other two atmospheric variables are obtained from a
fifth-generation reanalysis data set (ERA5) produced by the European Centre for Medium-Range
Weather Forecasts (ECMWF), scaled to our point of interest, i.e. Mast 9. More information about
ERA5 and the data acquisition is given in Section 5.1.1.

between the considered atmospheric variables. It is opted to use the difference in air and mast
temperature as one variable, with a positive difference indicating that the mast temperature exceeds
the air temperature. If first the effect of the wind on the mast temperature is considered, as shown in
Figure 3.22 (a) and (b), it is clear that this effect cannot be proven by the available hourly data. In
other words, the data shows that the wind does not influence the variation of the mast temperature
significantly. It is important to note, that the mast temperature has been measured approximately 0.5
m above the surface, which might also explain why the mast temperature does not show a significant
effect by the wind. Figure 3.22 (b) shows however that there are some data points indicating that
very heavy wind velocities can influence the mast temperature in summer. This is nicely shown by
the difference of the summer and winter trend line when daily averages are considered only.

Next, the effect of the solar radiation on the mast temperature can be called more significant based
on the plots in Figure 3.22 (c) and (d). The hourly data of both winter and summer combined
show a similar linear relation compared to the linear regression line of only the daily summer data
points. The winter trend line does not show a realistic relation between the air and mast temperature
difference and the solar radiation. This is clearly due to the lack of direct sunlight in the considered
winter months. In conclusion, it is certain that the solar radiation influences the mast temperature
significantly. The degree of influence of the air temperature, solar radiation and wind velocity on
the mast temperature can also be assessed by considering the correlation coefficient as elaborated
next. Furthermore, this coefficient allows a statistically founded quantification of the relation of for
instance the temperature and the solar radiation.
The correlation coefficient is a statistical unit of measurement to assess to what extent two variables
have a linear relationship, and this based on the covariance between the two considered variables [56].
In this case, the bivariate Pearson correlation coefficient ρXY is applied to assess the correlation
between two variables X and Y , which can be expressed as follows [56]:
ρXY =

with n being the amount of data points of each variable, ¯x and ¯y the sample mean of respectively
variable X and Y , and sX and sY the sample standard deviation of respectively variable X and Y .
rhoXY does not calculate the distance of the data points in a X-Y space to the identity line, but
calculates to what extent the two considered variables vary together by considering the deviation
from each data point with respect to its mean value. This is also clearly visible in the definition of
the covariance:
Cov(X,Y ) = ∑n

based on hourly data to ensure the correlation is investigated on a smaller scale, with less averaging
compared to the daily data.

Next, the correlation between the solar radiation and the wind is considered. As expected, these two

the correlation between air temperature and solar radiation. This indicates that not only the air
temperature influences the mast temperature, but also the incoming solar radiation onto the mast.
Regarding the correlation between wind velocities and the mast temperature, the same remark can

the soil, are the air temperature and the solar radiation.
Soil temperatures
Figure 3.23 summarises the collected soil temperature data, for both the top and bottom sensor per
logger location. A map of the area is attached as well to facilitate the interpretation of the graphs.
The temperature data of Logger 9, which is located approximately 20 m to the West relative to
the mast, is included in each graph as it is intended to consider these temperatures as it occurs in
natural soil, where the influence of the mast is non-existing. The location of Logger 9 has been
carefully selected, such that there is no surface water flow or other water flow visibly present.
A first observation is that the effect of the mast is clearly detectable compared to the temperatures
at Logger 9, i.e. the ’natural’ state: in the summer the surrounding soil is heated up significantly,
while in winter especially the loggers closest to the mast show a cooling effect by the presence of the
mast. The data suggest that the heating effect penetrates the soil with a larger radius compared to
the cooling effect, which might be due to the heat transport facilitated by water flows during the
summer months.
More specifically the effect can be quantified by comparing the reached minima and maxima of the
soil sensors close to the mast with the minimum and maximum of the soil temperatures of Logger
9. These calculations are summarised concisely in Table 3.2. It is clear that for the maxima of the
topsoil temperatures, the difference decreases with distance. For the minima this is not the same, as
the cooling effect of the mast has not the same radius of effect compared to the heating effect. For
the bottom soil temperatures, there is a difference for the direction downhill and parallel to the Fjord.
Parallel to the fjord the influence decreases with increasing distance, while for the downhill direction
the depth of some of the sensors differ too much as will be discussed later. In general, all sensors in
the radius of 5 m around the mast record a clear and undoubted influence by the mast.
Furthermore, every sensor recorded a plateau in the curve starting at the beginning of the winter,
i.e. starting around October - November. This isothermal plateau is a known phenomenon, and it
occurs when soil temperatures are considered during seasonal freeze and thaw due to the release of
excess latent heat originating from the phase change of moisture [57]. This isothermal period in
soil temperature curves is called the zero-curtain, as the temperature during the phase change of
moisture is by definition at 0 ○C.

The zero-curtain is able to provide some interesting information about the soil, as it is strongly
related to the presence of moisture. Indeed, in order to maintain a soil temperature of 0○C for a
period of a couple of days and longer, moisture needs to be present. Another requirement for the
formation of a zero-curtain, is that the thermal gradient must be equal to 0 ○C just above the base
of the active layer [57]. The presence of moisture and the timing of the zero-curtain also provide
information about the possible presence of buried ice-rich permafrost [57]. During the thawing season,
there exists generally speaking a high thermal gradient between the warming surface and the cold
permafrost such that heat is effectively conducted through the soil without forming a zero curtain.
During the freezing season, the cold ground surface and the cold permafrost create a low thermal
gradient, ensuring a condition at a certain depth in the soil where the temperature is at or near 0
○C for a period of some days and longer. These relatively high (vertical) thermal gradients during
the summer months and the relatively low (vertical) thermal gradients during the winter months are
also observed in our data as depicted in Figure 3.24. In conclusion, seasonally frozen ground with
no permafrost in the sub-surface may display a zero-curtain during the thawing season, while soil
with permafrost present in the sub-surface may display a zero curtain only during the freezing season
[57]. For future research, when a temperature data set is available of several years, this will be an
interesting aspect to consider.
Regarding the duration of the zero-curtain, the following can be said. Several observations show
that the duration of the zero-curtain can be used to obtain information of the active layer thickness:
the zero-curtain generally tends to get longer with increasing active layer thickness, whereas thinner
active layers show a relatively short zero-curtain [57]. The zero-curtain is of course not only influenced
by the soil conditions, as the atmospheric variables have a significant impact as well. Therefore,
the zero-curtain can also be used to map the eventual changes of the climate in function of time
and/or location and to identify extreme weather conditions. The starting date of the zero-curtain
can also be used to identify objectively the start of the annual thawing or freezing period. If data
over several years is visualised, potential variations in this can be considered as well in the monitoring
or assessment of the area.
Our data lacks the complete thawing period, but the freezing period is well documented. If the
natural situation in the upper soil layer is considered, i.e. data from the top sensor of Logger 9, the
zero curtain starts on October 8th, 2022 and last until December 3rd, 2022, which equals to a period
of 57 days. Such a long zero-curtain indicates that there must be a low thermal gradient as well
for a sufficient amount of time such that permafrost might exist in the sub-surface. The longer the
zero-curtain, the higher the probability for a thicker active layer. A more certain conclusion can only
be made with soil temperature data of several years [57]. Note that there is no zero curtain at the
bottom sensor for Logger 9, such that at this location the thickness of the active layer is smaller
than 1.1 m.
The comparison of the natural state with the other data shows the probable effect of the mast, the
change in water content and the difference in depths where the temperatures are measured. If the
temperature data from the two top sensors closest to the mast, i.e. from the top sensors of Loggers 1
and 5, are considered, the zero-curtain is in both cases shorter. For the other top sensors of Loggers
2,3 and 4 the same is true, but for the top sensors of Loggers 7 and 8 the zero-curtain is much longer
compared to the natural situation. This is due to an increased moisture content at those locations,
as during the installation of the sensors surface water flows and very wet, marshy areas have been
observed. However, it is also possible that this longer zero-curtain indicates a thicker active layer as
discussed before in this section.
In contrast to the absence of a zero-curtain at the bottom sensor of Logger 9, there is clearly a
zero-curtain present for all the other bottom sensors. The zero-curtain is the shortest for Logger 1,
while the zero-curtain of the other loggers depend mainly on the locally present moisture and the
cooling effect of the mast. This cooling effect shows to be dependent on the direction with respect
75
==PAGE:101==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
to the mast: sensors closest to the mast and downhill show a more significant cooling effect, while
the closest sensors to the mast, parallel to the Fjord do not really show this effect. The latter sensors
indicate that the soil shows a delayed cooling probably due to the heating of the mast from the
summer months.
A last note regarding the zero-curtain, is that it can also be used to assess the accuracy of the used
temperature sensors. As by definition the isothermal plateau should be located at a temperature
of 0○C, the deviations of the mean temperature of the zero-curtains in our measurements from 0
○C are a good indication of the precision of the temperature sensors. Table 3.3 gives a summary
of the occurring deviations, and it can be concluded that for the required accuracy in this project
these deviations are negligible. In other words, the measured temperature data can be treated as a
data set with a relatively low uncertainty, with the only exception on the low uncertainty of the data
being the unknown absolute depths of the sensors as a function of time.
