One drawback regarding the current sensor set-up, is that indeed both the absolute depths of the
sensors are unknown in time and that the depth at the time of the installation of the sensors differ
from logger to logger. This causes the difficulty for drawing conclusions on the degree of influence
on the temperatures by the mast, as the variation of the temperatures in function of the depth
might overrule the actual influence of the mast on the considered location. In order to investigate
how much the mast or the depth of the sensor influences the measured temperature, the vertical
and horizontal thermal gradient are considered as shown in Figure 3.24. The horizontal gradient
is computed for both the top and the bottom sensor, and as the distance between the loggers is
assumed to be constant in time, this gradient can be calculated over the whole period of the data
set with the distance as measured at the installation of the loggers. A similar reasoning is valid for
the vertical thermal gradient, which is computed per logger and can be computed as the spacing
between the top and bottom sensor is constant in time thanks to the design of the sensor set-up.
Figure 3.24 (a) for instance shows the relation between the horizontal and vertical thermal gradient
of Logger 1. It is clear that in this case the horizontal gradient is overruling the vertical gradient,
as many data points are located above the identity line. This is in strong contrast with logger 3,
where the main influence of the temperature variation is due to the vertical thermal gradient. A
similar situation is observed for the temperature loggers parallel to the Fjord, although with a smaller
horizontal thermal gradient for logger 5, which is in line with the observation that the downhill soil is
more affected by the mast. In order to assess the horizontal and vertical thermal gradient for all
sensors, the percentage of the amount of data points situated above the identity line is given per
sensor as shown in Table 3.4. The calculations show indeed that the two closest top sensors, in
both directions have the largest influence by the mast. Furthermore, at the top sensor of logger 8, it
76
==PAGE:102==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
seems that there is a significant amount of data points where the horizontal gradient is larger than
the vertical. This is probably due to fact that the distance between logger 7 and 8 is the largest, and
equal to approximately 3 m. As there is a surface stream at logger 8, this might influence the soil
temperature as well. If the resulting percentages in Table 3.4 for logger 4 are considered, nothing
significant seems to be going on. However, the graph in Figure 3.23 (d) shows that the bottom
temperatures at Logger 4 are larger compared to the bottom temperatures at Loggers 2 and 3. This
is simply due to the depth of the bottom sensor of Logger 4, which is shallower compared to the
depths of the Loggers 2 and 3. The difference in depth is also an answer to the fact that the maximal
bottom temperature of Logger 3 is higher than Logger 2.
In general, for all the sensors, the vertical gradient reaches larger values than the horizontal. For the
bottom sensors, the horizontal thermal gradient is less significant compared to the top sensors, but
the sensors closest to the mast experience an influence by the horizontal thermal gradient to a larger
extent compared to the two sensors furthest away and this in both directions.

The effect of the mast on the surrounding soil temperature can also be investigated by plotting the
soil temperatures in function of the measured air temperature. In that case, an increased linear
relationship between air and soil temperature indicates a larger influence of the mast on the soil
temperature. This is clearly shown by considering the temperatures of Logger 1 and 9 as shown in
Figure 3.25. The topsoil temperatures of Logger 1 in function of the air temperature show an almost
exact linear relation in the summer months when there is no snow present. This indicates that during
the summer, heat is transferred into the soil both vertically through the vegetation, organic- and soil
layers, and through the mast. During the winter months, with snow, it is clear that the insulation
properties of snow will keep the soil warmer and will not allow a significant heat transfer through
the natural layers. However, the mast will cool the soil down such that the soil temperatures are
slightly lower compared to the natural situation. The deeper soil temperature sensor does not show
any significant linear relationship, indicating that the mast and air temperature do not affect the soil
temperature at those depths significantly.
In conclusion, this representation of the data confirms that mainly the upper soil temperatures are
affected by the mast and that the at 20 m from the mast no influence is recorded anymore.

Soil temperature trumpets
As there are only soil temperatures known at two depths, and this with a maximal depth of 1.16 m,
the trumpets are certainly not representing the complete thermal regime at the location. Furthermore,
the available data does not cover an entire year, such that it is possible that the actual minimum and
maximum does not occur yet. However, based on the DMI data and the measured air temperature, it
seems that the actual minimum and maximum temperatures of the period 2022-2023 are covered by
the available data, such that the presented trumpets in Figure 3.26 can be considered as representative
for the considered thawing - freezing season.
Soil temperature trumpets are a comprehensive representation of seasonal ground temperature
variations in function of depth by plotting the reached minimum and maximum at the considered
79
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
depth for a certain period of time. This representation of temperature data provides some useful
information, such as changes in physical properties and parameters typically used in engineering [58].
The main influencing factors for the character and shape of trumpets are the seasonal amplitude of air
and surface temperature, snow thickness and the thermal properties of the snow. The combination
of all these variables influences the shape of trumpets heavily. For instance, the trumpet’s width
decreases with increasing snowpack and as the snow depth varies spatially due to snow drifts and
other wind effects, the trumpets can vary heavily as well.
One of the characteristics of a trumpet that provide useful information, is the Depth of Zero
Annual Amplitude (DZAA) of seasonal variations in the ground [58], which is the depth where no
temperature variation is recorded in function of time. In other words, at that depth the thermal
regime is constant and does not get influenced by the seasonal variations of the ambient conditions,
nor by the geothermal gradient. The trumpets in Figure 3.26 indicate that the DZAA is certainly
larger than 1.16 m, as at all depths there are some variations, even at the location of Logger 9. If
however the bottom temperatures of Loggers 2 and 7 are considered, it is clear that the annual
variation at that depth is limited, and equal to only 1.6 ○C and 1.1 ○C respectively. In other words,
the DZAA is likely not much deeper than 1.12 m and 1.16 m respectively. The comparison of the
trumpet of Logger 9 and Logger 3 in Figure 3.26 (a) illustrates again that the heating effect of the
mast is more significant than the cooling effect for sensors further away from the mast. The reason
that Logger 4 has a wide trumpet, might be due to both the shallower depth and perhaps a thinner
snow cover compared to Logger 3. A similar comment regarding the variation in snow can be made
for the difference in width of the trumpet of Loggers 8 and 6, especially when compared with Logger
9.
(a) Downhill
(b) Parallel to Fjord
Figure 3.26: Temperature trumpets for all soil temperature loggers.
In general, the same observation as discussed before can be made as well based on the trumpets.
Indeed, the downhill topsoil temperatures undergo a larger seasonal variation compared to the soil
temperatures measured parallel to the Fjord. The soil temperatures measured closer to the mast
indicate larger seasonal variations and the effect of the mast at the bottom sensors seems to be less
significant, considering the amplitude of variation recorded by Logger 9 at its bottom sensor.

soil samples have been collected very close to the ERT profiles to facilitate verification by this means
as well. Some information for the four ERT profiles is summarised in Table 3.5. There have been
several distances used between the electrodes as well, such that there are different accuracies and
depths covered by the profiles. Indeed, with a decreasing spacing between the electrodes, the reached
depth with the measurements decreases, but the accuracy of the measurement increases.

Grounding resistance
In a first instance the histograms of the grounding resistance for the four profiles are computed based
on code provided by Thomas Ingeman-Nielsen. Grounding resistances are the resistance, with the
unit Ω, that a current sent through an electrode experiences when entering the soil directly at the
electrode - soil interface [59]. The resulting charts are shown in Figure 3.28 and the values of the
resistances suggest that a significant amount of the electrodes measured high resistance values, i.e
in the range of ⋅103 −105 Ω. These high resistances indicate the presence of frozen soil, while the
resistance values in the range of ⋅101 −103 Ωindicate thawed soil [59]. The presence of frozen soil at
the surface, was confirmed during the fieldwork as well, as some of the electrodes had to be drilled
into the soil due to the presence of surface ice and frozen soil directly at the surface. The lower
resistances corresponding to thawed soil and water are also reasonable for the profiles closest to the
mast, as the active layer was already quite extensive around the mast. In SIS2F-04 there are no
measurements of resistance values in the range for water or thawed soil, which is as expected as
this profile is taken furthest away from the mast, where the active layer was very thin compared to
around the mast and many electrodes were placed directly into frozen, ice rich soil.
The value of these resistances is also of importance regarding the accuracy of the measurements.
Indeed, a higher resistance at the electrode implies that the current experiences more difficulties
entering the soil such that there is a risk that the electrode can give wrong or no resulting measurements
at all. In order to avoid this, ABEM Terrameter LS 2 measures the grounding resistances of all the
electrodes before the actual measurements of the apparent resistivities are carried out. In the case
one of the electrodes indicate a resistance value of 10 kΩor higher, an error occurs. There are two
possibilities in this case: reducing the resistance by for instance adding some salt and water in the
hole where the electrode was installed in, or simply not using the electrode for the measurement. In
our case, we could luckily solve these warnings by using some salt as it is stated in the graphs in
Figure 3.28 that all the 41 electrodes have ground contact, such that a current can be passed into
to soil.

Results after Inversion
In this section, the inverted profiles are discussed which are obtained by using the Aarhus Workbench
[54]. The theoretical background for the inversion is given in the literature study in Section 2.5.1.
Practically, the first step in the inversion process is the verification of the ERT data, i.e. checking if
82
==PAGE:108==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
there are no illogical apparent resistivities in the data series as illustrated in Figure 3.29 for SIS2F-04.
Measurements of one profile consist of apparent resistivities per electrode locations, at several depths
and the graphical representation as shown in Figure 3.29 allows the identification and removal of
outliers. As there is of course a significant uncertainty in the data, an estimate of this should be
given to the software as well. In this case, a 5 % standard deviation is used.
Next, the DEM and Orthophoto, obtained from the drone footage processing as discussed in Section
3.7.2, is loaded into the Workbench. Especially an accurate DEM is of crucial importance, as the
software will take the actual elevation of each electrode into account as also shown in Figure 3.29. By
doing so, it is ensured that each data point where the apparent resistivity is known, is located on its
actual elevation, such that actually neighbouring data points are located correctly in the workbench.
In other words, if no DEM is given, the lines as shown in the bottom graph in Figure 3.29 would be
horizontal, and the data points would be at a location in space which does not correspond to their
actual location. To decrease the uncertainties due to this aspect, the high accuracy of our DEM
proved to be very useful.
Once all the outliers are identified and removed, it is possible to initiate the inversion process. For
this project, a 2D LCI inversion type is chosen, which is an inversion scheme with lateral constraints
on resistivities, depths or thicknesses of the 1D model [42]. Furthermore, smooth boundaries are
assumed for the continuous resistivity data, which means that in the 2D domain, the 1D model
changes continuously in space. In other words, the 1D model of the soil layers where each layer has
a variable thickness, resistivity and depth is now variable, with smooth transitions, over the whole
2D space which has proved to give better results when sedimentary deposits are encountered [42].
27
20
25
1E08
1E07
1E05
1E04
Elevation [m]
Apparent resistivity [Ωm]
5

Figure 3.29: Example of the preprocessing of the apparent resistivity data of SIS2F-04. Two outliers
are indicated with black circles and have been removed before the inversion was started.
As the 2D LCI inversion also uses a 1D model, the next step is to define how this 1D model should
be in the initial phase of the iteration. In this case, a 20 layered 1D model was chosen, with a first
layer of a thickness of 0.2 m, and with the last layer starting at 18 m depth. All the other settings
83
==PAGE:109==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
are set to automatic, and the inversion is started. For all models the residual ended being slightly
larger than 1, which for this project was assumed to be sufficiently accurate, especially as there is
other information available to verify the inverted resistivity profiles.
When interpreting resistivity profiles after inversion of the measured apparent resistivities, one should
always keep in mind the uncertainties in both the value of the resistivity at a certain depth, the
layer thickness and actual depth. Especially when one of the cases of equivalent models appears as
discussed in Section 2.5.1. Another unknown is often the actual depth to bedrock, as both bedrock
and cold permafrost have a similar resistivity. For this project however, based on geological surveys
and field observations, it is assumed that the thickness of the sedimentary deposit on top of the
bedrock exceeds 20 m. This implies that by taking the uncertainties of the resulting resistivity profiles
into account, no bedrock should be reached with our measurements. Lastly, Table 3.6 summarises
the ranges of resistivity values for some geotechnically interesting media which can be useful during
the interpretation of inverted resistivity profiles as discussed next in this section.
Table 3.6: Suggested resistivity value ranges for certain media [23].

SIS2F-01 and SIS2F-02
These two profiles are overlapping each other and are positioned parallel
to the Fjord, approximately 5 m towards the North with respect to the mast. SIS2F-01 is shown in
Figure 3.30 and only gives information regarding the resistivities for a maximal depth of 5 m, which
is a relative shallow depth of investigation due to the small spacing between the electrodes. However,
the resolution of the resistivities closer to the surface is quite high and a lot of detail is included in
the resistivity chart.
A first observation is that there is a soil volume around the mast whose resistivity differs significantly
from the surrounding soil. Indeed, it seems that there is a lower resistivity soil volume encapsulated
in higher resistivity soil. At the surface, there are even very low resistivity layers detected, which
correspond the very wet, thawed soil layers. These layers have been observed during the fieldwork
and active layer probing as well, such that it is valid to make this conclusion. Furthermore, the black
line in Figure 3.30 represents the frost level in the soil on the July 7th, 2022, obtained by probing
the active layer along the ERT line. It is clear that the areas with a higher resistivity at the surface
correspond with very shallow active layers, such that it can be assumed that the red spot around 20
m distance at the surface is still frozen. This especially since there was even surface ice observed at
that location.
The frost level depth indicates furthermore that the yellow soil volume is still frozen, albeit only
below the frost level. This implies that the lower measured resistivity is probably due to a change in
ice content compared to the purple and red zones, and that in general the yellow soil volume has an
altering thermal regime, probably warmer than the surrounding soils. It is very difficult to state to
what extent the soil around the mast differs from the surrounding soil volumes, but that there is a
difference, that is fact.
Profile SIS2F-02 was performed with a larger spacing between the electrodes, such that a larger area
is surveyed and larger depths are reached. The similarities between SIS2F-01 and SIS2F-02 are clear
and both profiles give the same result. Based on the two profiles, the width of the effected soil volume
84
==PAGE:110==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
can be estimated to be approximately 20 m. Based on the information provided by Nukissiorfiit,
and the technical drawings of the mast foundation, the excavated pit for the construction of the
foundation was approximately 2.5 m deep. Based on the profile SIS2F-02 depicted in Figure 3.31,
the thickness of the affected soil is at least 5 m around the mast, which is much deeper than the
actual excavation. As the pit was filled again with excavated soil, the soil mineral composition
is exactly the same as in the natural state, so the change in resistivity will be exclusively due to
warmer soil temperature and different ice content. It is however impossible to determine the extent
of contribution of the construction and the presence of the mast to this altered soil volume. However,
after construction, the original equilibrium was heavily distorted and it seems it did not restore to
the natural thermal conditions, despite even the use of insulation materials.

SIS2F-03
This profile is downhill, to the East with respect to the mast and intersects with both
SIS2F-01 and SIS2F-02 as indicated in Figure 3.32. SIS2F-03 confirms that there is indeed a higher
resistance soil volume, close to the surface, around 25 m as seen in SIS2F-02 and in between the
distance range of 20 - 25 m in SIS2F-03. However, SIS2F-03 just maps the edge of the high resistance
soil volume, as especially SIS2F-02 indicates that the centre of that soil volume has a very high
resistance (order of magnitude 104Ω⋅m). The absolute values should not be compared directly, but
the general trend certainly corresponds.
In SIS2F-03, there is also a very low resistivity soil volume identified (order of magnitude < 103Ω⋅m),
which is on its turn covered by a high resistivity layer. As discussed in Section 2.5.1, this sequence of
very high followed by a very low resistivity soil layer might cause some difficulties in the inversion
process due to the existence of equivalent models. The exact case here is probably low resistivity
equivalence, but as the lower soil layers are too uncertain with the performed method and electrode set-
up, this is the best guess possible. In order to check for equivalent models, several starting resistivities,
number of layers and initial layer thicknesses of the 1D model have been tried. Furthermore, the
Aarhus software enables the removal of data points where the local residual after inversion is much
higher compared to other data points. As there were some high residual points around the area with
the low resistivity, these data points were deleted and the inversion was repeated. However, despite
all these actions, the same result was obtained after every inversion. For the sake of clarity, not all
data points in the low resistivity area showed high residuals, in fact most of the residuals of the data
points in that area resulted in low (≈0.5) residuals after inversion. Moreover, the high resistivity
layer close to the surface above the very low resistivity layer, corresponds with the observation that
the active layer seems to be thinner uphill, while the active layer thickness increases in the downhill
direction. In addition to this, is that exactly at 10 m distance in the chart of the ERT profile
SIS2F-03, there is an anchor located at approximately 1.5 m towards the East. The bottom of an
anchor foundation is located approximately at 2 m depth, which would correspond with the centre
of the very low resistivity soil volume. In addition, around the anchors, the upper soil layers were
extremely saturated, with several surface streams present, which can also be seen on the orthophoto.
Consequently, another option could be that the very low resistivity soil volume is simply due to the
disturbed thermal regime due to vicinity of the anchor.

that at a depth of approximately 1 m a thawed layer is present at the location of this borehole. This

inversion, of a high variability of the frozen state of the sub surface in the area, as the borehole is

Furthermore, SIS2F-03 indicates that there is also a thawed soil layer present at the surface, which
corresponds with the observations during the drilling of this borehole. There was also a significant
ground water flow observed, as the hole during drilling got filled up quite rapidly.
The deepest soil samples from this borehole end in the yellow area in the resistivity profile, as shown
in Figure 3.32. Although the yellow/green area shows lower resistivities, a larger ice content with
relatively larger ice lenses was found at those depths in the borehole. Soil samples coinciding with
the red area, i.e. with larger resistivities, showed less ice lenses. These findings might indicate that
the main reason for the change in resistivity might be the change of temperature.
Unfortunately, the borehole depth is just not sufficient to have soil samples from the depth where
the inversion identifies a very low resistivity soil volume. It could have been interesting to check
for instance the salinity of the pore water of the deepest soil samples, as it is also possible that it
is a locally saline soil volume. Alternatively, it could be locally thawed as well, due to the mast or
construction. However, as the low resistivity soil volume is located uphill, this seems unlikely and is
not in line with other findings such as the active layer thickness variation and the soil temperature
measurements. As the borehole is located at a distance of 3 m towards the east, it is also possible
that the ’red’ area reaches deeper and that the lower resistivity volume ceases to exist or is located
deeper.

SIS2F-04
Based on the last ERT profile, SIS2F-04, it can be concluded that sufficiently far away
from the mast most of the soil is completely frozen. Only at some places at the surface there
are thawed soil volumes observed, which coincides with the observations on site as well. At some
electrode locations we had to drill into frozen soil that was exposed directly on the surface, and
at some locations there was an active layer of approximately 20-40 cm. Due to the significant
87
==PAGE:113==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
distance of this profile from mast and anchors, it can be assumed that at this distance the natural
soil conditions prevail.
Figure 3.33 indicates the location of the second borehole SIS2022-04, which is in this case located
very closely to the fourth ERT profile. Due to time constraints, we could not drill as deep as the
first borehole, as the depth of this borehole did not even reach 1 m. However, all the soil samples
were extremely ice rich, with extensive ice lenses which is in stark contrast what has been observed
close to the surface in borehole SIS2022-03 (see also Section 3.7.5). As borehole SIS2022-04 seems
to cover an area of resistivities ranging from 2000 to 10000 Ωm, it can be assumed in combination
with the observations, that these resistivities represent ice rich permafrost. As the bedrock is located
deeper than the depth of investigation of the ERT profile SIS2F-04, it is very likely that exclusively
ice rich sedimentary permafrost is depicted in the considered ERT profile.
As already mentioned, it is difficult to state exactly what is found in the subsurface along the whole
ERT profile, based on the resistivities. However, the comparison of SIS2F-04 with the three other
ERT profiles indicates undoubtedly the effect of the construction and the presence of the mast on
the thermal regime and the composition of the soil.
3.7.5
Soil samples
There have been both frozen and thawed soil samples retrieved during the fieldwork. First, the
thawed soil samples are discussed into further detail, where after a short comment is given on the
frozen soil samples, as the main discussion of the frozen soil samples is held in Chapter 4.
Thawed soil samples
After the fieldwork, there was not much time left before my departure from Greenland. That is why
the processing of the thawed soil samples is quite limited. All the collected information regarding the
thawed soil samples is shown in Table 3.7. The limited amount of information gives an indication
about the state of the active layer in the beginning of July of 2022.
The soil samples have first been weighted and measured in order to obtain the bulk modulus ρb. After
this, each sample was cut into two subsamples. One part was weighted and put into the oven at 105
○C for 24 hours as prescribed in DS/CEN ISO/TS 17892-1 in order to obtain the water content of
the considered subsample [60]. The water content of a soil sample is calculated as follows:
w = mw
mdry
⋅100%
(3.3)
with mw the mass of water present in the soil which is determined by the difference in weight of the
sample before and after it has been in the oven, and mdry is the dry mass of the sample after drying
at 105 ○C. A more thorough discussion about the lab work is given in Chapter 4.
The colour of the soil samples was determined by using the revised standard soil colour charts by
Oyama and Takehara [55]. More information as shown in Table 3.7 will not be available for this
project for the samples from the active layer, as these samples have not been shipped to Denmark.

Frozen soil samples
During the field work, we were able to retrieve 11 soil samples, corresponding to approximately 2.5
m of frozen core. With the limited amount of preparation time, and many last-minute designs and
solutions for the portable freezer, it is still an understatement to say that the amount of retrieved
soil samples exceeded our wildest expectations. Pictures of the frozen soil samples from during the
field work, can be found in the Appendix in Section A.5, together with the resulting borehole logs
that are depicted in Figure A.13.
The resulting borehole logs, as shown in Figure A.13, are composed by using the ASTM D4083-89
Standard Practice for Description of Frozen Soils [20], to give accordingly a consequent description of
the observed ice content. The type of ice lenses, and the amount of the ice are described qualitatively
based on the charts shown in the Appendix in Figure A.12 [20]. There are three categories in the
mentioned norm, based on the shape and amount of the visible ice. The first category is the N
category and represents the frozen soil samples where ice is not visible to the unaided eye. There are
three sub-categories, depending on how well the soil is bonded together, and if there is uniformly
distributed excess ice is present in the soil sample. The V category comprises frozen soil samples
with a significant amount of segregated ice which is clearly visible to the unaided eye. A condition
regarding the present ice is that they need to be individual ice masses or ice layers of a thickness of
maximally 25 mm. The sub-categories are in function of the shape and layering of the observed ice
lenses. The last category, denoted as ICE comprises samples where the ice strata are larger than 25
mm. One sub-category comprises soil samples where there is still some soil present in the sample,
while the other category corresponds to ’soil’ samples existing almost exclusively out of pure ice.
More detailed data about these frozen samples are given in Chapter 4, where the results from the
lab are discussed.
The first observations on the field were that the active layer at both boreholes was around 15 cm thick,
and very wet. Especially at the location of borehole SIS2022-03 there was a significant groundwater
flow observed, such that the drilled hole would fill up almost instantly.
At borehole SIS2022-03, there is a brown, organic, silty looking layer observed in the first 50 cm of the
borehole. There were ice lenses visible, but they were relatively small. According to our observations,
around 50 cm depth, the soil changed to a greyer looking, clayey soil, and the deeper we got, the
more the colour tends to grey and the larger ice lenses and larger ice volumes are observed.
Another interesting observation were two soil samples (SIS2022-03 S5 and S6) that were retrieved
in a questionable state. On observation, it seemed that the complete soil sample was thawed. In
the field, it was unsure what the reason could be for this observation, but as the drilling took a
significantly longer time compared to other samples, and that after the soil sample was retrieved
probing of the borehole taught us that the bottom felt rock solid, i.e. frozen, we assumed that the
89
==PAGE:115==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
drilling might have thawed the soil sample completely. Furthermore, when sample 6 was retrieved,
the borehole was even smoking.
However, after analysing all the available results, it is possible that these soil samples were thawed
in situ. Indeed, the ERT profiles suggest that there is a variation in resistivity around the location
of the borehole. Moreover, in the field notes it is also stated that sample 6 looked very stiff and
could be compared to a consolidated clay. Keeping in mind, that the ’natural’ soil samples, further
away from the mast retrieved in borehole SIS2022-04 are extremely ice rich, such that if the sample
would be thawed by the drilling, we would absolutely not retrieve a nice cylindrical soil sample. Also
considering the ice content of sample 7 (see Figure 3.34 (c)) and 8, it is unlikely that a completely
thawed sample would remain in such a nice cylindrical shape.
In other words, it is a real possibility that in the range of 90 - 129 cm, uphill of the mast, there was
a thawed soil layer both covered and underlain by a frozen soil layer.
For the second borehole, SIS2022-04, the depth is a bit more than 60 cm, below the active layer.
Here, the upper layer was again organic and silty, but the change of colour to grey starts a bit sooner
compared to the other borehole. An immense difference regarding the other borehole is that the ice
lenses were extensive, starting from a depth of approximately 20 cm below the active layer. There
were also much larger parts of the soil samples composed out of pure ice.
In conclusion, the general trend is that at the surface, more organic, silty soil is observed, and the
silt content decreases in favour of the clay content with increasing depth. The change in ice content
of the two boreholes is very likely due to the effects of the construction and the presence of the
mast, and it is definitely a significant change. Lastly, it is an interesting observation that there is a
possibility of the presence of a thawed soil layer, covered and underlain by frozen soil.
An example of the encountered ice volume in sample 2 from borehole SIS2022-04 is shown in Figure
3.34. With the samples indicating that the sedimentary permafrost is very ice rich and clayey, it is
clear that the soil conditions pose an even greater risk onto the stability issue of the power line with
the ongoing climate change and Arctic Amplification.
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 3.34: (a and b) Example of the amount of ice encountered in sample S2 from borehole
SIS2022-04, and (c) an example of the ice layers from the Vs category as encountered in sample S7
from borehole SIS2022-03.

==PAGE:116==
Chapter 3. Fieldwork at the Second Fjord in Sisimiut
3.8
Summary
In the preparations for the fieldwork, we developed a cheap and very well-functioning set-up for
shallow soil temperature monitoring. This set-up consists of commonly used and widely available
materials and proved to be easy to install during the field work. When the data was collected in
the end of March 2023, it was clear that during a period of almost nine months the set-up was still
functioning.
The obtained soil temperature data showed a severe influence of the mast on the thermal regime,
close to the mast. There was also a different extent of influence measured, depending on the downhill
direction or parallel to the Fjord. The soil mass downhill seemed to be more affected by the mast
than the other direction. The trumpets showed also clearly that the soil close to the surface is more
effected by the mast, and that the deeper soil layers show a smaller amplitude.
The effect of the solar radiation, air temperature and the wind velocity on the mast temperature was
investigated as well. It was proven that the solar radiation plays definitely a large role in heating up
the steel structure, such that adapting the colour of the mast could be beneficial.
The active layer probing confirms the findings regarding the non-symmetrical influence on the thermal
regime by the mast on the surrounding soil. Indeed, the thickness of the active layer in Western
direction (approximately downhill) is significantly larger compared to the Eastern direction which is
more or less parallel to the Fjord.
The ERT profiles gave a detailed insight in the actual subsurface conditions. They showed undoubtedly
an altered soil volume around the mast foundation, compared to the surrounding, unaffected natural
soil. It is important to note here, that it is impossible at this stage to conclude what effect the
construction of the mast had on the alteration of the soil, and what effect exclusively the heat
transport via the mast foundation into the ground has on the thermal regime of the surrounding soil.
The only certainty here is that there is a severe alteration, probably due to a combined effect of the
presence of the mast and the construction, and that this process of altering the soil, i.e. decreasing
the ice content and changing the thermal regime, is a self-reinforcing process that deteriorates the
situation for ensuring a stable subsurface for the foundation. More details for the reasons that this
process is self-reinforcing are given in Section 4.3.
Lastly, the soil samples on field showed us mainly the difference in ice content compared to the soil
samples from the borehole close to the mast and the borehole 20 m to the West of the mast. This
observation is in line with all the previously mentioned observations and indicates that the ’altered’
soil volume in the ERT profiles shows a soil volume around the mast with a higher temperature
and/or a lower ice content. The natural soil (SIS2022-04) showed to be extremely ice rich, which
poses even higher risks on the future stability of the structure.
91
==PAGE:117==
Chapter 4
Laboratory testing of permafrost soil
samples
In order to obtain a good view on the actual soil conditions of the area around Mast 9 and due
to the fact, that on the Southern shore of the Second Fjord in Sisimiut there have not been any
detailed soil investigations performed in a laboratory yet, it was decided to process the collected soil
samples in the permafrost laboratory at DTU in Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark. The soil samples arrived
mid-December 2022 at DTU, and as the last semester of this Master is held at NTNU in Trondheim,
I travelled to DTU for two weeks to work in the lab and process the soil samples. Due to the limited
amount of time, it was required to select what would be prioritised, but nevertheless I can present
you a relatively complete characterisation of the soil retrieved from the two boreholes at the Second
Fjord. This is undoubtedly thanks to the help of Anton, Danielle, Sigrid and Katarina who came to
help me out, completely voluntarily. Furthermore, while I was back again in Norway, Aimilia went
back to the cold lab and performed some last tests on some of the soil samples for me.
This chapter discusses first the used methods to obtain some important characteristics of the soil,
after which the results are shown and summarised.
4.1
Methodology
4.1.1
Frozen soil classification
In the lab, the first task was to perform again a visual manual frozen soil description, especially
regarding the ice content based on the standard which was used in the field as well (ASTM D4083-
89:2007) [20]. All observations have been recorded with a high-quality camera on a tripod, to
document the soil samples, ice lenses, organic layers and other interesting observations as accurately
as possible. Furthermore, Revised Standard Soil Colour charts of Oyama and Takehara [55] are
consulted as well to determine the colour of the frozen soil samples, in the same way as it has been
done for the thawed samples.
At DTU, there are in total three cold labs where the temperature can be controlled, so it was possible
to take our time and record everything which was noteworthy per soil sample. The largest cold lab is
shown in Figure 4.1 (a) and some of the soil samples, as they were packed after the fieldwork in
July, are shown in Figure 4.1 (b). The frozen transport was carried out successfully, only one of the
samples got deformed during the long transport from the Second Fjord to DTU in Denmark.
92
==PAGE:118==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.1: (a) One of the cold labs in DTU, Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark and (b) some of the soil samples
after unpacking them in the cold lab.
4.1.2
Frozen bulk density
The frozen bulk density of the samples is determined by weighing the samples with a scale having an
accuracy of 0.01 g and determining the volume by using the EinScan SP Desktop 3D Scanner of the
3D Shining company [61]. During the storage and the transport, some excess ice has formed on the
soil samples. Furthermore, some of the sludge from the drilling was refrozen onto the samples as
well. In order to obtain the actual density of the in-situ soil, the newly formed ice and frozen sludge
was scraped off before measuring the weight and the volume.
The scanning device was first calibrated by following the manual, after it was placed in a box that
could be closed as seen in Figure 4.2 (a). A completely black background was provided as well,
to ensure the contrast between the object to be scanned and the background is as big as possible.
Figure 4.2 (b) shows the scanning set-up whilst operational.
In order to ensure that there was practically no loss of water content during the volume measurements,
the 3D scanning was performed in one of the cold labs. The measuring process of each sample
consisted of scanning each sample several times while the scanning device uses different light
projections to record the structure of the surface of the sample as correctly as possible. By rotating
the sample after each scan session, such that in the end all the faces are scanned by the device, the
software is eventually able to compose the complete sample in a 3D space. In some cases, where
there was a significant amount of ice, the scanning device did not record those areas. To solve the
issue, stickers provided by the manufacturer were put onto the sample to increase the contrast.
Once the 3D object of the sample was ready and processed further by the software, the volume
was calculated automatically. In general, the set-up proved to be very efficient for the volume
determination, and way more exact than approximating the volume as a cylinder with averaged
dimensions of the soil sample.
The expression for the determination of the bulk density ρb in [g/cm3] is as follows [62]:
ρb = mf
V
(4.1)
with mf being the mass of the frozen soil sample in gram [g], and V the volume of the frozen soil
sample in [cm3].

4.1.3
Water content
Once, the (frozen) bulk density is determined, it is possible to proceed with the next step, which is
the preparations for determining the water content of the sample. Based on the water content, other
properties such as the dry density of the sample, porosity and saturation can also be determined.
The standard that was used for the water content determination is the ISO 17892-1 norm [60]. The
used scale is shown in Figure 4.3 (a) and has an accuracy of 0.01 g, which is in accordance with the
requirement from the norm. Next the oven, shown in Figure 4.3 (b), is set on a temperature of 105
○C and is preheated before the soil samples are put into the oven. The soil samples were processed
immediately, such that the water content loss due to the exposure to the ambient conditions was
minimised.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.3: (a) The used scales in the DTU labs, and (b) the 105 ○C oven used for determining the
water content.
In order to ensure that all the moist from the soil sample was evaporated in the oven, the samples
have been placed into the oven for at least 24 hours, which is also in correspondence with the norm.
94
==PAGE:120==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
When the soil samples were taken out of the oven, there was generally a 5 minute period until the
soil samples were weighted, to allow them to cool down. There was extra attention paid to cover the
samples in order to not expose them to the ambient conditions as it poses a risk that moist can be
taken up again.
Once the weight of both the frozen and dried sample is known, the calculation of the gravimetric
water content is calculated as follows:
w = 100 mw
md105
(4.2)
with w being the gravimetric water content in [%], mw the mass of water present in the considered
sample, obtained by subtracting the mass of the sample before it was put in the oven and the mass
of the sample after it has dried at 105 ○C, which is denoted as md105. The masses have the unit of
grams [g].
Based on the dried mass of the soil sample (md105), it is possible to obtain the dry bulk density as
follows:
ρd = md105

(4.3)

4.1.4
Loss on Ignition
The Loss on Ignition (LOI) is the mass fraction lost by incineration of a dried sample to constant
mass at a specified temperature [63]. The LOI is an indication of the fraction of organic material in
the soil sample. According to the Danish Norm that deals with the determination of the LOI of soil
samples (DS/EN 15935:2021) [63], the specified temperature where the incineration should take
place is 550 ○C. Furthermore, it is required that the soil sample was dried at 105 ○C until constant
mass was reached. These requirements are needed, to ensure that the mass loss during incineration
is purely due to the loss of organic content. The time that the soil samples need to be put into the
incinerator is minimal two hours, and to ensure that eventually a constant mass was reached, it was
opted to leave the samples for four hours in the oven.
As the incinerated sample was already dried for 24 hours at 105 ○C, the following expression yields in
the LOI in % [63]:
LOI = 100md105 −md505
md105
(4.5)
with md105 being the dry mass of the sample after being at least for 24 hours at 105 ○C and md505
the mass of the sample after four hours at 550 ○C.
Figure 4.4 shows the used oven, with Figure 4.4 (b) giving a sight inside the oven during the
incineration. Note that there are flames visible in the back of the oven, due to the significant organic
content of that particular soil sample.

4.1.5
Soil particle density
The particle density was determined by following the methodology described in ISO 17892-3:2015
[64]. In this case, a fluid displacement pycnometer is used, as shown in Figure 4.5 (b), and it allows
the determination of the difference in volume of liquid, needed to fill the pycnometer with and
without the sample [64]. Hence, the resulting density of the soil particles is calculated from the dry
mass of the soil particles in the pycnometer, and the volume difference of the liquid. The norm
describes two testing procedures for fluid displacements pycnometers: a wet and dry method. For
both methods, three pycnometers should be used with three sub-samples of the investigated sample.
This is required, as it is only concluded that the test was performed with a sufficient accuracy when
the difference between the three resulting grain particle densities is below 0.03 g/cm3. During the
lab work, both methods have been used and therefore both are discussed below.
For both the wet and the dry method, a reference measurement was taken of each used pycnometer,
to ensure that the actual total volume of the pycnometer is known. First, the dry mass of a clean
pycnometer is recorded. Whereafter the pycnometer is filled with air and placed for the night or
longer in the vacuum chamber. This is required, to facilitate the removal of air bubbles out of the
demineralised water. An additional precaution regarding trapped air bubbles in the used water, is to
prepare already 1 or 2 litres of demineralised water by putting it for one day or longer in the vacuum
chamber prior the actual pycnometer tests are conducted. This allows the demineralised water also
to reach a stable, room, temperature. The latter is important, as the volume of demineralised water
changes as a function of temperature.
Once it is ensured that the water in the filled pycnometer is de-aired, the pycnometer is filled
completely, and the cap is placed on the pycnometer to ensure that the volume is the same for each
measurement. Without delay, the mass of the full pycnometer is determined, and now in function of
the temperature, and the actual mass of demineralised water in the pycnometer, the volume of the
considered pycnometer is found.
96
==PAGE:122==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.5: (a) The vacuum chambers in the DTU lab and (b) the used fluid pycnometers for the
tests.
The dry method uses oven-dried specimens according to ISO 17892-1 [60], such that the dry mass
in the pycnometer is known before the fluid is added. Before adding the soil sample, it should be
crushed such that a powder is obtained. This will ensure that the water, especially with fine-grained
soils, will get around as many grains as possible. The process of obtaining an adequate sample
consistency is shown in Figure 4.6 (b).
Once the dry weight is known, the pycnometer is filled with demineralised water and placed into
the vacuum chambers for at least a night. According to the norms [64], the soil sample should be
covered with 10 to 20 mm of fluid in order to ensure that during the time in the vacuum chamber,
the fluid can penetrate the soil sample sufficiently. After a sufficient amount of time in the vacuum
chamber, the pycnometer with the soil sample is filled completely with the fluid and is weighted with
its cap. Note that it is important to dry the pycnometer completely before weighing and that the cap
is pushed not just hard enough in the pycnometer. In the cap, there is a hair thin channel present,
that allows the excess water to escape. Due to capillarity, there will be in most cases a water drop
on the top of the pycnometer, and for the sake of consistency it was opted to remove that drop at
all times before weighing.
After measuring the temperature of the demineralised water, it was possible to obtain the volume of
the soil sample and hence the soil particle density ρs by using the expression [64]:
ρs = md105
Vd
=
md105
(m1−m0)

ρw3

with Vd being the volume of the soil sample in the pycnometer which is computed with the following
pycnometer weights: m0 is the dry weight of the pycnometer, m1 is the weight of the pycnometer
completely filled with demineralised water, m2 is the mass of the pycnometer only filled with the
dry specimen and m3 is the weight of the pycnometer with the specimen and completely filled
with demineralised water. As the density and consequently the volume of water depends on the
temperature, ρw1 represents the density at the temperature when the pycnometer was weighted
fully filled with demineralised water and ρw3 is the density when the weight of the pycnometer was
determined with both the soil present and fully filled with demineralised water.

The wet method can be especially for soils with a significant fraction of fine grain the preferred
method to use, as the use of a ’pre-soaked’ sample facilitates the penetration of the water around as
many soil grains as possible. The latter is required for an accurate determination of the volume of
the soil sample. The steps and points of attention are very similar as for the dry method, except that
after the pycnometer test, the dry mass of the sample must be determined according to ISO 17892-1
[60]. The eventual soil particle density is determined in the same way as explained for Equation 4.6.
Extra care should be paid for soil samples with an excessive amount of organic matter present. Such
a case is shown in Figure 4.6 (a), where the organic content escaped the pycnometer while in the
vacuum chamber, also taking the fine soil particles out of the pycnometer.
Furthermore, as organic content has generally a density below 1 g/cm3, this method is not accurate
enough to give reliable results [64]. The combination of both reasons caused the resulting soil particle
densities to be not consequent for this soil sample.

4.1.6
Particle-size distribution
The particle-size distribution (PSD) of a soil expresses the mass fractions of the sizes of mineral
particles which compose the considered soil material [66]. The PSD is a fundamental soil property
98
==PAGE:124==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
and is closely related to most physical and chemical soil properties. Therefore, it affects almost any
soil function and is used to determine the class of the soil. Generally speaking, soil particles cover a
vast particle size range, as all particle sizes from large stones and rocks to the finest clays (< µm)
can be encountered in soils.
As the soil particles with larger diameters are relatively easily determinable by means of sieving or
simply measuring the diameters, the determination of the PSD is generally exclusively concerning the
determination of the soil fraction of the fine soils with particle diameters below 2000 µm [66]. The
usual representation of the PSD is the use of a cumulative PSD curve, where the mass fractions of
the soil particles expressed as a percentage are plot in function of the particle diameter. In other
words, the cumulative PSD curve indicates per particle size what the mass fraction of the soil sample
is with particle sizes smaller than the considered particle size.
In soil science, the commonly used methodology to obtain the cumulative PSD curve, is the
combination of sieving and sedimentation techniques. This combination of methods is required, as
purely sieving is not accurate enough for the soil fraction with particle sizes smaller than 63 µm,
and to determine the actual cumulative PSD of the soil fraction with diameters smaller than 63 µm,
gravitational sedimentation methods are used [66]. The latter method determines the fraction of soil
particles with a certain diameter, in function of the density of the soil-fluid suspension at a certain
depth or the pressure in the suspension along a certain depth range. The monitored properties change
in time as the sedimentation takes place and are recorded. The particle diameter can now be derived
based on the change of the suspension property, as first the soil particles with the largest diameter
will sediment. There are three commonly used sedimentation techniques: the Pipette method and
the hydrometer method which use the change in the density of the suspension, and the integral
suspension pressure (ISP) method which as the name suggests relates the change in pressure to the
particle sizes. The ISP method is used by the user friendly PARIO measuring device from the Meter
group [67]. The PARIO device is shown in Figure 4.7 (a) and the discussion in the next paragraphs
concerning the theoretical background of the software of the PARIO device, is mainly focused on the
ISP method.
The ISP method uses the change of the pressure in time, measured with high accuracy at a certain
depth within the soil-water suspension to derive the PSD [66]. As the measuring device is a relatively
long cylinder, with the pressure sensor at the bottom, the measured pressure is in fact the result of
the integral of the suspension density over the distance above the pressure sensor. The actual PSD
is obtained, by inverse modelling using globally optimising algorithms to fit the measured pressure
time series, with a pressure time series based on both an assumed PSD and a mathematical model.
The fitting can be aided by adding known mass fractions of the cumulative PSD obtained by wet
sieving of the investigated sample.
The ISP method is certainly based on the same physical principals as the pipette and hydrometer
methods, but has some advantages as it leaves the sedimentation process undisturbed, yields a
continuous PSD, and does not require any simplification or linearisation in the analysis.
All sedimentation methods use the assumption that the sedimentation of the soil particles can be
with a sufficient accuracy described by Stokes’ law, which is shown in Equation 4.10. On this basis,
an equivalent diameter of a particle needs to be defined as the diameter of a sphere of the same
density which settles with the same vertical velocity in the suspension.
v = g (ρp −ρsol)
18η
D2
(4.10)
With v [m/s] being the terminal sedimentation velocity of spherical particles of diameter D [m], g
the gravitational acceleration, ρp [kg/m−3] the density of the suspended particles in suspension and
ρsol [kg/m−3] the density of the solution which is calculated as follows:
99
==PAGE:125==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
ρsol = ρw + msalt
Vsol
(4.11)
With ρw [kg/m−3] being the density of pure water, msalt [kg] the mass of added dispersants which
prevent that soil particles sticking together which would increases their equivalent diameter, and Vsol
[m3] the total volume of the solution.
The dynamic viscosity η [kg/(m s)] and the density of water ρw both depend on temperature and
can be calculated from some known empirical equations from literature. In the PARIO software this
is performed automatically.
At the very beginning of the sedimentation analysis (t=0), the initial pressure under hydrostatic
conditions is completely determined by the depth of measurement, the mass of added particles, the
total volume of the suspension, the density of the particles, and the density of the fluid including
dissolved salts. In other words, the starting conditions are independent on the measured pressure.
For t>0, the particle sizes D which are still in suspension in function of time can be determined as
follows, by rearranging equation 4.10 and by assuming that z = vt with z being the depth measured
from the surface of the suspension downwards.
D(z,t) =

fraction. A certain mass fraction is related to the cumulative mass distribution function F(D)
ranging from 0 to 1 for respectively the smallest possible diameter to the largest possible diameter in
the considered soil sample.
F(D) = m(x ≤D)

==PAGE:126==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
The next paragraphs give some extra information on the actual procedure that is recommended to
follow during PSD measurements with the PARIO device [68].
A first important point of attention is the state of the sample used for the PARIO measurements,
as many factors might interfere with the pressure measurements causing the obtained PSD to
be incorrect. Firstly, it is crucial to know the actual dry mass of the soil sample used for the
measurements. Another important source of error in the measurements, is the presence of organic
matter, as it is known that organic matter binds clay particles together which results in wrong
resulting PSD curves [68].
A problem that has occurred during PARIO measurements in this project, is a significant thermal
gradient in the suspension. This was caused by the temperature difference of the used demineralised
water to make the suspension, and the temperature of the demineralised water where the PARIO
sensor was placed in, in preparation of the measurement. A last point of attention is that the amount
of gas bubbles should be reduced as much as possible during the mixing of the soil suspension, as
gas bubbles can cause incorrect pressure readings.
According to the manual, the first step in the soil sample preparation is to grind air-dried soil, that
has drying for at least 24 hours, and ensure that the maximal diameter is smaller than 2 mm by
sieving the ground soil sample. It is also important to use soil samples with an organic content lower
than 1.5 %. If the organic content is higher, it should be removed by using H2O2. Extra care should
be taken when soil samples consist of a significant fraction of plasters and salts, but this was not the
case for our soil samples.
It is important to use air-dried soil instead of 105 ○C dried soil, as the higher temperatures might
alter the particles such that not a representative PSD is obtained from the PARIO measurements.
The next step is to prepare a soil suspension as follows. It is important to ensure that the soil
particles stick as less as possible to each other. This is achieved by first using a chemical dispersant
and by performing a physical dispersion as well. In this project, the dispersant used is Na4P2O7 and
instead of making a 40 g/L solution of the mentioned salt, it is opted to add the dry salt to the dry
soil sample instead. Like this it is ensured that the exact dry mass of the dispersant is known.
The dry soil sample, and the dry dispersant is added in a sealable bottle, and it is ensured that the
masses are noted down exactly. Next, the demineralised water is added and in order to mix the
substances sufficiently, the solution is placed on a shaking table which makes circular motions, as
shown in Figure 4.7 (c). The suspension is mixed for at least 12 hours.
To ensure that the used air-dried soil sample does not contain any moisture anymore, a sub-sample
is taken and dried according to ISO 17892-1 [60] such that if needed a correction of the dry mass in
the prepared suspension can be performed regarding the eventual water content.
In order to get consequent and comparable results, it is crucial to prepare the sample and initiate
the measurement as precisely as possible according to the manual. The initialisation process of
PARIO measurements starts always with ensuring that all the sample preparation steps have been
completed and double checked. If this seems to be the case, the actual first step of the initialisation
of the measurements is the preparation of two cylindrical containers, with one of them containing
demineralised water. In order to ensure that the thermal gradient between the sensor and the soil
suspension is not exceeding the acceptable range, it is recommendable to prepare these two cylinders
in advance, such that the equipment and the demineralised water are on room temperature.
Just before the suspension is removed from the shaker, the sensor can be connected to a PC and
placed in the waiting cylinder with demineralised water in it.
In this project, the PARIO Classic mode is used, such that no samples of the suspension during the
measurements take place are required, so once the software is set up accordingly, a new project is
created and the relevant input parameters are given, the soil sample is taken from the shaker and the
sedimentation cylinder is prepared further. For the PARIO Classic mode, the minimal duration for
the test is 8 hours, and for this project a minimal time span of 12 hours is used.
101
==PAGE:127==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 4.7: (a) The operational Meter PARIO device, (b) the soil fraction with a particle diameter
smaller than 65 µm, (c) the shaking table in order to mix the soil sample, with the demineralised
water and dispersant agent and (d) the used sieves in order to give additional information of the
PSD for the PARIO software.
First, the soil solution is emptied in the larger sedimentation cylinder, and a demineralised water
spraying bottle can be applied to get the last remaining soil particles out of the mixing bottle. Next,
the volume of the suspension in the sedimentation cylinder is completed with room temperature
demineralised water until exactly 1 L is reached. This is possible as the height of the fluid column
corresponding with one Liter is indicated on the cylindrical containers. Once the volume is exactly
one Liter, a rubber plug should be used to close the container in order to prevent evaporation.
The next steps are guided by the software, once it is indicated that the measurements can start.
Once the PARIO sensor, which is still in the sand-by cylinder with demineralised water, is connected
and ready to start the measurements, the sedimentation cylinder is homogenised for 60 seconds by
102
==PAGE:128==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
performing an overhead shaking movement with the cylinder closed with a rubber plug. On the
last second of the 60 second count down, the last movement should be placing the cylinder upright
on the table where the measurement is going to take place, removing the rubber plug and placing
immediately the PARIO sensor into the sedimentation cylinder. The sensor is activated by rotating
the sensor to a horizontal position while transferring it from the standby cylinder to the sedimentation
cylinder.
After this the measurements will commence and it is crucial that the temperature remains stable,
and that the cylinder or sensor are untouched or not subject to vibrations.
If the measurements are finalised, the soil sample in the sedimentation cylinder needs to get sieved
through a 63 µm, 250 µm, 500 µm, 1 mm and 2 mm sieve combination as shown in Figure 4.7 (d).
This sieving process is the wet method, such that the remaining fraction of the soil samples on each
sieve need to be dried according to to ISO 17892-1 [60]. Once the dry masses are known, this data
is put into the software, and it will calculate by global optimisation the PSD curve which fits the
pressure measurements the best.
4.1.7
Atterberg limits
The Atterberg limits are determined based on the Danish Standard DS 17892-12 [69], and consist
of the Liquid limit (wL) and the plastic limit (wP). These two limits form the boundary of a range
of water contents where the soil behaves as a plastic medium as shown in Figure 4.8. Once the
water content is above the liquid limit, the soil behaves more as a liquid and can lose for instance its
bearing capacity. If the water content drops below the plastic limit, the soil starts to behave more
and more as a solid.
In order to obtain wL and wP, two separate tests are conducted as explained later in this section.
Before going into the practical aspects of the tests, some other indices are discussed.
wnat
wP
wL
w
IP
Figure 4.8: The location of the plastic and liquid limit on the water content axis of a certain soil.
The natural water content and the plastic index IP are also shown.
As shown in Figure 4.8, the range of water contents in which the soil behaves as a plastic medium is
described by the plastic index IP, which can be determined by the expression in Equation 4.17. The
range of water contents of the soil where it behaves plastically, increases with an increasing plastic
index.
IP = wL −wP
(4.17)
Next, in order to determine in what state the in-situ soil is, it is possible to determine the consistency
index IC by using the expression in Equation 4.18. The higher this value, the firmer and crumblier
the soil will be. Negative values indicate that the in-situ soil is in the plastic region.
IC = wL −wnat
wL −wP
(4.18)
Before any test can take place, it is crucial that the soil sample is homogenised and that the coarse
fraction (>0.4 mm), roots and other significant organic particles are removed.
In order to obtain now the liquid limit, the fall-cone method is performed. During this test, the
penetration of a 60 g cone with an angle of 60 ○is measured as a function of the water content.
103
==PAGE:129==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
The used apparatus is shown in Figure 4.10 (b) and consists of a releasing mechanism for the falling
cone, a vertical rod in which the height of the cone can be changed, and a container in which the
soil sample can be placed. The tip of the cone should be placed precisely above the surface of the
levelled soil sample, such that the cone and the surface of the prepared sample are just not touching.
The test needs to be repeated for several water contents of the soil sample, such that the penetration
depths range between 7 and 15 mm. The water content is changed by using a glass plate, rubber
stop, and a dryer as shown in Figure 4.9 (b). In the case the water content needs to be increased, a
spray bottle with demineralised water is used and the rubber stop is applied on to the sample on the
glass plate to make the soil sample as homogenised as possible. At least five fall-cone tests for five
different water contents should be recorded per soil sample. After each successful cone penetration
reading, the water content of the soil directly around the tip of the cone is determined according
to ISO 17892-1 [60]. Once the water content is known as a function of the penetration depth, the
data points are plotted on a semi-logarithmic graph, with the water content on a linear scale and the
penetration depth on a logarithmic scale. The liquid limit wL is now found as the water content at a
penetration depth of 10 mm, based on the linear trend line of the data points in the semi-logarithmic
space.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.9: (a) A glass plate and rubber stopper used to smoothen the soil sample, and (b) shows
how the water content was decreased in the soil sample during the Atterberg limit testing.
In order to determine the plastic limit of a soil sample, the Danish Standard prescribes the following
test. First, approximately 20 grammes of soil sample is required and is dried on a ceramic or glass
plate until it is possible to shape the soil sample into a ball. Next, by using the heat of one’s hands
during rolling in between one’s fingers and hand palms, the ball is dried further until small cracks are
observed on the surface of the ball. At that point, the ball is divided into two equal subsamples, and
the following procedure is performed on each subsample. Each subsample is now again divided into
four parts. Each part is remoulded in between one’s fingers until the moisture is equally redistributed
in the considered part. The next step is now to roll the sausages on a ceramic plate, with movements
from the tip of the finger towards the second joint, as illustrated in Figure 4.10 (a). A slight and
uniform pressure should be applied to the sausage so that the diameter is reduced to 3 mm. In
the author’s opinion, the most practical way of ensuring that the correct diameter is reached, is by
comparing the sausage with a rod of diameter 3 mm as also shown in Figure 4.10 (a). If the sausage
cracks both longitudinally and transversely when the diameter of 3 mm is reached, and the soil pieces
do not gather together after they have initially crumbled, the first crumbling point represents the
plastic limit. The crumbled soil samples should be collected in a sealable container until all four
104
==PAGE:130==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
parts of the subsample are rolled until the first crumbling point occurs. Once the whole subsample is
processed, the containers can be processed according to ISO 17892-1 [60] in order to obtain the
water content. The eventual average water content of both sub-samples is the plastic limit of the
investigated soil sample.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.10: (a) Sausage rolling in order to determine the plastic limit and (b) the falling cone
apparatus used for the determination of the liquid limit.
Based on the Atterberg limits and certain characteristics of the cumulative grain size distribution
curve, it is possible to classify the soil sample according to the Standard Practice of the Unified Soil
Classification System (USCS) described in the ASTM norm D2487-11 [21]. The classification is
performed by following flow charts as presented in the norm and are used and discussed in Section
4.2.5.
4.1.8
Permittivity measurements
The permittivity measurements were carried out by using the HydraProbe (HP) sensor from Stevens
Water Monitoring Systems [47]. As explained in the Literature Study in Section 2.5.3, the permittivity
as a function of temperature allows determining the relation between soil temperature and (unfrozen)
water content. The HP sensor is shown in Figure 4.11, and consists of two main components: the
four tines with a diameter of 3 mm and a length of 45 mm, and the head of 42 mm in diameter where
the microprocessors and other electrical components are present and protected from the elements
[17]. The four tines are positioned in such a way that one tine is placed concentrically onto the
sensor head with respect to the three other tines. The HP sensor is a frequency domain reflectometry
sensor (FDR), such that the three surrounding tines emit a 50 MHz signal into the soil that creates
a coaxial transmission line [17]. The central tine functions as the actual sensing tine, as it receives
the electromagnetic waves and transmits an electrical response which can be processed and related
to the capacitance of the circuit. The capacitance is on its turn related to the complex dielectric
permittivity.
The HP sensor has a temperature sensor in its head as well. However, as the soil temperature is
required around the tines, and not the whole sensor (tines and head) is covered in the investigated
soil, it is opted to use an external temperature sensor as shown in Figure 4.14 (c).
A common source of error with these types of sensors is that air gaps between the medium and the
tines can influence the measurements significantly. So special care should be taken in order to ensure
105
==PAGE:131==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
good contact, and during the first measurements monitoring of the permittivity can identify contact
problems between the medium and the tines immediately.
Figure 4.11: The HydraProbe sensor of Stevens Water Monitoring Systems [17].
The CR6 Automated Monitoring Platform from Campbell Scientific, Inc., as shown in Figure 4.12,
is used as the data logger to connect and control the temperature and HP sensor [70]. This data
logger also allows to store the data without even being connected to a computer or a power source
and proved to be a very practical and versatile monitoring and data collection system.
Once it is made sure that the sensors are connected properly, and that the monitoring of the sensor
readings is operational, the recording of the measurements is verified if it is according to the code
that controls the operations of the sensor. In the latter code, it is stated that every 5 minutes, the
HP and temperature sensor should carry out 5 measurements every 8 seconds. The eventual resulting
value of the measured variables is reported as the average of these five measurements.
The installation of the sensor into the frozen soil sample required precise drilling work as shown in
Figure 4.14 (a) and (b), in order to ensure a good contact between the tines and soil, and in order
to avoid air gaps between the soil and tines.
The hole for the centre tine was drilled with a 4 mm drill bit, while the holes for the three surrounding
tines were drilled with a 4.8 mm drill bit. This choice was made to allow for some flexibility during
the installation of the sensor in the well. Indeed, as the soil sample was completely frozen, it did
not allow some slight deformations when the four tines were carefully pushed into the four drilled
holes. Only with some larger holes and a mallet as shown in Figure 4.14 (d), the sensor could be
successfully installed.
The temperature sensor was next installed at a distance of 7 cm above the aluminium ring covering
the sensor head as shown in Figure 4.14 (c). The aluminium ring ensured that the soil sample could
be packed in such a way that during the thawing of the sample, the risk of losing water from the
sample was minimised. Indeed, as shown in Figure 4.14 (e) and Figure 4.13 (b), there was a hard
plastic cylindrical cover placed around the soil sample, such that the plastic cover and the aluminium
cover around the sensor head fitted perfectly. By the addition of plastic foil and tape directly around
the soil sample, and plastic foil and tape around the plastic cylindrical cover, there was no leakage
observed during the test.

==PAGE:132==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples

soil sample and sensor.
It was important to keep the soil sample upwards, which means that the HP sensor should be placed
in such a way that the tines point vertically upwards. If the sensor would be lying horizontally,
there is a risk that the frozen soil sample, while thawing, could slide of the tines resulting in false
measurements. Therefore, the soil sample, sensor, and plastic cover combination was placed in a
Styrofoam box, filled with Styrofoam pellets as shown in Figure 4.13 (b) and vertically placed as
shown in Figure 4.13 (a). The Styrofoam also functioned as an insulator to try to slow the rate of
temperature change, allowing the collection of as much as possible permittivity measurements as a
function of the soil temperature.

The actual measurements of the permittivity started once the frozen sample was placed in the
Styrofoam box in the laboratory by room temperature, where the data logger was connected with a
PC to ensure the readings could be monitored. In order to obtain measurements of the hysteretic
behaviour of the unfrozen water content as a function of the temperature, the Styrofoam box with
the soil sample was moved to the cold lab, where the data logger could continue logging without
being connected to a PC while the temperature decreased again. After a sufficient amount of time,
such that the soil temperature was around - 10 ○C, the Styrofoam box containing the soil sample was
places in the lab on room temperature again. The test eventually ended when the soil temperature
reached approximately 20 ○C. In total, the whole measurement process for one soil sample took 24
hours.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.15: Calibration of the HydraProbe sensor by, measuring the complex permittivity of (a) 99.9
% ethanol and (b) air.
It is also important to calibrate the used HP sensor, to ensure that the deviation between the recorded
permittivity values and the (assumed) actual values is as minimal as possible. This is required, as
the HydraProbe does not measure the unfrozen water content directly, or in other words, there is no
physical correlation between the permittivity, which is measured, and the unfrozen water content.
Indeed, the permittivity is measured directly and linked with some known empirical relationships
to the unfrozen water content. Hence, the measured permittivity should be calibrated to ensure a
correct resulting unfrozen water content. The calibration of the HP sensor can be performed by
measuring the permittivity in function of temperature of three media, of which the permittivity as a
function of temperature is well documented in literature. The three chosen media for this project
are demineralised water, air, and 99.9 % ethanol, and the calibration process of (a) ethanol and (b)
air is shown in Figure 4.15. Next, a calibration plot is used to determine the needed correction of
the measurements. Such a calibration plot is a very practical visual proof to assess the extent of
deviation of measurements from the expected values. The horizontal axis of the plot represents the
measured values while the vertical axis represents the theoretical values. The identity line is a last
tool in a calibration plot to facilitate the assessment of the extent of deviation between measurements
and theoretical (or expected) values. During the measurement of the permittivity of ethanol, the
container was wrapped with plastic and tape, to ensure that the evaporation of the ethanol would be
as minimal as possible. Each measurement was performed for at least one hour, to obtain a stable
reading with a minimal temperature variation.
109
==PAGE:135==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
For the determination of the real part of the dielectric permittivity of water, an interpolation
function proposed by Ellison is used which precisely represents the complex permittivity, at standard
atmospheric pressure, for frequencies and temperatures in the ranges of [0,25] THz and [0,100]
○C respectively [71]. The interpolation function was formulated in 2006 based on the available
experimental permittivity data for pure water at that time. As the calibration measurements were
performed in a lab at room temperature, and as the HydraProbe operates at a frequency of 50 MHz,
it is valid to use Equation 4.19 in order to obtain a value for the real part of the dielectric permittivity
of pure water in function of the measured temperature t and the applied frequency ν.

completeness a copy of the coefficient values is attached in Appendix A.9. As an example, for a
temperature of 21.25 ○C, the real part of the dielectric permittivity for water equal 79.73.
The real part of the dielectric permittivity of air in practical purposes is assumed to be equal to unity,
as the actual value is approximately 1.0006 in the temperature range of [5,50] ○C and its variation in
function of the applied frequency can be neglected for the purposes of our experiments [72].
Lastly, the real part of the dielectric permittivity for 99.9 % ethanol at a temperature of 21.48 ○C is
deduced based on measurements published by Gregory and Clarke [24]. The temperature at which
the permittivity is required, is equal to the mean temperature of the calibration measurement of the

temperatures 20 and 25 ○C, extrapolation and interpolation are applied on the tabulated values, in
order to obtain the final result. Table 4.1 depicts the used values in order to obtain the eventual real
part of the permittivity of εr = 25.05.
