Once the calibration plot is composed, and the eventual correction for the measured real parts of the
dielectric permittivity is known, the data can be processed further until the unfrozen water content
as a function of the soil temperature is obtained. This data processing is discussed into further detail
in Section 4.2.6.
4.1.9
Specific Heat measurements
In order to obtain information regarding the thermal properties of soil, it is possible to use Specific
Heat (SH) sensors of the dual needle heat pulse type, manufactured by the East 30 Sensors company
[73]. The respective sensor is depicted in Figure 4.16 and it is clear that it consists of two pins with
a length of 30 mm and a diameter of 1.27 mm [73]. The distance between the two pins is 6 mm.
One of the pins is an Evanohm heater and functions according to the control code in this project as
a heat pulse transmitter which produces every hour a heat pulse, over a period of six seconds. The
other pin functions as the receiving sensor which is able to register the temperature in function of
time and consists of a 10 kΩthermistor.
According to the manufacturer, the guarantied measuring accuracy of the thermistor is ≈5 % which is
assumed to be sufficiently accurate for this project as temperature differences are considered instead
of absolute temperatures. An important point of attention regarding the accuracy of the sensor, is
the exact pin geometry [18]. Indeed, even the smallest deviations in the distance between the pins,
the diameter or the orientation of the pins can influence the resulting thermal properties. Therefore,
before starting the measurements, it is crucial to measure the state of the sensor.
The eventual set-up for the specific heat measurements is shown in Figure 4.17. For the data logging
and process control, another data logger is used than with the permittivity measurements. As shown
in Figure 4.17 (a), a CR800 data logger from Campbell Scientific is used [74]. The set-up is such
that the logger is able to collect data without being connect to a PC, and a back-up battery ensures
that the data logging can continue during an eventual power blackout. Although in this project only
one SH sensor is coupled onto the system, it is possible to connect and control several sensors at the
same time.
As during the tests, the soil sample should not lose any water through the bottom of its container, it
is important that a watertight container - sensor configuration is used. Therefore, the configuration
as shown in Figure 4.17 (b) is deployed during this project, being a combination of a watertight,
cylindrical container where the SH sensor is incorporated into the base of the container. The base of
the container is made from Polyoxymethylene (POM), and the sensor is inserted and sealed exactly
in the middle of the base with wetroom grout [18]. This base has been developed during the Thesis
of Jakob Nielsen with the cooperation of DTU SkyLab, in order to ensure that the sensor pins are
111
==PAGE:137==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
for every measurement placed exactly in the centre of the soil sample. Furthermore, as previous
experiments during the thesis of Jakob Nielsen showed that an earlier used aluminium base effected
the measurements, the less conductive POM material proved to be a solution for this issue.
Receiver
Transmitter
6 mm
30 mm
Figure 4.16: The used specific heat sensor manufactured by East 30 sensors [18].
(a)
POM base
SH Sensor, sealed
(b)
Figure 4.17: (a) the used CR800 data logger set-up with the possibility to connect several sensors at
the same time, and (b) the used SH sensor incorporated into a cylindrical, sealed container.
The thermal properties of the investigated medium are determined based on the theory as explained
in Section 2.5.2. The actual model that enables the calculation of the thermal properties has been
elaborated by Ingeman-Nielsen et al. [48] and is based on the assumption that the sensor heater
is an infinite line source. The model yields the thermal properties of the investigated medium by
performing an inverse optimisation of the match between the solution of the differential equation
as expressed in Equation 2.33 in cylindrical coordinates, and the measurements by changing the
thermal properties of the medium in the optimisation algorithm. Note as in our experiments there is
no phase change taking place, the solution of the temperature difference that is optimised with the
measurements is in fact expressed in Equation 2.54.
Before an actual soil sample was tested, the resulting thermal properties based on the measurements
of the specific heat sensor were verified, by using glass beads as the tested material. As the glass beads
112
==PAGE:138==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
are specially manufactured to serve as a reference material of which the properties are known and are
not due to significant changes in function of temperature, it is possible to verify the calculated thermal
properties from the inverse problem. It is assumed that in the case the thermal properties provided
by the manufacturer are obtained from the reference tests, the method is considered validated and
reliable. As the glass beads form a porous medium, similar to (sandy) soils, a positive conclusion
from the reference test would confirm that the method can be applied for the determination of
thermal properties for soil samples as well. The properties of the used glass beads are summarised in
Table 4.2.
Table 4.2: Technical specifications of the used glass beads, as specified by the manufacturer [25, 18].

During the reference testing, tests on both saturated and dry glass bead samples were conducted.
The purpose of this was to investigate whether the difference in water content would impact the final
result of the inversion problem. Theoretically, the presence of water should not alter the thermal
properties of the glass beads, which are considered as the ’grains’ in these reference tests.
The procedure that results in the thermal properties of the considered medium, starting from the
actual measurements, is illustrated in Figure 4.18. The first step is indeed to conduct temperature
measurements at the receiver pin of the sensor, where the change in temperature is recorded due
to an emitted heat pulse at the transmitting pin of the sensor. For this project, the lab at room
temperature is used, and the measurement is performed for several cycles. As temperature differences
are considered, the slight fluctuation in the room temperature does not affect the eventual result
significantly, but it is still beneficial to try to have several measuring cycles at a similar starting
temperature.
Once the measurements are available, the cycles are selected with a minimal variation on the starting
temperature as shown in Figure 4.18. The code, provided by Thomas Ingeman-Nielsen, calculates
the mean power of the heat pulse at the start of each cycle as well, as this is required in the solution
of the considered differential equation.
After taking the average of the temperature difference in function of time of the selected cycles (step
3), it is possible to start the optimisation algorithm in order to obtain the thermal conductivity and
the volumetric heat capacity of the considered medium.
The preparations of the soil sample for the thermal measurements, were similar as for the Atterberg
limit determination, as it is again important to obtain a homogeneous soil sample, with no lumps
and no coarse sand or gravel. Once such a soil mixture is obtained, the soil sample was placed into
the cylindrical container as depicted in Figure 4.19 (a). In order to consolidate the soil sample in the
cylinder, such that no air bubbles or larger voids were present, the cylinder with the soil sample was
put for a couple minutes on a shaking table that is normally used for sieving. This procedure seemed
to work efficiently.
Figure 4.19 (b) shows lastly how the testing configuration was placed and monitored in the lab at
room temperature.

Figure 4.19: (a) a shaking table was used to ensure that there were no air bubbles trapped in the
prepared soil sample, and (b) the operational Specific Heat sensor set-up.
114
==PAGE:140==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
The obtained thermal conductivity and the volumetric heat capacity from the inversion are a bulk
property and not the property of the actual soil particles. As the thermal properties and behaviour of
water, ice and air are known, it is possible by using the expressions explained below to obtain the
thermal property of the soil particles.
The thermal conductivity is calculated based on the geometric mean expression in Equation 4.22
proposed by Johansen (1975) [4], which is similar to Equation 2.41, as in the latter equation the
saturation is not taken into account due to the assumption of a fully saturated soil (Sw = 1). Hence,
by knowing the saturation, (unfrozen) water content and porosity, it is possible to obtain the required
parameter value. In our experiments, only the bulk unfrozen thermal conductivity was measured,
which implies that the normalised unfrozen water content fraction is ϕ = 1, as no ice is present in
our soil sample. There is a need of making a distinction between thermal properties in frozen and
unfrozen state, as a when a material freezes, the soil composition and structure alters causing a
different response in both thermal conductivity and heat capacity [4].
Therefore, Johansen’s expression can be further simplified and rewritten to obtain the actual thermal
conductivity of the soil particles in unfrozen state.
λsat = λ1−n
s
λ(nSw(1−ϕ))
i
λnSwϕ
w
⇒λsat = λ1−n
s
λnSw
w
⇒λs = ( λsat
λnSw
w
)
1−n
(4.22)
Once λs is known, it is possible to use the original expression of Johansen, in the left part of Equation
4.22, to obtain the bulk thermal conductivity in function of the temperature, as ϕ is the unfrozen
water content fraction obtained through the permittivity measurements, in function of temperature.
As only the unfrozen thermal conductivity is known however, it will be assumed that for this project
the unfrozen and frozen thermal conductivity is equal.
In order to obtain the volumetric heat capacity of the soil particles, the expression as discussed for
Equation 2.47, can be used. Note that in this case again, only the unfrozen thermal property was
measured.
cvu = cs(1 −n) + cwn ⇒cs = cvu −cwn
1 −n
(4.23)
An alternative way for computing the unfrozen bulk volumetric heat capacity is by using Equation
4.24. Based on the volumetric heat capacity of water cvw, the dry density of the soil ρd and the
density of water ρw, it is indeed possible to calculate the unfrozen volumetric heat capacity cvu in
function of the water content w as expressed in the following equation [4]:
cvu = ( ρd
ρw
)(0.17 + 1.0 w
100)cvw
(4.24)
Similarly, for the frozen volumetric heat capacity cvf, the expression in Equation 4.25 can be used
[4]. Here, the heat capacity is calculated in function of the unfrozen and total water content being
respectively wu and w.
cvf = ( ρd
ρw
)[(0.17 + 1.0wu
100 ) + 0.5(w −wu
100 )]cvw
(4.25)
Both the unfrozen and frozen volumetric heat capacity can now also be linked to calculate the bulk
volumetric heat capacity, in function of the unfrozen water content, as explained in Equation 2.47.
4.1.10
Electrical conductivity measurements
The salinity of the soil samples has been carried out in accordance with the theory as explained
in Section 2.5.4. The Salinity is deduced based on conductivity measurements and the PSS 78
115
==PAGE:141==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
algorithm, which yields in a Practical Salinity. The used code to compute the Practical Salinity has
also an extension according to the suggestions of Hill et al. [51], to make the Practical Salinity scale
valid for salinities lower than 2 h.
The testing procedure is according to the recommended lab practice of Thomas Ingeman-Nielsen,
which contains the following steps:
• Prepare two sub-samples of a soil sample: one sub-sample is used for the gravimetric water
content determination, and the other sub-sample is prepared for the conductivity measurements.
• Determination of the gravimetric water content according to ISO 17892-1 [60] of one of the
sub-samples.
• Preparation of the other sub-sample for the conductivity measurements, by drying it for 48
hours at 50 ○C. If for the conductivity measurements one would use material dried at 105
○C, as described in ISO 17892-1 [60], clay minerals could get altered and might influence the
conductivity measurements.
• Preparation of demineralised water the day before the conductivity measurements take place,
such that the demineralised water can reach a stable room temperature.
• Instead of 5 g dry and crushed sample material, 10 g has been used to ensure there is enough
material around the sensor of the device depicted in Figure 4.20.
• The crushed and weighted soil material is added to a plastic bottle, together with 25 ml of
demineralised water (which has been placed at room temperature the day before).
• In this project, per soil sample, five soil samples are prepared as described in the two previous
points.
• All the samples are placed on a shaking table for at least 12 hours, in this case exceptionally a
shaking table normally used for sieving had to be used.
• After 12 hours, the samples are taken from the shaker, and let to rest for 10 minutes at room
temperature.
• Next, a stable reading of the conductivity and the temperature are recorded for each sample,
immediately after the 10 minutes of rest, as more time might alter the results.
• The air pressure, needed in the calculations, is retrieved from the meteorological services of
the location of the testing.
• The whole conductivity and temperature measuring process is performed three times, to ensure
stable readings have been obtained.
• The obtained data is processed with the code based on the PSS 78 algorithm and Hill correction
for lower salinities.
The measuring device that was used to determine the conductivity and the temperature is shown in
Figure 4.20. The YSI 3200 conductivity instrument is able to measure the conductivity, but in the
lab the device was calibrated to measure the resistivity with an accuracy of ≈0.1 % and resolution
of 0.01 kΩcm [75]. The temperature is measured with a resolution of 0.01 ○C and an accuracy of ≈
0.1 ○C [75]. The used conductivity cell is the ’D3255’ type as shown in Figure 4.20, which has a
built in thermistor and an extremely stable black platinum electrode [75]. The material used for the
cell is Pyrex 7740, which is a borosilicate glass [75]. The suspension is sucked up by using a syringe
116
==PAGE:142==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
as indicated in Figure 4.20, and the cell is kept filled for a couple seconds, in such a way that the
electrode is completely submerged, to ensure that the reading becomes stable.
Cell
Figure 4.20: The device used for the determination of the conductivity of the soil sample.
As the resistivity was measured, there is a need for a unit transformation to the unit of conductivity
which is in this case in the used code in milliSiemens per centimetre [mS/cm]. The conversion from
the measured values to the required unit of conductivity is as follows:
x Ω⋅cm = x cm

with x being the value of the measured resistivity in Ωcm.
4.2
Results
4.2.1
Frozen soil classification
The visual inspection, which is non-destructive, resulted in almost exclusively the same conclusions
as during the inspection in the field. Regarding borehole SIS2022-03, there was only a significant
ice content observed in the depth range 129 cm - 189 cm. The upper soil samples proved to be
siltier than the lower soil samples where the clay content seemed to be increasing and dominating.
Regarding borehole SIS2022-04, the upper soil sample showed some more organic content and
silt compared to the deeper samples, However, here the ice content difference was striking, as for
SIS2022-04 the ice content was already significant very close to the surface. The findings of both
the lab work and the field work, regarding the soil classification are summarised in the Tables 4.3
and 4.4. Lastly, the Appendix in Section A.8 gives a borehole log, updated with pictures of the soil
samples and information obtained during the lab work.

4.2.2
General soil characteristics
Before soil samples were put in the oven in accordance with the norms SO 17892-1 [60] and DS/EN
15935:2021 [63], the volume of some of the samples was determined by using the EinScan SP
Desktop 3D Scanner of the 3D Shining company [61] as explained in Section 4.1.2. The scanning
took place in one of the cold rooms in the lab at DTU, to ensure that the soil sample would not melt
and loose water during the measuring process. The scanning device was placed in the cold room well
ahead the scanning, to ensure the objects that would come into direct contact with the soil samples
would be sufficiently cold as well.
Only a certain selection of soil samples was measured with the 3D scanning device, due the limited
period of time during I was present at the Cold Lab at DTU in Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark. For future
work, a more structured approach, for instance according to the attached flow chart in the Appendix
A.10 from the thesis of Jakob Nielsen [18], should be followed instead. With respect to the amount
of soil samples that were available to be investigated, a two-week period was definitely not sufficient
either.
Figure 4.24 depicts the 3D model of soil sample SIS2022-03 S1A that was obtained with using the
scanning device. Based on this model, the software that performs the scanning, is able to compute
the volume of the 3D object. This volume was consequently used to determine the bulk and dry
density, void ratio, and porosity. The volumes and the other general soil characteristics of the
measured soil samples are summarised in Table 4.5 and Table 4.6 for samples of borehole SIS2022-03
and SIS2022-04 respectively.
Figure 4.24: 3D model of soil sample SIS2022-03 S1A obtained with the EinScan SP Desktop 3D
Scanner.
The difference in soil characteristics between the two boreholes is immediately clear, which is
undoubtedly due to the effect of the mast and construction on the soil closest to the mast (SIS2022-
03).
Indeed, the water content is much lower in those samples, compared to the samples of
SIS2022-04, approx 20 m towards the West of the mast. Furthermore, the water content is increasing
with increasing depth for soil samples of borehole SIS2022-04, which is in line with the increasing
amount of ice with increasing depth.
The suspicion about the thawed state of samples 5 and 6 of SIS2022-03 seem to be in line with the
lower water content for these soil samples and with the higher dry and bulk density. In general, the
organic content is decreasing with depth for all soil samples.

4.2.3
Particle density
There have been four soil samples investigated regarding their soil particle density, with all relatively
similar results. The measurements for sample S8 from borehole SIS 2022-03 went completely
according to the norm ISO 17892-3:2015 [64], and the difference between the three measurements is
below the threshold value of 0.03 g/cm3 as shown in Table 4.7. Hence, the obtained mean value is
equal to the actual particle density, with is in the range of the acceptable uncertainties.
However, the results of the samples SIS 2022-03 4 A,B,C and SIS 2022-04 1C, 2 showed some
anomalies. The difference between two of three measurements was each time slightly larger than the
threshold of 0.03. As this was only the case for the difference between two of the three measurements,
the actual particle density is calculated by taking the mean of the two measurements with a difference
below the threshold value. A possible reason of these inconsistent measurements is the lower accuracy
of the scales used for the considered samples: at the moment of testing of the two mentioned soil
samples, there was indeed only a 0.01 g accuracy scale available instead of 0.001 g accuracy scale.
Sample SIS 2022-03 2B showed a relatively high organic content, with even organic content escaping
the pycnometer while in the vacuum chamber as discussed in Section 4.1.5. This event caused
probably the significant deviations in the results as shown in Table 4.7.

In general, the soil particle densities in function of depth are approximately constant, with a mean
value roughly around 2.7 g/cm3. However, for the particle density of sample S8 of borehole SIS2022-
03, which is taken from a depth of approximately 170 cm below the active layer, the particle
density is approaching even 2.8 g/cm3. If the soil particle densities are compared with the particle
density of 2.73 g/cm3 reported in a technical report regarding silty and sandy clay samples from the
Ilulissat area [76], it can be concluded that the obtained values are realistic values for silty clay, from
Western-Greenland.
4.2.4
Atterberg limits
The Atterberg limits, together with the plasticity and consistency index, are determined according to
ISO 17892-12:2004 [69] as explained in Section 4.1.7. There were only measurements performed on
samples of SIS2022-03 due to time restrictions.
The obtained values are according to Ingeman-Nielsen et al. [59] in the same order of magnitude for
lean clays, as during the field work on Storsletten in Ilulissat, Western-Greenland, generally a liquid
limit of 30 % and a plasticity index of 15 % was encountered. Here the plasticity index proves to be
a bit lower, which is probably due to a larger silt or sand content in some of the samples.
Soil sample 1C, closest to the surface, shows a relatively narrow water content range with plastic
behaviour compared to the other samples. On the other hand, it has the highest plastic and liquid
limit of all tested soil samples, which implies that the soil only starts to behave plastically from a
water content larger than 26.64 %. Liquid behaviour is reached from water contents over 35.67 %.
With the natural water content of the soil sample located above sample 1C being equal to 37 %, it
is clear that the natural conditions are such that in fact the soil is probably behaving as a liquid,
if it would have been in a thawed state. This is also indicated by the negative consistency index.
The only reason this soil sample is ’stable’, is because it is frozen. Indeed, due to the excess ice and
hence a water content larger than the liquid limit, this soil sample would have been liquid until the
excess water flows away.
The other two soil samples show approximately similar liquid and plastic limits, and for both the
natural water content is in the plastic region. However, as the excess amount of ice increased with
depth, and as the natural water content for sample S7B is very close to the liquid limit (consistency
index close to 0), a stable situation of the soil in the deeper soil layers is only existing due to the
frozen state of the soil. As samples from borehole SIS2022-04, and their water content showed, the
vast amount of ice implies that if the soil would thaw, vast deformations would occur. As in this case
a power line mast is present, this poses indeed a serious risk for the stability of the construction, and
with the current ongoing climate change, and the large area with ice rich permafrost, deformations,
and displacements with a change in thermal regime are inevitable in the soil. The reason that the
water content in sample S6C is relatively low compared to other samples and compared to soil
samples in the other borehole furthest away from the mast, i.e. SIS2022-04, is possibly that these
samples were thawed when retrieved from the borehole, as no visible ice was observed and the drilling
was significantly more difficult. Here the excess water seeped from this location during time and
some consolidation might have taken place as well.

4.2.5
Grain size distribution and Unified Soil Classification System
In total, the cumulative grain size distribution of five soil samples was determined with the PARIO
device (or as earlier denoted as the cumulative particle size distribution (PSD)). During the data
processing, the pressure measurements in function of time were fitted with a theoretical curve of the
pressure in time derived from an assumed grain size distribution based on actual sieving results as
shown in Figure 4.25. The optimisation yielded consequently in the eventual cumulative grain size
distribution curves for the investigated soil samples as show in the Figures 4.26, 4.27 and 4.28. The
Root Mean Square Error (RMSE) for each fit of the measured and model pressure is given at the
corresponding figures.
The measurements showed to be very sensitive to temperature differences between the PARIO sensor
and the soil sample suspension exceeding 2 ○C. This is the reason for a failed PARIO measurement of
sample S1C and S2 of borehole SIS2022-04 as shown in Figure 4.28. Indeed, the sample suspension
was already sieved, when the error in the pressure measurements was discovered due to the so called
’temperature drift’, i.e. an excessively large temperature difference between sensor and suspension.
For future use of the PARIO device, which resulted in all other cases in continuous and accurate
cumulative grain size distribution curves, it is strongly recommended to always check the temperature
drift warnings and ensure in the first place that the temperature of the pressure sensor and soil
sample suspension is practically identical.
The resulting cumulative grain size distribution curve for sample S4 from borehole SIS2022-03 is
depicted in Figure 4.26 (a). This sample is retrieved at a depth between 80 - 90 cm below the
active layer and shows a significant silt fraction of approximately 85 %. The silt fraction shows to
be well graded, as the curve shows that every particle diameter in the silt fraction is present in the
investigated soil sample.
Soil sample S5, from the same borehole but at a depth between 90 - 106 cm, shows an increased
clay fraction compared to the previous soil sample as depicted in the grain size distribution graph
in Figure 4.26 (b). In this case, there is also a sand fraction observed of approximately 10 %, and
the clay fraction is slightly more than 50 %. Note that unlike for the previous soil sample, the silt
fraction is not as well graded, as soil particles with a diameter in the range of 0.063 - 0.04 mm are
missing in the soil sample.
If the next two soil samples are considered in Figure 4.27 from borehole SIS2022-03, it seems that
the clay fraction decreases in soil sample S6-S7 in a depth range of 106 - 164 cm. The clay content
reaches approximately 30 %, while the silt fraction accounts again for practically 70 % of the soil
sample and is very well graded. Moreover, the sand fraction is still present but with a much lower
fraction than soil sample S5. The deepest soil sample, S8, from the depth range of 164 - 189 cm,
clearly shows the highest clay content of all samples, approximately 65 %. The silt content is also
still significant, with approximately 30 % of the soil sample. In this case, the silt fraction is poorly
graded, as soil particles with a diameter in the range of 0.013 - 0.0063 are missing in the sample.
The sand fraction for S8 is similar as for S6-S7.
124
==PAGE:150==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
Figure 4.25: The measured and fitted pressure, in function of time for the PARIO measurement of
soil sample S4 of borehole SIS2022-03. The temperature drift phenomenon is also shown, which is in
this case in an acceptable range.
In general, our visual observations of borehole SIS2022-03 regarding the soil type is confirmed by the
cumulative grain size distribution curves. The clay content seems to increase with depth, while in
the upper soil layers there is more silt present.
For borehole SIS2022-04, there is only one cumulative grain size distribution curve composed as
shown in Figure 4.28. Due to excessive temperature drift, the PARIO measurements could not be
used to compose a complete and continuous curve for the soil fraction with diameter lower than
0.063 mm. Therefore, only the sieving data is available for discussion.
In the upper soil layers, in the depth range of 0 - 50 cm, there is a well-graded sand fraction of
approximately 30 % and a gravel fraction of just more than 10 %. The silt and clay fraction are also
still accounting for a fraction of just more than 60 % of the soil sample.
The Unified Soil Classification System has been developed by Casagrande and relies in the first
place on the Casagrande diagram, which is a liquid limit - plasticity index space with different zones
identified depending on the soil type as shown in Figure A.14 in the Appendix. In other words, the
values of the liquid limit and plasticity index are one of the soil characteristics used by Casagrande
to classify soils. As the plastic behaviour does not give a definite answer regarding the soil type,
characteristics of the cumulative grain size distribution curve are used as well. Two widely used
coefficients, describing the shape of the cumulative grain size distribution curve are the coefficient of
curvature Cc and the coefficient of uniformity Cu. Their expressions are given in Equation 4.26 and
4.27 respectively [21].
Cc = (D30)2
D10D60

Cu = D60

to respectively 10 %, 30 % and 60 % of the complete soil sample.

The coefficient of uniformity gives information about the range of present grain diameters, with a
value below 4 indicating that the soil sample is uniform, i.e. poorly graded as only a limited amount
of grain sizes are present in the soil [21]. Values larger than 4 indicate a well graded sample, with a
wide variety of grain sizes present in the soil sample.
The coefficient of curvature is another coefficient which can be used to assess if a soil sample is
well or poorly graded. It gives a better insight in the shape of the curve, where a well graded soil
sample yields in values greater than 1, while a poorly graded soil yields in values lower than 1 [21].
The values of the two coefficients are summarised in Table 4.9 and confirm that soil sample S4 and
S6+S7 are well graded, while the others are also well graded based on Cu, but the value of Cc is
able to identify the kink the curve in the silt fraction.

At this point, all the required information about the soil samples is known, and the soil classification
according the USCS is carried out as follows.

SIS2022-03 S4
Based on Figure 4.26 (a), almost 100 % of the soil sample is passing the 75 µm,
which is clearly more than 50 % as specified by the norm. Hence, the soil is certainly a fine-grained
soil, which is also inorganic based on a low organic content (LOI = 0.93 %). Next, as the liquid
limit is assumed to be equal to approximately 35 % and the plasticity index equal to approximately
9 (based on soil sample S1C), it is clear that sample S4 falls below the A line in the Casagrande
diagram in Figure A.14 in the Appendix. Hence, S4 is classified as an Inorganic Silt.
SIS2022-03 S5
Based on Figure 4.26 (b), there is again clearly more than 50 % of the soil sample passing through
the 75 µm sieve, indicating its classification as a fine-grained soil. The liquid limit is also below 50
%, as in this case the Atterberg limits of sample S6C are assumed to be valid as well for sample
S5. The soil is assumed to be inorganic, due to a LOI equal to 1.09 %. Based on the Casagrande
diagram in Figure A.14, a liquid limit of approximately 30 %, and a plasticity index of approximately
13 %, this soil sample ends up above the A line in the Casagrande diagram, which implies that this
sample is a clay. As the fraction on the 75 µm sieve is less than 15 %, this soil sample is classified
as a Lean Clay.
SIS2022-03 S6+S7
Figure 4.27 (a) proves that there is practically 100 % passing the 75 µm sieve, and based on the LOI
equal to approximately 1 %, this soil sample is also consisting of an inorganic fine-grained soil. With
a liquid limit of approximately 30 % and a plasticity index of approximately 13 %, this soil sample is
a clay. As the fraction on the 75 µm sieve is less than 15 %, this soil sample is also classified as a
Lean Clay, with even a smaller fraction of sand compared to sample S5.
SIS2022-03 S8
The cumulative grain size distribution curve depicted in Figure 4.27 (b), and an organic content
below 1 % imply that this soil sample is also consisting of an inorganic, fine-grained soil. A liquid
limit of approximately 29 %, and a plasticity index of approximately 11 % classify this soil based on
the Casagrande diagram as a clay. The fraction of approximately 3 % on the 75 µm sieve yield in a
final classification of a Lean Clay.
SIS2022-04 S1+S2
The incomplete cumulative grain size distribution curve depicted in Figure 4.28, indicates that again
more than 50 % is retrieved on the 75 µm sieve. As for this borehole no Atterberg limits have been
determined, it is assumed based on visual similarities that the Atterberg limits of sample S1C of
borehole SIS2022-03 are representative for this soil sample. Therefore, the liquid limit is assumed to
be approximately equal to 36 %, and the plasticity index approximately equal to 9 %. This implies
that this soil sample consists of silt, and as the fraction remaining on the 75 µm sieve is larger than
30 %, the sand fraction is larger than the gravel fraction, and the gravel fraction is lower than 15 %,
this soil sample is classified as a Sandy Silt.
Note that as all the soil samples were in fact fine-grained soils, both the coefficient of uniformity and
curvature where not needed to classify the soil samples.
4.2.6
Unfrozen water content
The first step in the processing of the permittivity measurements, is the calibration of the sensor
by using a calibration plot as depicted in Figure 4.29. The values of the real part of the dielectric

permittivity for water, air, and 99.9 % ethanol, as reported in literature (Section 4.1.8) are equal to
respectively 79.73 at a temperature of 21.25 ○C, 1.0006, and 25.05 at a temperature of 21.48 ○C. This
calibration plot shows what the required correction on the measured real dielectric permittivities is.
As by mistake, tap water was measured during the calibration measurements instead of demineralised
water, it is opted to not use this data point in the calibration plot for the determination of the
correction due to the variability of the mineral composition of tap water and the lack of sufficient
literature about the dielectric permittivity of tap water.
Even though the data point for water is not taken into account (measured tap water - theoretical
value of demineralised water), Figure 4.29 shows that the trend line based on air and ethanol follows
the deviation of the data point for water anyway. Hence, the required correction on the measured

During the lab work, the permittivity of a soil sample from borehole SIS2022-03 and SIS2022-04 was

contact between the tines and the soil sample. Therefore, in what follows, only the measurements of
sample S1B of borehole SIS2022-04, i.e. the borehole furthest away from the mast, are discussed.
Before a temperature correction is performed according to Equation 2.58, the measured real permit-
tivities are first corrected according to Equation 4.28. The effect of each correction is shown in Figure
4.30, together with the combined correction. The temperature and calibration correction result for
the real permittivities in subzero temperatures in a similar decrease of the measured value, while in
positive temperatures the calibration correction increases the value, and the temperature correction
decreases the value of the measured permittivity. The combined correction results in a slight increase
of the permittivity as the maximal soil temperature is approached, while the permittivity in the
subzero temperature range is decreased in value.

==PAGE:156==

As at this point, thanks to the calibration and temperature corrections, it is assumed that the real
permittivity data set corresponds to the actual value as it was in-situ in the soil sample, it is possible
to proceed to the determination of the volumetric water content as a function of temperature.
Immediately at the end of the permittivity measurements, two soil subsamples have been taken right
around the tines to determine the water content of the soil directly around the tines. This water
content will allow to scale the curve of the volumetric water content as a function of temperature
such that it coincides with the measured water content at the end of the test.
Figure 4.31 depicts the volumetric water content obtained by using the proposed equation by
Ingeman-Nielsen et al. [45] (see Equation 2.57) which is indicated with ’IN’, and by using some
equations proposed by Bellingham [22] (see Table 2.1) which are indicated with ’B’. Instead of using
coefficients proposed by Ingeman-Nielsen et al. or Bellingham, the coefficients of the same type of
equation as used by Ingeman-Nielsen et al. were optimised according to the measurements. This
optimisation of fitting the measured data yields in the following equation:
wvol = 0.115735 ⋅√εr,TC −0.1
(4.29)
Figure 4.30: Original measurements of the real permittivity as a function of the temperature, together
with temperature and calibration corrections.
The resulting curve of Equation 4.29 showed a good fit of the final volumetric water content in
the positive temperature range, however, it is clear that the unfrozen water content in the subzero
temperature range seems to be overestimated in comparison with the other equations. Especially when
keeping in mind that the upper soil layers consist of soil with a significant silt content, the deviating
behaviour of the own fit does not seem realistic compared to the expressions used by Bellingham and
Ingeman-Nielsen et al. Therefore, it is opted not to use an own fit of the data, but instead it is opted
to scale an existing equation such that it matches with the measured final volumetric water content.
Both the Silt Clay equation of Bellingham and the equation of Ingeman-Nielsen et al. were scaled to
fit the final volumetric water content. The only significant difference between both scaled equations
is in the subzero temperature range, and as Ingeman-Nielsen et al. calibrated their equation based
on West-Greenlandic, silty clay samples, it is decided to use this scaled data set for further use.
131
==PAGE:157==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
As it is known that hysteresis can occur in the relation of the unfrozen water content as a function
of the temperature, the permittivity has also been measured while refreezing the soil sample in order
to measure the extent of this hysteresis.
This hysteresis occurs mainly due to the following two phenomena: (i) the super-cooling effect and
(ii) the ink bottle effect of the pore bottleneck behaviour [77]. The super-cooling effect of water
implies that water does not turn instantaneously into ice when the temperature drops below its
freezing point. Some required conditions for super-cooling to occur, is that water needs to be cooled
slowly, and that there are no nucleation sites for crystallization to begin [78]. These conditions are
often fulfilled when soil cools slowly down during the freezing season. These conditions are not met
during the thawing season, resulting in the absence of super-cooled fluid in the soil. Consequently,
the unfrozen water content is lower at the same temperature during thawing compared to the freezing
cycle. The second reason for hysteresis to occur, is the ink bottle effect of the pores [77, 79]. The
ink-bottle effect is mainly due to the smaller size of pore entries compared to the size of the pore
bodies. This will cause a bottle neck to appear during the phase change of water to ice, as the
already formed ice blocks the smaller pore entries and water gets trapped and remains liquid. The
thawing is again less affected [79].
Figure 4.31: Volumetric water content as a function of temperature, for several empirical relations.
The eventual used relation for further calculations is indicated as ’IN Scaled’.
Figure 4.32 shows the measured hysteresis for sample S1B of SIS2022-04. The data set of the
measured real permittivity when the soil sample was thawed covers a temperature range of [-8.4 ,
16.2] ○C, while the data set of the measured real permittivity when the soil sample was frozen again,
covers a temperature range of [-0.3 , -7.9] ○C. It is clear that there is indeed hysteretic behaviour
observed, with a higher unfrozen water content at a certain temperature when the sample is frozen
compared to the thawing case.
132
==PAGE:158==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
Figure 4.32: Measured real permittivities, after temperature and calibration correction, during both
thawing and freezing of the sample with the hysteretic behaviour clearly visible.
A last step in the processing of the permittivity measurements, is the fitting of the theoretical
expression in Equation 2.62 to the measurements. The optimal fitting is obtained by minimizing the
sum of the absolute error (SAE) by changing the values of a and b from the considered theoretical
expressions, and the use of these expressions results in smooth curves that benefit the numerical
stability of the thermal model in a later stage of the project. The eventual fitted curves are shown
in Figure 4.33 and for the calculations it was assumed that the porosity is equal to the volumetric
water content, which implies that the soil sample is assumed to be fully saturated. The values
of the coefficients a and b for both the thawing and freezing curve are summarised in Table 4.10.
The average of the thawing and freezing theoretical curve is implemented in the thermal model in
TEMP/W, as it is not possible to implement hysteretic behaviour in the used software.
Note that the sudden increase in the measured volumetric water content around 5 ○C is not included
in the theoretical curve of the volumetric water content as a function of the temperature.

4.2.7
Thermal properties
Based on the principles elaborated in Section 2.5.2, and based on the methodology addressed in
Section 4.1.9, this section reports and discusses the obtained thermal conductivity and volumetric
heat capacity of the investigated soil sample SIS2022-03 S6C. There have been tests performed on
dry and saturated glass bead samples as well, in order to verify if the measuring methodology results
in trustworthy results as both the thermal conductivity and volumetric heat capacity of glass, air
and water are assumed to be known and specified with a sufficient accuracy in literature. However,
according to the results, the theoretical value of especially the thermal conductivity of the glass beads
cannot be derived based on the discussed theoretical background from the measured bulk thermal
conductivity. Therefore, the possibility that an inappropriate model is used for the computation of
the effective thermal conductivity that does not correspond with reality is also investigated at the
end of this section.
Measurements and results
Firstly, Figure 4.34 (a) depicts the measurements on dry glass beads with the SH sensor. One
measurement is defined as a cycle, which starts with the emission of a heat pulse for six seconds
and of the measurements of the temperature in function of time at the receiving needle as shown
in Figure 4.16. One cycle lasts for one hour and it is important that a sufficient number of cycles
are measured to ensure that eventually enough cycles are available at approximately the same initial
temperature. The selected cycles for further calculations, which are indicated with green, are chosen
accordingly.
Figure 4.34 (b) depicts the averaged curve of the temperature differences with respect to the mean
initial temperature of the selected cycles. The deviation at each data point of the averaged curve is
also shown, and this deviation should be as minimal as possible to ensure that a stable data set is
134
==PAGE:160==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
chosen with low uncertainties for all the data points. In this case, as will be discussed later on, there
is certainly a stable data set measured and used for further calculations. Lastly, the average power of
the heat flux is also shown in the latter graph, and has an average value of approximately 100 W/m.
Next, the optimisation of the fit of the measured temperature differences with the analytical expression
of the temperature difference as discussed in Section 2.5.2 is performed. Note that in the proposed
code by Ingeman-Nielsen et al. [48], there is an extra term bt added to the general solution as shown
in Equation 4.30. This extra term improves the numerical stability of the optimisation process.
∆T =
q
4πλt ⋅exp(−r2
4κt) + bt
(4.30)
with κ being the thermal diffusivity, t being the time, q being the power of the heat pulse in [J/m]
and r being the distance between receiving and emitting needle of the sensor. b should be around
zero in order to have a realistic result.

Regarding the wet glass bead sample, which was created by simply pouring water into the dry glass
bead sample, as carefully as possible until the water level was exactly the same as the height of the
glass beads. The water as poured very carefully, such that on impact the glass beads on the surface
barely moved. The inside of the cylindrical container, reaching above the glass beads was dried with
paper towel.

at DTU, and only once the data was being processed in Trondheim a couple weeks later, the error of
not having a sufficiently long measurement for reaching stable initial temperatures was discovered.
On site, only the measurements of the dry glass beads were verified. However, the data is still useful,
with a slightly larger uncertainty, as the temperature variation is limited to 0.6 ○C and as in the end
not the absolute temperature, but the temperature differences are considered.

Before the actual results are discussed, it is important to point out that the use of the Specific Heat
sensor for the described application does not require any calibration opposed to the unfrozen water
measurements as discussed in Section 4.2.6. This is due to the fact that the actually measured
temperature is physically directly related to the thermal properties. The only aspect that could
have been calibrated, is the third order polynomial that is used in the software of the sensor, which
relates the measured voltage with the temperature. This relation can be calibrated by using another
temperature sensors to verify this relation. However, there was no other temperature sensor available
during the tests, and the calibration performed by the manufacturer and previous users is assumed to
be accurate enough, such that only a sensitivity analysis will be conducted for this project.
The fitting of the measurements with the analytical expression, resulted in thermal properties for the
glass beads as shown in Table 4.11. It is immediately clear that there is significant deviation between
the reported thermal conductivity λ by the manufacturer and the obtained thermal conductivities by
this methodology both by Jakob Nielsen and myself. The presented results used the geometrical mean
relation (see Equation 4.22) between the thermal conductivity of water, air and soil to obtain the
(measured) bulk thermal conductivity. The significant deviation is especially true for the measurements
for dry glass beads. The wet glass bead measurements result in a thermal conductivity much closer
to the reported theoretical value, this is again the case for both Jakob Nielsen and myself.
The volumetric heat capacities cv are for both Jakob Nielsen and myself again similar regarding
value, but underestimate heavily the reported values by the manufacturer. Before going into further
detail regarding cv, the focus in the last part of this section will be on the deviation of the thermal
conductivity, and further investigations regarding the volumetric heat capacity is suggested for future
research and projects.

Sensitivity
The sensitivity analysis considers the influence on the computed bulk thermal properties with Equation
4.30 due to variations on the values of (i) the distance between the needles of the SH sensor, (ii)
the average value of the power emitted by the heat pulse, and (iii) the initial temperature when the
heat pulse is emitted. The used data set in the sensitivity analysis is the measurements of the dry
glass beads, as the used measurements have been recorded at a quasi-constant initial temperature.
To investigate the change in the initial temperature, two approaches have been used. The first
approach is using one cycle instead of averaging several cycles as input in the analytical inversion
algorithm. The second approach is using a certain factor, which increases or decreases the measured
temperature differences.
Firstly, the effect of the distance between the needles is very important, as previous studies have
shown that the measurements and eventual resulting thermal properties are very sensitive to a change
of distance between the needles of the sensor [18]. The sensitivity analysis regarding the distance
between the needles is depicted graphically in Figure 4.37. The eventual value of the volumetric
heat capacity seems to be influenced heavily by a changing distance between the needles as shown in
Figure 4.37 (a), with an increase in volumetric heat capacity of approximately 40 % if the distance
decreases from 6 mm to 5 mm, and with a decrease of approximately 25 % when the distance
increases from 6 mm to 7 mm.
The sensitivity of the thermal conductivity is depicted in Figure 4.37 and shows a significantly less
sensitivity on the change of the distance between the needles. In this case, the thermal conductivity
increases with approximately 0.1 % when the distance decreases from 6 mm to 5 mm. If the distance
is increased from 6 mm to 7 mm, the thermal conductivity decreases with a similar percentage is in
the other case.
It is clear that only the volumetric heat capacity is heavily sensitive to changes in the distance
between the needles, so it is crucial to ensure that the used distance between the needles is according
to the input value in the optimising algorithm.
The sensitivity of the thermal properties on changes of the value of the emitted power is depicted in
Figure 4.38. It is clear, that both considered thermal properties have a similar relationship in function
of the power and are both sensitive to the considered variable. Note however, that the scale of the
vertical axis is in this case different as in Figure 4.37. Indeed, both the volumetric heat capacity
and the thermal conductivity change with approximately 0.3 % per J/m, which is significantly less
sensitive compared to the sensitivity of the volumetric heat capacity with the distance between the
needles. Hence, it is concluded that the emitted power has a relatively low influence on the resulting
thermal properties.
138
==PAGE:164==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.37: Sensitivity analysis of thermal parameters: effect of distance between emitting and
receiving needle.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.38: Sensitivity analysis of thermal parameters: effect of the change in power of the heating
needle.
The last variable that is considered in the sensitivity analysis, is the effect of the initial temperature.
There is only a limited range of initial temperatures used, as actually measured cycles are processed in
this analysis. The volumetric heat capacity seems to vary linearly, as shown in Figure 4.39 (a), with
an average change of 36 % per ○C if the relationship is extrapolated. This indicates however that an
insignificant change in the initial temperature might result in severe changes in the volumetric heat
capacity.
The variation of the thermal conductivity as a function of the initial temperature, is not as linear as
for the heat capacity. If the slope between the two extreme points in the range is extrapolated, a
change of 34 % per ○C is observed. This indicates again that an insignificant change in the initial
temperature might alter the bulk thermal conductivity significantly.
However, as only a limited range of initial temperatures is considered, and as in general only
measurements at a quasi-equal initial temperature should be used, the relatively low effect on the
139
==PAGE:165==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
values of the considered thermal properties in this limited temperature interval is considered being
acceptable. To ensure a low effect due to variations in the initial temperature, the measurements
should be performed as long as a stable initial temperature is reached.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.39: Sensitivity analysis of thermal parameters: effect of the initial temperature of the
Specific Heat measurement.
The effect of the change of the temperature difference with respect to the initial temperature is
considered next in Figure 4.40. This analysis is performed, in order to investigate what the effect
is when the used thermistor gives a complete false temperature reading at one point, and that
consequently the value of the temperature difference is false. It is immediately clear that severe
deviations in the temperature difference influence both considered thermal parameters severely. As
the general practice is to average several cycles, measured at a quasi-constant initial temperature,
theses severe deviations are assumed to be not common.
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.40: Sensitivity analysis of thermal parameters: effect of change in temperature difference
∆T.
However, even for a decrease of 10 % of the temperature difference, there is already a change of
140
==PAGE:166==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
approximately 10 % for both the volumetric heat capacity and the thermal conductivity. Hence,
deviations in the temperature difference are certainly affecting the thermal properties and extra care
should be taken in selecting the used measurements.
Model for calculating the bulk thermal conductivity
As already mentioned, the suspicion exists that the geometrical mean model (see Equation 4.22)
does not represent the actual reality correctly, such that the resulting thermal conductivity of the
glass beads based on the measurement of the bulk thermal conductivity is deviating from the real (or
the reported) value. The geometric mean model is assumed to give correct results for most soils,
but as discussed in Section 4.2.5, natural soils generally exist of particles with sizes in a wide range.
The dimensions of the used glass beads are limited to a relatively narrow interval, such that the
geometrical structure of a sample built from glass beads is fundamentally different than a silty clay.
This also implies that the deviating results for the glass beads do not mean immediately that the
derived parameters for the natural silty clay are wrong. However, in future research, the glass beads
might be a better reference for sands, as sand particles have more similarities with glass beads than
clay and silt particles.
In what follows, the effect of the used model on the eventual resulting thermal conductivity of the
glass beads is investigated. The considered models which couple the bulk thermal conductivity with
the conductivity of each constituent in the multi-phased medium, both for the dry and wet glass
bead samples, are summarised below.
Firstly, the formula proposed by De Vries in 1963 [82] is considered, as this formulation has been
successfully used before for wet soils [83]. This theory is based on the method of Euken which was
originally designed to calculate the electrical conductivity. As the thermal and electrical conductivity
represent similar physical processes, the used models can be used equivalently for both cases. Euken’s
method assumes that the particle is immersed completely in a continuous medium [82], which
translates for soils that the continuous medium can be a combination of air and water. De Vries
proposed the following formulation to obtain the thermal conductivity in soils [82]:
λ = Fsxsλs + Faxaλa + Fwxwλw + Fixiλi
Fsxs + Faxa + Fwxw + Fixi
(4.31)
with
Fj = 1

−1
n = a, b, c
j = s, a, w, i
(4.32)
and
ga + gb + gc = 1
(4.33)
gn is a shape factor and for spherical particles, such as glass beads, the three shape factors are equal
to 1
3.
Another option, is the relatively simple formula that considers small cubic dispersions in the continuous
phase, proposed by Pande in 1984 [84]. This formula is only valid for a two-phase system and is
expressed as:
λ = λs (1 −1.545n(2/3))
(4.34)
with n being the porosity, λ the bulk thermal conductivity, and lambdas the thermal conductivity of
the solid particles in the porous medium, which is for this analysis the thermal conductivity of glass
beads.
141
==PAGE:167==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
Next, a formulation for the thermal conductivity is discussed which assumes that the particles, both in
size as in geometrical placing, are positioned completely randomly in the volume [44]. The weighting
between the thermal conductivities presents in the considered porous material is performed by the
square root mean, which is believed to have a greater physical basis than the geometric mean. This
method was proposed by Roy et al. in 1981 and can be expressed as:
λ = (xs

√
λw + xi

(4.35)
with λw being the thermal conductivity of water, assumed to be equal to 0.599 W/mK [85], λi the

the space filled with solids, xw the volumetric fraction of the pore space filled with fluid and xi the
volumetric fraction filled with ice. These fractions are related as:
xs = 1 −n,
xw = n ⋅ϕ,
xi = n −xw
(4.36)
with n being the porosity, and ϕ the unfrozen water content, normalised with respect to the porosity.
In the random formulation in Equation 4.35, fully saturated conditions are assumed, and no air is
considered in the first place. However, the same principle can be used to obtain a formulation for dry
conditions with the absence of both ice and water:

the thermal conductivity of air assumed to be equal to 0.026 W/mK [85].

upper and lower boundary for the bulk thermal conductivity [82]. Note that here the air fraction is
also taken into account.

can be expressed as follows:

Lastly, Landauer’s relation is discussed, which is valid for materials with a porosity between 15 % and
65 % [86]. In this porosity range, the connectivity of the pore phase leads to a stronger dependence
of the thermal conductivity on the pore volume fraction, which is interesting to consider when glass
beads are investigated, which form a homogeneous mixture and as the pores are not filled with
smaller sized particles such as with silty clays. Landauer’s relations is expressed as follows:
λ = 1
4 [λp (3n −1) + λs (2 −3n) + {[λp (3n −1) + λs (2 −3n)]2 + 8λsλp}
1/2]
(4.43)
with λp the thermal conductivity of the pore in the porous material, which is equal to λa in the dry
case, and equal to λw in the fully saturated case.
It is of course still possible that the actually measured bulk thermal conductivity is false. Therefore,
other glass bead experiments in literature are considered into further detail. Tiefenbacher et al.
published in 2016 a study regarding the influence of the probe geometry on measured bulk thermal
conductivities [87] and the reported bulk thermal conductivities of glass beads, with a very similar
diameter, are of the order of magnitude 0.19 W/mK. The used glass beads are also produced from
soda lime glass, similarly as for the used glass beads in our experiments.
Hütter reports in her PhD dissertation in 2011 [85] similar ranges for the bulk thermal conductivity of
soda lime glass beads. However, she used for the thermal conductivity of glass a value of 1 W/mK,
which would fit much better with our result of the saturated glass bead sample as shown in Table
4.11.
By keeping the thermal conductivity of glass, i.e. λs, a variable in the earlier discussed formulations
for the bulk thermal conductivity, it is possible to assess how the bulk thermal conductivity changes
in function of λs. In the first analysis where the dry glass bead sample is considered, the effect of the
uncertainty of the porosity and the thermal conductivity of air is also assessed as shown in Figure
4.41.
Based on the presented graphs it is immediately clear that the geometrical mean model underestimates
the theoretical value of glass severely. The Maxwell and De Vries models, that coincide for a two-
phased medium, give values for the thermal conductivity of glass beads that approach the theoretical
value. The assumed value of the conductivity of air does not influence the eventual results significantly
for the majority of the considered models, except for the Maxwell and De Vries case. It is quite
striking that a slightly lower porosity, would result exactly in the reported thermal conductivity for
the glass by the manufacturer, if the models of Maxwell and De Vries are used as shown in Figure
4.41 (c). So, the deviation by the Maxwell and De Vries model with parameter values for n and λa
in the reference case is probably due to uncertainties on the used values of the porosity and the
thermal conductivity of air.
Hütter discusses in 2012 that bad contact between thermistor - glass bead, and sensor - glass bead
might also influence the measurements. However, the bulk thermal conductivity that has been
measured is in the same order of magnitude as both in the PhD of Hütter [85] and as reported by
Tiefenbacher et al [87]. So, it is very likely that the deviation of the thermal conductivity of the
glass beads from the reported value by the manufacturer is due to a wrong assumption of the model
that links the bulk property with the properties of the constituents of the medium.

The measured bulk thermal conductivity for the other considered case, with a saturated glass bead
sample, equals approximately 0.837 W/mK. The resulting curves for the bulk thermal conductivity in
function of λs are shown in Figure 4.42, which is representing the reference case for the wet glass
bead sample, as the value for the porosity is equal to the actually computed value, and the values for
the thermal conductivity for both air and water are equal to the recommended values from literature.
Furthermore, fully saturated conditions are assumed in the reference case as well.
A first observation is that the variation of the value of the thermal conductivity of glass, around
the measured bulk thermal conductivity is very limited, indicating that practically all models yield
in similar values for λs. This explains also why the geometrical mean model gives a value closer to
reality in the saturated case, while in the dry case the value is questionable. To investigate which
model is the most accurate representation of reality when saturated conditions are considered, a
sensitivity analysis is performed as shown in the next four graphs.

Figure 4.43 (a) shows the effect of a lower value of the thermal conductivity of water (0.5 W/mK
instead of 0.599 W/mK in the reference case). This graph suggests that a lower value of the thermal
conductivity of the medium filling the pores would result in practically the thermal conductivity as
specified by the manufacturer.
Figure 4.43 (b) on the other hand, implies that a larger porosity than the reference case does not
affect the results significantly. This observation can again be explained by stating that the pore
medium should have a smaller thermal conductivity.
145
==PAGE:171==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
(a)
(b)
Figure 4.44: Change of saturation
Another way of decreasing the thermal conductivity of the pore volume, is by not assuming fully
saturated conditions. Indeed, as air has a significantly lower thermal conductivity compared to water,
even a couple percents of air that is present in the pores can reduce the thermal conductivity of
the pore volume significantly. This is illustrated by the graphs in Figure 4.44, where the expression
of De Vries yields exactly in the thermal conductivity reported by the manufacturer when air is
filling 20 % of the pore space. On the other hand, the geometrical mean formulation yields in the
thermal conductivity of glass as specified by the manufacturer if air fills the pore volume for only 8
%. Note that in the last considered case, the expression of De Vries is just slightly underestimating
the manufacturer specified value for the thermal conductivity.
In conclusion, it is valid to state that the formulation of De Vries seems to result in the most realistic
values, especially when the assumption of fully saturated state is omitted. In further research and
projects, the effect of air in saturated samples, and which model is the most accurate for sandy soils
and glass beads is certainly worth investigating in further detail. The geometrical mean model shows
to be especially inaccurate in the dry state, and yields as many other models, closer to the theoretical
value in the wet case. However, De Vries gives the best performance in both dry and saturated cases,
and hence this model is assumed to represent reality best for a sample composed out of glass beads.
Thermal properties in function of temperature
In this section, the resulting relations between temperature and both thermal conductivity and
volumetric heat capacity are discussed in further detail. These resulting relations are based on specific
heat measurements and permittivity measurements. The relation between temperature and thermal
conductivity is obtained by using the expression in Equation 4.22, where the (unfrozen) water content
is linked with the thermal conductivity. As the unfrozen water content was measured during the
permittivity measurements, the thermal conductivity is consequently also known in function of the
temperature. A similar approach is used for the frozen volumetric heat capacity, where Equation
4.25 is used to obtain a relation as a function of temperature. Note that there is only a temperature
dependency in the sub-zero temperature range, and that the unfrozen volumetric heat capacity is
assumed to be constant and is computed based on the final water content in the positive temperature
range by using Equation 4.25.
Figure 4.45 gives the curves of the bulk thermal conductivity of our soil sample, as a function of
temperature. The actually measured bulk thermal conductivity is also given, and the unfrozen thermal
146
==PAGE:172==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
conductivity in the curves is relatively close to the measured value. In the thermal model, the average
of the thaw and freeze graph of the theoretical and smooth curves is used.
Figure 4.45: Bulk thermal conductivity of the measured soil sample SIS2022-03 S6C in function
of temperature. The curves with subscript ’meas’ indicate that the water content is based on the
empirical relation in Equation 2.57, and ’theo’ indicates that the water content expression as in
Equation 2.61 is fitted to the measured water content. The actually measured (unfrozen) bulk
thermal conductivity is also added to the graph.
The measured relation between the bulk thermal conductivity and the temperature is compared with
theoretical relations as described in Andersland and Ladanyi for Silty Clays and lean Clays [4]. Figure
4.46 (a) represents a collection of similar curves, which are computed with an assumed unfrozen
water content as a function of the temperature that is computed by using Equation 2.42 with values
for α and β as reported by Andersland and Ladanyi for Suffield Silty Clay [65]. The effect of a change
in the unfrozen and frozen thermal conductivity for the soil particles (λs) is shown, and there is a
general good correspondence between the theoretical curves for silty clay as reported by Andersland
and Ladanyi and our own measurements.
The effect of the assumed unfrozen water content equation, for different values for α and β in
Equation 2.42, is shown in Figure 4.46 (b). It is clear, that there is a good correspondence of our
obtained between relation and both the Suffield Silty Clay and the Inuvik Clay. The transition at 0
○C is in our measured case however not as smooth as for the reported curves by Andersland and
Ladanyi.
Lastly, the obtained curve for the volumetric heat capacity in function of the temperature is shown
in Figure 4.47. In this case, the obtained values for the unfrozen and frozen heat capacity are in
the expected range, however, the unfrozen heat capacity that yields from the calculations for the
curves, does not correspond with the measured bulk capacity with the Specific Heat sensor set-up.
The deviation might be due to measuring error or also due to a wrongly assumed model as discussed
before.
It must be noted as well, that the unfrozen water content was measured in a soil sample with a
higher silt content compared to the soil sample where the thermal properties have been measured.
This can also cause the deviation in the obtained thermal properties.

4.2.8
Salinity
This data clearly shows that the pore water is not saline at all, as the practical salinity for eventually
resulted in an average value of 0.38 for soil sample S5 retrieved from borehole S5. Comparison with
the practical salinity of seawater, which is equal to 35, confirms this statement. For SIS2022-03 S5,
which is a sample taken at approximately 1 m below surface, there are consistent measurements
available with a sufficiently low standard deviation, such that it can be stated that the measurements
148
==PAGE:174==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
represent the actual situation with respect to the inherent uncertainties of the used method.
The fact that soil sample S5 is not saline, is an extra argument that the soil samples S5 and S6 of
SIS2022-03 were thawed when extracted due to the construction and the mast, and not due to a
saline soil layer.

4.3
Discussion
The laboratory testing of the frozen soil samples provides us with crucial and very useful information
and data. It enables us to characterise the encountered soil at two locations around Mast 9, and to
determine their thermal behaviour. Based on these two boreholes and the ERT profiles, it is possible,
with the needed precautions, to conclude how the soil layer is configured. Figure 4.48 and Figure
4.49 summarise in comprehensive graphs the most important soil characteristics for both boreholes
that were discussed in this chapter.
The results for the soil samples from borehole SIS2022-03 show in generally a much lower ice and
water content in the upper part of the borehole, while the deeper soil samples show a much larger
fraction of ice in the samples. These results confirm our observations from during the fieldwork.
Furthermore, the suspicion that samples 5 and 6 of this borehole might have been thawed and
somehow consolidated during the years, is confirmed by a slightly lower water content and void ratio
than for the soil samples deeper in the borehole. The additional result that the pore water is not
saline, is another argument to state that this soil layer can be thawed due to the construction and
the presence of the mast. Regarding the soil type, it is confirmed that the uppermost layer consists
of a peaty, very organic layer, which is followed by a silty soil. Eventually, with increasing depth, the
silt fraction decreases, and lean clay is found.
The soil samples of SIS2022-04 showed a significant difference in ice content compared to the soil
samples of borehole SIS2022-03. SIS2022-04 is assumed to represent the natural soil conditions, as
it is located 20 m to the West of the mast foundation column. This borehole showed practically
from the surface and downwards a vast ice content in the soil samples. There were no ice lenses
observed anymore, the whole soil sample at certain depths consisted purely out of ice, with some
smaller soil layers in between larger ice volumes. The soil close to the surface looked again quite
silty, and a similar evolution in visual characteristics as for SIS2022-03 was observed with increasing
depth. Unfortunately, the depth of borehole SIS2022-04 did not reach the depth where the assumed
thawed layers were found to double check this observation, however, based on the ERT profile no
thawed layer should be encountered at those depths at the location of borehole SIS2022-04.
149

Based on the two boreholes, it can be confirmed at this stage that the construction and the presence
of the mast altered the soil composition drastically. The reduced ice content and a possible thawed
layer underlain and covered by a frozen layer in July 2022 prove the presence of a changed thermal
regime around the mast. This changed thermal regime, changes on its turn the hydrological situation
and the present vegetation as well.
Due to the loss of the ice content close to the mast, the heat capacity of the soil directly around the
mast decreases as well. This implies that the heat can penetrate more easily downwards and can
be transported more easily laterally, such that the whole process is a clear self-reinforcing process.
150
==PAGE:176==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
Indeed, if every season a certain amount of ice disappears around the mast, the heat transport,
and the radius of affected soil around the mast will gradually increase after each thawing season.
Therefore, adequate, and quickly effective adaptation measures are certainly required in the very
soon future to stop or slow down this process.
