The permittivity measurements resulted in reasonable and realistic temperature curves for the unfrozen
water content, which got confirmed by comparison with literature. However, the determination of
the thermal properties with the Specific Heat sensor proved to be a bit more challenging. The same
151
==PAGE:177==
Chapter 4. Laboratory testing of permafrost soil samples
problems occurred as during the Thesis of Jakob Nielsen, as during the verification tests with glass
beads, the deduced thermal conductivity and the volumetric heat capacity from the measured bulk
thermal properties, did not match the specified value of the manufacturer. Therefore, it is difficult
to quantify the uncertainty on the deduced soil properties, based on the same method. Nevertheless,
the obtained thermal parameters for the investigated soil sample are according to literature in the
correct order of magnitude.
There is also a short study performed, on the effect of the used geometrical model, which couples the
bulk property with the properties of each constituent of the multi-phase material. In this case, this
multi-phase material was the glass - air and glass - water (-air) combination. The study showed that
the widely used geometrical mean method is not the best option when glass beads are considered.
The formula of De Vries proved to result in better values instead, and therefore it is suggested
for further research to perform some more tests to investigate the performance of the De Vries
formulation. Another aspect that is recommended in this case, is to use a sandy soil or a pure sand
as the soil type instead of silts and clays. The structure and grain size distribution of sand is closer
to that of glass beads and therefore a verification of the measuring method with the Specific Heat
sensor would make more sense.
152
==PAGE:178==
Chapter 5
Thermal modelling
In this chapter, several models regarding the thermal regime, both for future and historical climate,
are discussed by using the GeoStudio TEMP/W software. In this project, the modelling is limited
to one dimensional (1D) and two dimensional (2D) axisymmetric models where no hydrological
processes are taken into account. There are several scenarios considered however, including two
adaptations methods: the use of thermosyphons to cool the subsurface and the construction of an
embankment, to pull the frost level up.
First the methodology is discussed, which deals with the collection and processing of the climate
data used for the thermal modelling. Next the actual model set-up is discussed, and this chapter is
concluded with the results and conclusions based on the considered models.
5.1
Methodology
5.1.1
Climate data: ERA5
For the thermal modelling of geotechnical problems, one requires a sufficiently long (historical)
time series of climate data of the region of interest. As Mast 9 is located approximately 30 km
northwards from the DMI weather station in Sisimiut, it is likely that not all the measured parameters
at the weather station will be representative for our region of interest. Furthermore, the installed
temperature sensors on Mast 9 itself will not give a long enough data set in time, and the only
available climatological data is the air temperature which is not sufficient. Therefore, an alternative
climatological data set is required for the thermal modelling.
A very suitable data set is the ERA5 hourly data, which is the fifth generation ECMWF reanalysis for
the global climate and weather for the past eight decades [88], provided by the European Centre for
Medium-Range Weather Forecasts (ECMWF) [89]. Climate reanalysis is a very strong and useful
method in order to obtain reliable and consistent time series of multiple climate variables [90] based
on historical data and models using the laws of physics. The resulting time series are represented
as 3D grids in function of time. More specific for the ERA5 data set, the data is represented on a
horizontal grid with a resolution of 0.25○x 0.25○, covering the whole world, with 37 pressure levels in
vertical direction [90]. At the latitude of Sisimiut, this resolution equals approximately 28 x 11 km2.
Additionally, the ERA5 data set gives also atmospheric parameters on land-surfaces and sea-surfaces.
Pressure levels represent the vertical height where the atmospheric parameters are given by means of
atmospheric pressure. Lower pressure indicates a higher elevation with respect to the land- or sea
surface.
For this project, the air temperature, specific humidity, short-wave radiation, precipitation, wind
velocity and air pressure are extracted from the ERA5 data set and used for further processing. Note
that under precipitation, only liquid precipitation is understood. Hence, snow depth is not reported
by the ERA5 data set and has to be calculated. Furthermore, as the ERA5 data set does not report
153
==PAGE:179==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
the relative humidity, it is opted to use the method proposed by Felipe G. Nievinski (2023) [91] to
obtain the relative humidity in function of time, temperature, pressure, and the available specific
humidity in the ERA5 data set. The method is concisely available as a Matlab function and yielded
in reasonable values, as it is often problematic to convert large data sets of specific humidity to
relative humidity.
The atmospheric data in the ERA5 data set is given on a limited amount of grid points, which
implies that it is very likely that the point of interest for a project does not coincide with any of the
grid points in the data set. This is why the TopoSCALE method is used developed by Fiddes and
Gruber (2014) [92]. This method solves both the issue with the insufficiently low spatial resolution
of gridded climate data for simulations on land and the significant lateral variability of atmospheric
parameters. Indeed, the method is able to downscale coarse-grid climate variables to a fine-scale grid
by using accurate climate models of the atmospheric column and high-resolution digital elevation
models (DEMs). By considering the exact location of the point or region of interest on a DEM, it is
possible to take the effect of surrounding mountains into account as well. Another important factor
which is taken into account in the TopoSCALE method, is the cloud coverage, whose effect was also
clearly observable in our own temperature measurements at Mast 9.
In this project, the Matlab implementation of the TopoSCALE method developed by Aalstad and
Ingeman-Nielsen [93] was applied on ERA5 data for our point of interest, that covers a period from
1959 to 2022. The TopoSCALE method enables the user to specify the amount of grid points that
should be taken into account during the scaling of the coarser gridded data set. In this case there
are 24 grid points considered as shown in Figure 5.1, where also the DEM of the area of interest is
shown. The DEM was retrieved from the Copernicus database [94] and has a resolution of 30 m.
Note that it is assumed that the resolution of 30 m is accurate enough, as Fiddes and Gruber [92]
used a 25m DEM in their example.
Figure 5.1: DEM used for TopoSCALE. Mast 9 is indicated with a blue diamond, while the red dots
represent the used grid points from the ERA5 data set.
The scaled data by the TopoSCALE method is before use in the thermal model verified with both the
available DMI data from the weather station in Sisimiut [1] and our own temperature measurements
at Mast 9. The obtained graphs are shown in Figure 5.2 and are based on daily averages for the
indicated periods. It is clear that for the air temperature our own measurements at Mast 9 are almost
exactly following the scaled ERA5 data, indicating the scaling was performed successfully.

and the DMI weather data for the same weather station is shown in Figure 5.2 (b) and indicates
that the scaled ERA5 data follows the weather station measurements, with some minor deviations.
However, the order of magnitude of the deviations are for our thermal modelling negligible and might
also be due to the uncertainties in the ERA5 data and the lateral variations not account for in the
TopoSCALING method. Similar observations can be made for the comparison of the ERA5 scaled
data and DMI data for the relative humidity and wind velocity. In conclusion, the scaling of the
ERA5 data resulted in a time series of several atmospheric parameters which represent the actual
conditions of our point of interest in a sufficiently accurate and realistic manner.

Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
5.1.2
Future climate scenarios
In this project, a simplified method is used to assess what the thermal regime will be in the future,
based on daily climate projections of some selected climate variables. These variables are reported
in several climate scenarios with data on single levels from the fifth phase of the Coupled Model
Intercomparison Project (CMIP5), available via the Copernicus Climate Change Service [19]. The
climate projections have been computed by using an extensive number of experiments, models and
periods.
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.3: Comparison of ERA5 data and CMIP5 climate data. In (a) the missing scaling of future
climate projects is illustrated, and in (b) the clear mismatch between the two data sets is visible [19].
The used data set contains indeed a collection of climate projection experiments in correspondence
with the Representative Concentration Pathways (RCP). For this project, the RCP 2.6, 6.0 and 8.5
are considered, which provide different pathways of the future climate forcing, covering the period
2006-2100. RCP scenarios are based on radiative forcing, which is the difference between incoming
and outgoing radiation [95]. The amount of incoming and outgoing radiation is closely related to
156
==PAGE:182==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
the concentration of greenhouse gases, such that the radiative forcing in [W/m2] is a practical input
for climate models and a good tool to describe the amount of greenhouse gasses in the atmosphere.
The considered RCP scenarios 2.6, 6 and 8.5, imply consequently that the radiative forcing for each
scenario is respectively equal to 2.6 W/m2, 6.0 W/m2 and 8.5 W/m2. The expected warming of our
climate for the considered climate scenarios is respectively 1.6 ○C, 2.8 ○C and 4.3 ○C [95], and this
without taking the Arctic Amplification into account (!) [31].
It is a known problem that the future climate scenario data sets do not always match with historical
or measured data, as future climate data can be shifted in time. This phenomenon was in our case
also observed. Furthermore, it was not possible to implement the TopoSCALE method on the climate
scenarios in the used CMIP5 data set, such that an average of nine grid points around our point of
interest was taken of the considered atmospheric variables.
For these reasons, there is a significant discrepancy between the ERA5 data, which covers the
historical climate data, and the CMIP5 data set consisting of the three considered RCP scenarios as
illustrated in Figure 5.3 for the air temperature data.
In order to cope with this discrepancy, it is opted to compute the linear trend line of the CMIP5
climate scenario data, and to apply the obtained trend, i.e. the slope of the trend line, onto the
ERA5 data set of the last 20 years. This method allows to create a representative data set for the
future which takes the radiative forcing in a simplified manner into account. This method ensures
furthermore a continuity in time of the data set as well, which is important for a realistic model of
the thermal regime. In this stage of the project, it is opted to only scale the temperature based on
the climate scenarios. Other parameters are simply repeated cyclically in time without applying a
factor based on a trend of climate scenario projections for the considered variable.
The trend lines for the three considered climate scenarios are given in Figure 5.4 for the air temperature.
As expected, the climate scenario with the largest radiative forcing yields in a trend line with the
largest slope, compared to the other scenarios. These slopes are now used to amplify the historical
ERA5 data, and to eventually obtain the future climate projections. As the ERA5 data is hourly, the
slope is reformulated to give the increase of the air temperature per hour that passed beginning from
01-01-2023 00:00:00.
Figure 5.4: Trend lines of the climate scenarios for (a) the temperature and (b) the specific humidity.
Important note to these assumptions is that future extreme events, such as for instance extreme
157
==PAGE:183==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
precipitation or temperature events, are not predicted by applying this simplified method. This is
definitely a significant drawback of this simplified method. However, at this point, this method is the
most feasible method to apply. The temperature is also the main driving factor in thermal regime
modelling, such that changing this climate variable, a non-negligible climate change is automatically
modelled as well, without taking the effect of climate change on precipitation or relative humidity
into account.
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.5: (a) Shows the good match between the original ERA5 data and the scaled ERA5 data
based on the RCP climate scenarios, and (b) illustrates the difference between the considered climate
scenarios later than 2100.
5.1.3
Snow cover and Albedo estimates
The snow depth as a function of time is estimated based on the considered ERA5 data as follows.
Hock [96] reports a very useful summary of a wide range of methods to model possible snow
melt. Her work is consequently used to compose a melt model for this project by using the scaled
ERA5 data which will be representative for the location of Mast 9. As many studies showed a
significant correlation between snow melt and air temperature, and as many other research projects
use temperature-index methods, it is opted to focus here on the latter method as well. Such methods
link empirically air temperature with snow melt and Hock mentions an extended temperature-index
method, where the solar (short-wave) radiation is taken into account as well, which proved to be
quite successful in predicting the melt with a limited amount of input required. Such an extended,
temperature-index model can be expressed as follows:
M =
⎧⎪⎪⎨⎪⎪⎩
fm(Ta −T0) + aR
Ta > T0
0
Ta ≤T0
(5.1)
with M being the melt of snow in for instance mm/hour depending on the unit of the melt factor fm
and the radiation factor a, Ta being the mean temperature over the considered time interval, which
in this example is equal to one hour, T0 a threshold temperature beyond which melt is assumed to
occur, a being a coefficient related to the radiation and R some type of radiation.
Equation 5.1 implies that there is no melt at all, when the air temperature is lower than the threshold
value, and that in the opposite case the melt is influenced exclusively by the air temperature and the
short-wave radiation.
158
==PAGE:184==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
An example of a good functioning model of the type discussed above, is according to Hock the
model created by Kane et al. in 1997 [26]. This model is of particular interest in our case, as it only
requires downward short-wave radiation and air temperatures as input to determine the snow melt.
Their combined Temperature and Radiation Index Method can be expressed as follows:
M =
⎧⎪⎪⎨⎪⎪⎩
Ct(Ta −T0) + Cr ∗(1 −αsnow)Qsi
Ta > T0
0
Ta ≤T0
(5.2)
with Ct being the melt factor with respect to the temperature, Cr a melt factor with respect to the
radiation, αsnow the snow albedo and Qsi the downward shortwave radiation. Kane et al. performed
optimisation calculations on the coefficients shown in Equation 5.2 based on snow depth measurements
during the period of 1987 - 1990 in Imnavalt Creek, Alaska. The snow depth calculations based on
the available scaled ERA5 data are based on the coefficient values presented in the mentioned paper
and are shown in Table 5.1. Note that the calibrated coefficient set for the year 1990 is used, as
they fit according to Kane et al. the measured snow depth best.
Table 5.1: Calibrated coefficient values for the snow melt model of Kane et al. [26] based on snow
depths from Imnavalt Creek, Alaska.
Unit
Value
Year
-
1990
Ct
mm
h○C
0.075
Cr
mm⋅m2
W⋅h
0.00265
R2
-
0.99
Next, as the melting behaviour of the snow based on our scaled ERA5 data is known, the precipitation
has to be transformed into snow depth. This can be performed by expressing which fraction of the
precipitation falls in the form of snow in function of the air temperature. Vionnet et al. [97] present
in order to do so the following expression:
fsnow =
1
1 + aexp(b(Tw −273.15 + c))
(5.3)
with fsnow being the fraction of the total precipitation which is falling as snow and Tw is the wet-bulb
temperature in Kelvin. The parameters a, b and c have been optimised by Wang in 2019 and showed
good performances for data of a case from the United States. Their values are respectively 6,99×10−5
, 2 K−1 and 3,97 K [97].
The wet-bulb temperature Tw is the temperature measured by a thermometer covered in a soaked
cloth, with water at ambient temperature and with a constant air flow over the cloth [98]. By
following this procedure, it is possible to account for heat loss due to evaporation when the relative
humidity is below 100 %. Stull presents in his paper [98] the empirical expression in Equation 5.4,
based on a function fit to data that allows the determination of the wet-bulb temperature based on
air temperatures Ta and relative humidity RH [%].
Tw = arctan(0.151977(RH + 8.313659)(1/2)) + arctan(Ta + RH)
−arctan(RH −1.676331) + 0.00391838RH(3/2) arctan(0.023101RH) −4.686035
(5.4)
Now the fraction of the total scaled ERA5 precipitation, which is assumed to be snow, is known
and calculated. The precipitation in the scaled ERA5 data base is given as mm (liquid) rain per
hour. This unit can be interpreted as a water mass with a volume of 1 mm in height times 1 m2 in
surface per hour. Hence, after multiplication with the snow fraction fsnow, we obtain the amount of
159
==PAGE:185==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
snow as mm liquid/hour. This unit is compatible with the definition of the snow melt M such that
the fraction of the snow that melts in function of time can be calculated directly. However, for the
thermal modelling, we need the actual snow depth. This depth is obtained, by assuming that 1 mm
rain on 1 m2 which precipitates as snow, has the same mass and surface area, but a different height.
This can also be expressed as follows by using the densities of the two media:
1m2 ⋅hwρw = 1m2 ⋅hsnowρsnow
(5.5)
With hw being the precipitation as given in the ERA5 data in mm, ρw the density of water, hsnow
the equivalent snow height in mm and ρsnow the density of the snow. Consequently, the snow depth
in mm based on the ERA5 precipitation data and assumptions on the snow density, is computed with
the following expression:
hsnow =
ρw
ρsnow
hw
(5.6)
As there are snow depth measurements available of the 31st of March 2023, it is possible to calibrate
the snow density in order to fit the data. Note that the actual snow density changes in time, so this
approach is a severe approximation of reality. However, the variation in snow depth as input for our
thermal model is a good approach at first instance to obtain useful results of the thermal state and
regarding the adaptation methods such as thermosyphons and embankment constructions.
Based on the snow depths obtained from the ERA5 data, and the fitted expressions in the paper of
Chen et al. [99], it is possible to obtain the albedo in function of the snow depth in time. Chen et al.
make a distinction between wet and dry snow regarding the maximally possible value of respectively
0.65 and 0.75. The minimal value for both snow types is 0.2, which coincides with the assumed
value for green vegetation. The proposed equations are given below, where the unit of the snow
depth hsnow is cm.
α =
⎧⎪⎪⎨⎪⎪⎩
(0.75hsnow + 0.22)/(hsnow + 0.97)
Dry snow
(0.66hsnow + 0.79)/(hsnow + 3.66)
Wet snow
(5.7)
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.6: (a) Albedo, and (b) Snow depth for a selected period covered by the ERA5 data set.
The distinction between dry and wet snow is made as follows: Chen et al. discuss that wet snow is
occurring when the upper snow layer starts to melt, while dry snow is only encountered when there
is freshly fallen snow present [99]. In other words, during the period of snow accumulation, it can
160
==PAGE:186==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
be assumed that the snow conditions correspond with dry snow, and during the ablation period the
snow conditions correspond to wet snow. In the code, these periods are separated by considering
the first derivative, with a positive derivative indicating the accumulation period, and a negative
derivative indicating the ablation period.
Based on the available scaled ERA5 data, a data set for the same period as for the ERA5 data is
obtained for the snow depth and the albedo coefficient. This snow depth does not take accumulation
due to wind effects, topography, and other effects into account, but gives an indication of the snow
depth if purely temperature, precipitation and radiation is considered. An extract of the obtained
snow depth and albedo curves is shown in Figure 5.6.
5.1.4
Materials
At this point, the climatological variables are determined for both future and historical scenarios.
In this section, the configuration of the used materials and boundaries in the modelling with the
GeoStudio TEMP/W software is discussed into further detail.
GeoStudio TEMP/W is a finite element software product provided by the Seequent Limited company
and is a practical and comprehensive tool to model heat transfers and phase changes in porous media.
Especially the possibility to use a built-in, holistic Surface Energy Balance boundary condition and
a built-in boundary condition that simulates the working principles of thermosyphons, make this
software the ideal tool to compose a thermal model for this particular project. The software allows
the user to also combine thermal models with hydrological models and to configure 1D, 2D and 3D
models. However, due to time limitations, this was not possible and is definitely recommended for
future projects regarding this topic.
Figure 5.7: The used relation of the normalised unfrozen water content in function of the temperature
as implemented in GeoStudio TEMP/W.
Soils
In total, there are six different soil types used in the models. Three of the six are used in the models
where the temperature fits the measurements closest to the mast, while four of the six materials
161
==PAGE:187==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
are used to match the natural soil conditions. This section gives detailed information about the
characteristics of these soils, with a summary in Table 5.2 of the characteristics as implemented in
the software. The used material model for the soils is the ’Full Thermal’ material model [100]. This
model takes the latent heat that occurs during the phase changes into account. Furthermore, this
material model accounts for a variable unfrozen and frozen thermal conductivity, and it incorporates
the phenomenon that the phase change does not occur instantaneously at one specific temperature.
This material model enables the modelling of thawing-freezing cycles in an accurate manner, with
the assumption that the absolute amount of water or ice remains constant over the whole period of
the analysis.
The unfrozen water content is assumed to be the same for all soil samples, except for the organic
layer. This assumption is valid to make, as TEMP/W requires the unfrozen water content to be
normalised with the porosity [100]. The unfrozen water content in function of the temperature is the
theoretical fit of the measurements as discussed in Section 4.2.6 and Section 4.1.8, and is showed for
the sake of completeness in Figure 5.7.
For the organic layer, the thermal conductivity and the unfrozen water content in function of
temperature are based on standard values for a medium compacted organic silty soil as specified
in Andersland and Ladanyi [4]. For the unfrozen water content in function of the temperature, the
values for the coefficients in Equation 2.42 are α = 9.6 and β = −0.364. The obtained curves are
shown for the two variables in Figure 5.8. The unfrozen and frozen volumetric heat capacity is also
based on Andersland and Ladanyi [4], as specified in Table 5.2.
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.8: (a) Unfrozen water content, and (b) thermal conductivity for an organic layer based on
standard values form Andersland and Ladanyi [4].
The thermal conductivity is changing in function of the porosity, volumetric water content, bulk
density, grain particle density and saturation. All this is determined per soil layer by following the
same methodology as explained in Section 4.1.9 and 2.5.2. As the shape of the curve of all soil
samples is similar due to the fact that each time the same (measured) unfrozen water content
function is used, and just the eventual values of the unfrozen and frozen thermal conductivity change,
it is opted to only report these values in Table 5.2.

Other materials
The other considered materials are steel for the mast and foundation column, wood for the foundation
foot, polystyrene for the insulation, and gravel for the embankment considered in one of the scenarios.
These materials, with the exception for gravel, are implemented in the model by using a ’Simplified
Thermal’ material model, and require only the unfrozen water content, the unfrozen and frozen
thermal conductivity, and the unfrozen and frozen volumetric heat capacity as input for the considered
materials [100]. The eventually used values are summarised in Table 5.3.
The ’Simplified Thermal’ material model is only suitable for materials in which the latent heat of
phase change is not that important to the freezing process [100]. Hence, for structural objects, not
being part of the subsurface, this effect is negligible. Furthermore, this material model assumes that
the frozen and unfrozen thermal conductivities are constant as a function of temperature and that
the water content does not change during the period of the analysis. Lastly, in strong contrast with
the ’Full Thermal’ material model, the ’Simplified Thermal’ model assumes that all the water in the
material freezes instantly at one specific temperature [100]. As for the considered materials there is
no water content modelled, this issue is not relevant for our project, but would cause the modelling
of unrealistic soil behaviour.
For the sake of clarity, it is stressed that the gravel is modelled similarly as the other soil materials,
as a ’Full Thermal’ material model, where the conductivity and unfrozen water content vary with the
temperature.

5.1.5
Model set-up
The first step of the set-up of a transient thermal model, is the configuration of a 1D model
corresponding with the soil layers in the 2D axisymmetric model. Indeed, a transient model is
configured, as we are interested in the evolution of the thermal regime.
In practice, it is required to simulate the response of the considered domain to several annual cycles
of the climate before reliable initial thermal conditions in the domain are reached. This method
is often called as a ’spin-up’ analysis and ensures realistic initial thermal conditions for a transient
analysis. The main goal of such a spin-up analysis is to cancel out the influence of a randomly
chosen initial temperature profile on the overall simulated response [105]. In this case, as suggested
in Figure 5.9, it is indeed opted to reduce the considered 2D axisymmetric domain to a 1D domain,
to reduce computational time. For the spin-up, the ERA5 data of 1959-1969 is used and cycled
several times until the temperature in the lowest node of the 1D domain reaches a constant value.
Once stable initial temperature conditions are reached, the temperature profile obtained in the 1D
model is extrapolated linearly over the complete 2D axisymmetric model domain. In a first phase,
the 2D model represents the situation before the construction of the mast and intends to model
until just before the actual construction of the mast. It is important to note that one should pay
attention in interpreting eventual results of an axisymmetric model in GeoStudio, as the projection of
the 3D domain on the 2D axisymmetric plane, is not a projection of a slices with unit thickness that
is the same over the whole projection. As shown in Figure 5.10, the projection of the 3D domain
onto the 2D axisymmetric domain is a sector with radius Bmax and angle equal to 1 radian. This is
important to consider for the resulting heat fluxes in the soil for instance, as with increasing distance
from the axisymmetric axis, the volume of a unit cell increases and hence the flux per volume is also
different compared to closer to the axisymmetric axis. For these reasons, the focus in the discussion
will be put mainly on temperature profiles.
The last part in the modelling of the historical thermal regime, is the scenario where the mast is
constructed. It is assumed that the mast is built instantaneously on the 1st of January 2006. This is
not in line with reality, as the construction was carried out in the summer months and later than
2006. The assumed construction in January implies furthermore that the whole construction was
placed into a completely frozen active layer and that the mast could start cooling the sub surface
as well, which is beneficial for the thermal regime but not realistic with reality. So, the eventual
results of this model, should be interpreted with this assumption of the presented model in mind.
The reason that this modelling approach was chosen, is for a more practical implementation of the
climate data and the uncertainty of the actual construction date of Mast 9.
The model runs with the future climate projection of the temperature as input is not depicted in
Figure 5.9, but they will have the same configuration as for the period 2006-2022, and will use the
temperature distribution in the model domain resulting from the model output with no adaptation
measures on the data 31-12-2022 23:00:00 as starting temperature.
Figure 5.9 also shows the used boundary conditions: the lower boundary condition is in all cases
a no-flux boundary condition and the boundary conditions at the surface and for the mast are the
Surface Energy Balance boundary conditions. The no-flux boundary condition implies that soil below
this boundary is not influenced by the heat fluxes above it. In other words, there is no heat transport
through this boundary to deeper soil layers. This is a valid assumption, as seasonal variations are
normally not influencing soil deeper as 15 m. A more realistic boundary condition for the lower
boundary could have been the use of a heat flux corresponding to the geothermal gradient [105].
164
==PAGE:190==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
1D
2D Axisymmetric
1959-1969
1970-2005
2006-2022
SPIN UP
No Flux BC
Surface Energy
Balance
Bmax
Bmax
Figure 5.9: Illustration of the followed thermal modelling methodology in GeoStudio.
The surface energy balance boundary conditions on the other hand are a more realistic and holistic
approach to represent the thermal interactions between soil and the atmosphere, and it was described
specifically for permafrost for the first time by Goodrich and Gold in 1981 [106]. Goodrich and Gold
stated that in nature, the ground surface boundary condition is in fact the net heat flux arising
from absorbed solar and long wave radiation and from sensible and latent heat transfer between the
ground surface and the overlying air [106]. This collection of processes can be concisely written as:
QG = −λG
δT
δn + QSW + QLW + QH + QE
(5.8)
with QG being the heat flux at the ground surface, λG the thermal conductivity of the ground surface,
δT
δn the thermal gradient at the ground surface, QSW the net flux of solar radiation, i.e. short-wave
radiation, QLW the net flux of long wave radiation, QH the net flux of sensible heat and QE the net
flux of the latent heat associated with evaporation of moisture from the surface.
So in other words, this boundary condition is applying a heat flux condition on the specified boundary,
similarly as the no-flux boundary condition. In this case, the value of the flux is variable in time and
depends on the variable atmospheric interaction in time with the ground surface.
165
==PAGE:191==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
Bmax
2D sector
Foundation
column
Wooden foundation
foot
1 rad
Figure 5.10: Top view in the 3D domain of the modelled 2D axisymmetric domain, with the projected
part on the 2D axisymmetric plane. The dimensions are in meters.
Figure 5.11 shows the dimensions of the implemented mast in GeoStudio. The diameter of the
foundation column, the width and depth of the wooden foundation foot are all according to the
technical drawings. However, as this model is axisymmetric, the cross section of the mast and the
wooden foundation foot is not rectangular, but circular instead. The height of the mast was reduced
to two meter above surface, to account for the fact that in GeoStudio it is not possible to implement
concisely a truss construction, and especially not in a 2D axisymmetric representation. Therefore,
by trial and error, a height of 2 m proved to produce a model where the temperatures up to 1.2 m
depth seemed to coincide with the measurements. It is however unsure how this assumption affects
the deeper soil layers, and the eventual results should be interpreted with this assumption in mind.
Especially, as apart from a lower heat transfer through solar radiation, the wind effect is lower in the
model and also the effect of snow and rain falling through the trusses is definitely not taken into
account in this way.
0.15

1.8
0.15
0.6
1.75
Figure 5.11: The mast, as implemented in GeoStudio. The dimensions are in meters.
Regarding the material choice, the mast foundation and simplified mast structure are made from one
material, being steel and the foundation foot is specified to be constructed from wood. Based on
166
==PAGE:192==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
the technical drawings and field observations, there is also an insulation layer provided on the mast
column of 2 cm thickness. Note that there is 5 cm soil covering this insulation layer, to ensure that
the surface energy balance boundary condition is possible to implement on the whole surface, as
the insulation material is implemented as a ’simplified thermal analysis’ material which required less
input parameters.
In the next sections, each scenario is discussed in more detail and eventual additional details are
given in the corresponding sections.
5.2
1D model
As explained before, a one-dimensional model is used to obtain a realistic initial temperature profile of
the soil layers used in a later stage in the 2D axisymmetric modelling. The 1D model is represented as
a line, subdivided into several regions corresponding to the soil layers modelled in the 2D axisymmetric
model. At the top node of the 1D model, a Surface Energy Balance boundary condition is assigned,
while at the lowest node a no-flux boundary condition is assigned. The Surface Energy Balance
boundary condition is configured with the scaled ERA5 climate variables of the period 1959-1969 as
input. These climate variables are cycled as many times as needed over the period of time for which
the 1D model is ran until stable temperature conditions are reached in the lowest node. The model
applied a time step of two days, which means that every two days the thermal regime was computed
based on the climatic conditions, boundary conditions, and the initial or previous thermal regime
in the domain of the 1D model. The total period that was required until stable temperatures were
reached in the lower part of the 1D model is 820 years.
Figure 5.12: Convergence of the temperature in the lowest node of the 1D model, during the spin-up
phase based on scaled ERA5 climate data of the 1959-1969 period.
A schematic representation of the 1D model is given in Figure 5.9 and Figure 5.13 (b) gives the
resulting initial temperature profiles in the modelled soil column corresponding to a vertical 1D cut as
show in 5.13 (a) of the model configuration corresponding to the case without horizontal insulation
as represented in Figure 5.15. All the 1D models reach from 0 to 100 m depth, and their discretised
mesh have in all cases in total 948 nodes, with the interspacing between two nodes increasing with
increasing depth. Note that during the modelling process, better results were obtained with respect
to the soil temperature measurements if the snow depths based on the scaled ERA5 data set were
167
==PAGE:193==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
reduced with a factor 0.5. Therefore, unless stated explicitly, the snow depth in the model runs was
decreased accordingly.
Figure 5.12 depicts the graph of convergence of the temperature of the lowest node in the 1D model
as a function of time. Interestingly, convergence was only reached after almost 800 years of cycling
the same climatic variables of the scaled ERA5 data from the period 1959-1969. This is certainly
not as expected, as also based on the manual of TEMP/W [100, 105], usually 10 years is more than
sufficient. It is unclear until now why this extremely long spin-up period was required.
90
9
1
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.13: (a) 1D model as implemented in GeoStudio TEMP/W, and (b) The eventually obtained
initial temperature profiles. The dimensions are given in meters.
It is clear, based on the temperature profiles shown in Figure 5.13 (b) that there is a thawed soil
layer, ranging from practically the surface until 10 m below the surface. This thawed soil layer is not
expected, as the ERT profile SIS2F-04 depicted in Figure 3.33 representing the natural soil conditions
in July 2022, does not show such an extensive thawed soil layer. As there was no thawed layer last
summer, it is very unlikely this would have been the case at the end of 1969 without having a mast
constructed yet. However, for the initial temperatures for the spin up of -4 and -5 ○C the temperature
is barely exceeding 0 ○C and this thawed soil layer might be occurring due to uncertainties in the soil
properties, but also due to uncertainties or numerical errors induced by the finite element method or
meshing method used by GeoStudio TEMP/W. Another possibility why this thawed layer occurs close
at the surface, is the unusually long spin-up period. It is possible that there is an extreme hot event
or period, or a period with an extremely high snow depth in the 1959-1969 climate data, such that
the surface and soil layers close to the surface are heated up more and more in time. Nevertheless,
this spin-up needs to be sufficiently long to reach stable temperatures in the lower part of the 1D
model, so this thawed layer might be one of the consequences of using the spin-up method when no
measured initial temperature profile is available.
168
==PAGE:194==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
In any case, this thawed soil layer is assumed to be unrealistic, and the absolute values of temperatures
in all the discussed models should be interpreted with caution. However, the discussed models and
results are definitely useful for comparing the differences between several scenarios, and by assessing
the effect of the considered adaptation methods.
5.3
Considered scenarios of 2D axisymmetric models
There are several scenarios considered for the following reasons. Firstly, the effect on the thermal
regime of the soil of the different considered adaptation methods is assessed, and this both based on
historical scaled ERA5 data and extrapolated scaled ERA5 data based on RCP scenarios. Furthermore,
the effect of the choice of the soil material and the soil layers on the thermal regime is considered as
well. Lastly, as during the field work insulation pieces were observed on the surface, it is investigated
to what extent the insulation is still effectively protecting the underlying permafrost.
For the 2D axisymmetric models, a time step of one day was used, and the total period depends on
the considered historical or future period. The use of a lower time step can be a point of improvement
in order to obtain more accurate results. However, as the hydrological influences are not taken
into account in this case, although they are assumed to have a significant influence on the thermal
regime based on observations during the field work of several surface streams and ground water flows
around the mast, a smaller time step would still not take away the uncertainty of not including all
the significantly influencing physical processes. A relatively larger time step made the running time
of the models also shorter, which was an important factor to consider.
Lastly, in all cases the element types used to discretise the model domain are a combination of
triangular and rectangular elements. The boundary conditions are for all 2D axisymmetric models as
explained in Section 5.1.5, and they are adapted according to the considered ERA5 or future climate
data.
5.3.1
Horizontal insulation
The first considered 2D scenario, is the case where it is assumed that after the construction of the
mast, the horizontal insulation remained on the depth as it was designed. Horizontal insulation
covering permafrost soil, causes the decrease of the amplitude of the soil temperature variation, which
is beneficial for the protection of permafrost soils, as the penetration of heat from the atmospheric
conditions downward into the permafrost is reduced. However, Martin Nauta, responsible project
engineer at Nukissiorfiit, pointed during one of our discussions to the fact that in this case the
horizontal insulation might trap the heat conducted by the mast, instead of keeping the heat out of
the lower soil layers. This would cause an even faster thawing of the underlying permafrost, which
might explain the sudden settlements that have been observed. Therefore, this scenario is discussed
first, together with a scenario where the horizontal insulation is partially damaged, as during the field
trip we observed pieces of insulation sticking out of the surface and lying on the surface scattered
around the mast.
Figure 5.14 (a) shows the implemented 2D axisymmetric model in GeoStudio TEMP/W, where the
soil box has dimensions of 100 x 100 m2 and consists purely of the silty clay which is indicated with
ID 1 in table 5.2. In the same soil box, but with ’damaged’ or discontinuous horizontal insulation,
the second case of this scenario is represented in Figure 5.14 (b). The same mesh is used for both
cases, where 2772 elements and 2919 nodes are used for the discretisation. The mesh consists of a
combination of triangular and rectangular elements, and the element sizes around the mast start at
0.02 m, and increasing in size with increasing distance from the mast with a maximal element size of
10 m.

5.3.2
Without horizontal insulation
In order to model the situation, where no insulation is present to protect the underlying soil directly
around the mast, the model configuration as depicted in Figure 5.15 is considered. There are two
reasons for considering the case: the observation of damages at the horizontal insulation which could
imply that there is no (sufficient amount of) insulation present anymore as it was designed, and to
assess the actual effect of horizontal insulation on the thermal regime around the mast.
Figure 5.15 shows that there are three types of soils used in this model. This soil structure was chosen
based on optimisation of the model with respect to the available temperature data, and also based
on the ERT results, that clearly show a soil volume directly around the mast, that has significantly
different properties than the ’natural’ soil. The reason for not using this soil configuration for the
scenario with both continuous and discontinuous horizontal insulation is that the results indicated
that the insulation does not have any effect anymore on the thermal regime until a maximal depth of
1.2 m below the surface.
The soil box has the same dimension as for the case with horizontal insulation, being 100 x 100 m2.
The discretisation of the model domain resulted in 2578 elements and 2573 nodes, with the smallest
element being close to the mast and having a size of 0.02 m, and the size of the elements increase
with increasing distance from the mast with a maximal element size of 7 m. The chosen material for
the soil regions is indicated in Figure 5.15 with their ID of material as given in Table 5.2.

dimensions of 100 x 100 m2/. The smallest element was again closest to the mast with a size of

5.3.4
Thermosyphon
The modelling of thermosyphons in a 2D axisymmetric model is not as straightforward as in a 3D
model, due to the fact that it is not possible to simply model a vertical tube, with the diameter of the
evaporator, at a certain distance from the mast. Indeed, as illustrated in Figure 5.17, a thermosyphon
is modelled as a line, with no physical thickness in the model, such that in the 3D space this element
is in fact a vertical cylinder or a pipe enclosing the mast foundation. However, by ’spreading’ out
the effect of the thermosyphons over this cylinder, it is possible to model the combined effect of a
specified amount of thermosyphons. In this case, four and eight thermosyphons per mast foundation
are considered.
As already mentioned, a thermosyphon in a 2D axisymmetric model is implemented as a line element
in GeoStudio, and the software allows to assign a thermosyphon boundary condition to that line.
The length of the line in this case represents the length of the evaporator, and the dimensions of
the evaporator and condenser are taken into account as well as the dimensions are an important
influencing factor of the heat extraction capacity. The dimensions are therefore one of the variables
which can be tuned in order to reach the required heat extraction to keep the ground at a certain
172
==PAGE:198==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
temperature for instance.
The boundary condition as implemented in GeoStudio, requires apart from a factor where both the
dimensions and the heat extraction capacity are included, also the air temperature and wind velocity
of the modelled period.
100
Thermosyphon
2D sector
Foundation
column
Wooden foundation
foot
Figure 5.17: Top view of the 2D axisymmetric model with the four thermosyphons in 3D space. The
dimensions are in meters.
The ability of extracting heat out of the ground is often described as the conductance capacity which
can be estimated by a heat transfer coefficient, usually per unit surface area of the evaporator of the
thermosyphon [105]. Furthermore, the amount of heat that can get extracted from the ground is
dependent on the wind velocity moving past the condenser [105]. The GeoStudio software requires
that this key property of the thermosyphon is described as a general data point function, in function
of the wind velocity. Based on laboratory tests, Haynes and Zarling presented in 1988 a set of
equations for the total heat conductance of some commercially available thermosyphons at that time
[107]. Haynes and Zarling described based on those experiments that the conductance capacity of a
thermosyphon with an evaporator hooked to a condenser with a length of 2.08 m, a diameter of 0.05
m, a length of 6.01 m, and sloping at 12○, can be estimated as follows:
C = 16.5 + 25v0.62
(5.9)
with C being the conductance capacity in W/○C and for the sake of clarity it should be emphasised
that C is not equal to the heat transfer coefficient. v represents the wind velocity in m/s. This
formulation of the conductance capacity is also recommended by elaborated examples in the GeoStudio
TEMP/W manual [105, 100].
The surface area of the evaporator can be computed as:
Aevap = π × d × l = π × 0.05m × 6.01m = 0.94m2 ≈1m2
(5.10)
The division of the conductance capacity C, obtained by Equation 5.9, with the area of the evaporator,
yields eventually in the heat transfer coefficient of the modelled thermosyphon.
This coefficient is required as input for GeoStudio in the thermosyphon boundary condition. However,
as this heat transfer coefficient is calculated for one thermosyphon, and in the considered models for
this thesis four or eight thermosyphons are assumed along the modelled ’cylinder’ of thermosyphons
enclosing the mast, the heat transfer coefficient needs to be adapted by multiplying it with the ratio
rTS:
rTS = nTS × π × d
π × D →r4
TS = 4 × π × 0.05
π × 2
= 0.1
(5.11)
173
==PAGE:199==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
With nTS being the amount of considered thermosyphons, d the diameter of the evaporator of one
thermosyphon, and D the diameter of the modelled ’pipe’ of thermosyphons in the 2D axisymmetric
model, which is dependent on the distance of the thermosyphon to the axisymmetric axis. As in this
case the thermosyphon is placed at 1 meter from the axisymmetric axis, D is equal to 2 m. The
eventual value of rTS for four thermosyphons is given as an example.
This procedure yields in the heat transfer coefficient in function of the wind velocity as depicted
in Figure 5.18 for four thermosyphons. The other input parameters in this scenario regarding the
climate are the same as for the other scenarios. The used materials and the dimensions are exactly
the same as for the ’Without horizontal insulation’ case discussed in Section 5.3.2.
Figure 5.18: Curve of the heat transfer coefficient, calculated for four thermosyphons in function of
the wind velocity.
There are two different discretisations of the model applied for this scenario. For the modelling with
the historical scaled ERA5 data, the mesh of the model consists of 2592 elements and 2688 nodes,
with the element sizes ranging between 0.02 m and 7 m. The size of the elements increases with an
increasing distance from the mast and the thermosyphon. For the sake of reducing the computation
time, the mesh was made coarser for the runs with future climate projections. Therefore, the mesh
configuration reduced to a combination of 1018 elements and 1026 nodes, with the element sizes
ranging in the same way as before, between 0.01 m and 10 m.

5.3.5
Embankment
The last considered scenario, is the case where a circular embankment is constructed around the
mast, in order to get the frost level closer to the original surface level. This embankment has a
height of 0.8 m and the toe is located at a distance of 3 m from the foundation column of the mast.
The upper surface of the embankment has a radius of 2 m, such that the side of the embankment
is sloping at an angle of 39 ○. This angle might be slightly too large but is relatively close to a
more realistic slope that ranges between 30-35 ○for gravel. Therefore, it is assumed that for this
thermal model this slightly unrealistic geometry is insignificant. Note that the height of the mast is
increased to 2.8 m, to ensure that only 2 m is exposed to the ambient conditions (i.e. the surface
boundary conditions indicated in red). The ID’s of the used materials as depicted in Figure 5.20, are
in correspondence with Table 5.2 and Table 5.3.
For this model, two discretisations have been applied. One finer mesh was used to model the historical
scenarios with the scaled ERA5 climate data. This mesh consisted of 1864 elements and 1899 nodes,
with the size of the elements ranging from 0.02 m to 7 m. For the future climate scenarios, the
domain was discretised by using 1328 elements and 1317 nodes, with element sizes ranging from
0.03 m to 10 m. For both cases, the element size increases with increasing distance from the mast.

5.4
Results
5.4.1
Without mast or any adaptation methods
In accordance with the methodology as depicted in Figure 5.9, before the scenarios are run where
the mast is constructed, a 2D axisymmetric model consisting just of the soil box corresponding to
the considered scenario, is run with climate data that covers the period from 1970 until 2005. The
initial temperature distribution for the latter model is provided by the 1D model, and the 1D results
are linearly extrapolated over the 2D domain.
For these runs a time step of one day was chosen, and a relatively coarse mesh proved to be adequate
for this purpose. In total, the discretisation of the 2D domain resulted in a combination of 1086
triangular and rectangular elements, with a corresponding amount of 860 nodes. The element sizes
range from 0.02 m to 15 m, with the element size increasing with an increasing distance from the
surface.
176
==PAGE:202==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
Figure 5.21 (a) depicts the temperature on the 31st of December 2005, based on the scaled ERA5
data and based on the 2D axisymmetric model with the ’Natural Soil’ configuration, but without a
mast present at the axisymmetric axis. Figure 5.21 (a) depicts on the other hand the temperature
trumpet of the year 2005, with an active layer thickness of approximately 1 m. This situation is
assumed to be the natural situation, as it was before the constructions started. An active layer
thickness of approximately 1 m is as expected for the Sisimiut area, such that these results are
considered as reasonable.

5.4.2
With horizontal insulation
It is clear, based on the modelling results depicted in Figure 5.22 and Figure 5.23, that the horizontal
insulation is not operational, as it should according to design. As during the field work, parts of the
insulation were found on the surface and as some of the insulation, especially at the anchors, was
sticking out of the ground, frost heave and other deformations in the subsurface probably caused
the disintegration of the horizontal insulation layer. Another possible reason why the temperature
measurements are not in line with how it should have been with horizontal insulation, is that the
horizontal insulation has been constructed inappropriately. However, as in Figure 5.23 the results for
the discontinuous insulation case are depicted, it is clear that the gaps of 10 cm width in between
the horizontal insulation plates are not causing the temperature fluctuations as it has been measured.
So the destruction of the insulation must be more severe than just some gaps.
177
==PAGE:203==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
(e)
(f)
(g)
(h)
(i)
(j)
Figure 5.22: Soil temperature in function of time, both modelled with discontinuous horizontal
insulation and measured data.
It is also clear what effect the horizontal insulation should have in an ideal situation on the deeper soil
layers, as the temperature variation is limited significantly below the insulation. This also shows that
the thermal regime distortion in the soil around the mast is not only due to heat fluxes originating
from the mast foundation, but that the net effect on the thermal regime of the soil around the
mast is a combination of heat fluxes from the surface downwards (assuming that the upward heat
fluxes due to the thermal gradient are negligible), and through the mast foundation column. The
thermal properties of the soil have therefore a large effect on the heat transport to the deeper soil
layers as well. In conclusion, principles of using horizontal insulation are certainly with the purpose of
effectively protecting underlying permafrost. However, not accounting for frost heave processes or
the inappropriate construction caused the malfunctioning of the concept.

5.4.3
Without horizontal insulation
Based on the results from the case with horizontal insulation, which was however according to the
design plans of the construction, the case where no horizontal insulation is present is considered
next. The modelled soil volume consists of three soil types, including a soil volume directly around
the mast with deviating soil properties. This modelling choice is based on the ERT observations as
discussed before.
The results as depicted in Figure 5.24 show a relatively good match with the measurements closest to
the mast, keeping in mind the simplicity of the 2D axisymmetric model and the deviating temperature
179

Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
measurements in downhill direction or parallel to the Fjord. On the other hand, the temperature
measurements at Logger 9, which is the logger furthest away from the mast, do not show a match
with the model output whatsoever. The probable reason for this is discussed further in Section 5.4.4.
As close to the mast the thermal regime up to a depth of 1.2 m is verified based on the relatively
limited amount of temperature data, it is assumed that the shown soil layer configuration is a good
representation of the actual shallow conditions close to the mast. Therefore, the modelling of the
adaptation scenarios around the mast are performed with this soil set-up, and so are the model runs
with the future climate projections. This is a valid assumption, as the main goal in the modelling is
to investigate how the thermal regime evolves around the mast in the several considered scenarios.

up to a distance of 5 m from the mast just exceeds 1 m, which allows the lower foundation foot
to be constantly in frozen soil. This measure, to install 8 thermosyphons of the considered type,
seems to be an adequate solution to compensate for the disturbances to the thermal regime of the
surrounding soil due to the construction of the mast, and the presence of the mast. In Section 5.4.7,
where the response of the model in function of the considered future climate scenarios is considered,
it will be assessed if eight thermosyphons will be sufficient in the (near) future.
5.4.6
Embankment
In the case an embankment, constructed out of gravel is completed at the same time as the
construction of the mast, the model results in the following temperature trumpet as shown in Figure
5.29. In this case, the snow depth is not reduced, and the redistribution of the snow is not taken
into account. On the other hand, Figure 5.30 shows the temperature trumpets, for the case the
snow depth, resulting from the scaled ERA5 data, is reduced with a factor 0.5 to account in the
184
==PAGE:210==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
most simple way for snow redistribution by the wind.
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 5.29: Resulting soil temperature trumpets for the year 2022, based on the scaled daily ERA5
data covering the period of 1959-2022, for the scenario with an embankment around the mast.
The effect of the snow is clearly visible if both cases are considered. In Figure 5.29, where the snow
cover is twice as large than in the other case, the active layer thickness is practically 5 m, with the
temperature just exceeding 0 ○C in the depth interval of [-1 -5] m. This shows that the soil is not
able to cool down sufficiently in the winter months.
If the other case, when a reduced snow pack is considered in Figure 5.30, it is clear that this ’thawed’
zone in the range of [-1 -5] m, is not present anymore and that active layer thickness is around 1 m,
proving the effectiveness if an embankment would have been constructed from right in the beginning.
Keeping the embankment snow free, would benefit the stability of the underlying permafrost even
more, although this job seems very difficult to perform in such remote locations and in such harsh
conditions during winter.

5.4.7
Future projections
In this section, the model results are discussed, based on the RCP 2.6 and RCP 6 climate scenarios.
Instead of depicting the results as trumpets, it is opted to plot the soil temperatures in function of
time, at 1 m of the mast by using contour plots. It is opted to not show the complete modelled
period, instead only the first and last 5000 days of the modelled period are shown.
RCP 2.6
For the climate scenario predicting the lowest global warming, the model results in soil temperatures
as a function of time for the case where no adaptation measures are taken as shown in Figure 5.31.
It is clear that the heat will penetrate deeper and deeper into the sub surface and that in the very
near future already the lower boundary of the active layer will reach depths up to 6 m and more.
Based on these results, it is fact that the stability of the mast is not guaranteed anymore. Note that
the actual depth of the lower boundary of the active layer is questionable, but it is sure that the
186
==PAGE:212==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
active layer depth will increase.
(a) 01-01-2023 until 09-09-2036
(b) 13-06-2091 until 20-02-2105
Figure 5.31: Contour plot of the temperature distribution at 1 m distance from the mast in function
of time and depth, for the RCP 2.6 climate scenario in the case no adaptation measures are taken.
The model results with an embankment constructed around the mast for the future projections
according to the considered RCP scenario are depicted in Figure 5.32. There is a significant difference
observed between the contour plots for the case where no adaptations are taken. The active layer
thickness is less extensive, and to some extent the frost level is pulled up. However, there are even in
the near future active layer thicknesses which would exceed the depth of the wooden foundation
foot. Especially when the last 5000 days plot in Figure 5.32 (b) is considered, the heat has clearly
penetrated below the lower boundary of the mast foundation. Keeping in mind that this climate
scenario models the best possible outcome for the future, an embankment is according to this model
definitely not sufficient to stabilise the mast.
Note that the depth in the contour plot starts at 0 m, meaning that the temperatures in the
187
==PAGE:213==
Chapter 5. Thermal modelling
embankment itself are not included in the plot.
(a) 01-01-2023 until 09-09-2036
(b) 13-06-2091 until 20-02-2105
Figure 5.32: Contour plot of the temperature distribution at 1 m distance from the mast in function
of time and depth, for the RCP 2.6 climate scenario in the case there is an embankment constructed
around the mast.
Lastly, the results of the model when the mast is retrofitted with eight thermosyphons is shown in
Figure 5.33. It is clear that the thermosyphons are able to cool the whole subsurface significantly.
The model even suggests that the thermosyphon configuration would be able to cool the ground up
to a depth of 8 m. During the summer however, there will be still a penetration of the heat into
the soil, but thanks to the extensive cooling during the winter months, the penetration depth of the
heat is limited to approximately 1.5 m at a distance of 1 m from the mast foundation column. This
penetration depth seems to be even constant up to the year 2100. Note that this is again for the
most beneficial climate model, and therefore the results for the RCP 6 scenario need to be considered
as well.

RCP 6
The model output with the air temperatures based on the climate scenario RCP 6 are discussed in
this section. Figure 5.34 shows the soil temperatures at a distance of 1 m from the mast foundation
column, in the case no adaptation measures are taken. It is clear that there is more severe heat
penetration compared to the RCP 2.6 case. Hence, in this climate scenario, the mast integrity is
endangered even more.
189
==PAGE:215==

The results based on the model where the embankment is included for the RCP 6 scenario are shown
in Figure 5.35. It is clear that in the first 5000 days the heat penetration is not as deep and severe
compared to the reference case with no adaptation measures. However, the embankment cannot
cope anymore with the higher temperatures in the further future, and the plot of the last 5000 days
shows a situation as almost as severe as for the reference case. It is clear now, that the embankment
definitely does not provide a suitable solution to stabilise the mast for the future.

==PAGE:216==

On the other hand, the results of the model with eight thermosyphons included around the mast
are depicted in Figure 5.36. It is clear that the thermosyphons are able to cool the sub surface and
maintain the subzero temperature for the whole modelled period. However, the heat penetration
depth increased compared to the RCP 2.6 case, but is only in the last 5000 days approximately 2
m. A more detailed model could be used to make sure eight thermosyphons of the considered type
are sufficient to stabilise the thermal regime for the future. However, it is still valid to state that
the effect of these thermosyphons is significant, and perhaps minor changes of the configuration of
the thermosyphons will ensure stable soil conditions for the mast foundation for the whole modelled
future period. Especially as an older type of thermosyphons has been used in the modelling here,
current thermosyphon models could have the required heat extraction capacity.
